The Syllogism
intermediate
5 min
General Conditions of Syllogism: No Two Negatives, Conclusion Follows Weaker Premise
Author explains rules of syllogism: no two negatives or two particulars, conclusion follows weaker premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a syllogism is invalid due to two negative premises or two particular premises, and apply the rule that the conclusion must follow the weaker premise in quality and quantity.

Core Idea
Two negatives or two particulars break the syllogism; the conclusion must follow the weaker premise.

In this passage, the author deepens our understanding of syllogistic validity by explaining two crucial rules. First, a syllogism cannot contain two negative premises because the middle term then fails to connect the two extremes. Second, the conclusion must align with the weaker premise in both quality and quantity—a principle rooted in the distribution of terms. These rules prevent overreach in logical deduction and ensure that conclusions remain grounded in the premises.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main rules stated at the beginning
Find the phrases 'وَلَا سَالِبَتَيْنِ' (no two negatives) and 'النَّتِيجَةُ تَتْبَعُ أَخَسَّ الْمُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ' (the conclusion follows the weaker premise). These are the pillars of this passage.
The author elaborates on these rules throughout the passage.
2
Understand why two negatives fail
The author explains that two negative premises separate the terms: the minor premise separates the minor from the middle, and the major premise separates the middle from the major. Thus, all three terms are disconnected, and no conclusion can follow.
This is the 'secret' of why the middle term must be distributed in at least one premise.
3
Analyze the rule of distribution (al-istighrāq)
The author links the above rules to distribution: if a term is distributed in the conclusion, it must also be distributed in its premise. Otherwise, the conclusion would be 'larger than the premises,' which is logically unjustified.
This applies to both subject and predicate terms in the conclusion.
4
Apply the rule to two particular premises
The author notes that two particular premises (affirmative or negative) also fail because the middle term is not distributed in either, mirroring the problem with two negatives.
This is an 'effect' (اثر) of the distribution rule.
5
Test the rule with a hypothetical universal conclusion
Consider a case where the conclusion is universal but the term was particular in its premise. The author says the intellect would not justify such a conclusion—this violates the distribution condition.
This clarifies the need for consistency between premise and conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَا سَالِبَتَيْنِ (١) ، وَالنَّتِيجَةُ تَتْبَعُ أَخَسَّ الْمُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ كَما(٢)، فلا ينتج قياس مركب تلك وبالتالي لا يلزم أن يكون الأفراد التي أشير إليها في الصغرى هي الأفراد التي أشير إليها الكبرى ، ، فالمقدماتان إذن منفصلة بعضها عن بعض، وبالتالي لا وجه لاستنباط نتيجة من القضيتين كل منهما منفصلة عن الأخرى. وهذا سر اشتراط كون الحد الأوسط مستغرقا في إحدى المقدمتين. ومن آثار هذه القاعدة - قاعدة الاستغراق ـ ما صرح به المصنف، وهو: أن لا إنتاج من جزئيتين موجبة أو سالبة؛ لأن الحد الأوسط في الحالة لا يكون مستغرقا في شيء من المقدمتين. انظر تيسير القواعد المنطقية، د/ محمد شمس الدين إبراهيم: ٢١١ - ٢١٦ . (۱) وهذا الحكم مترتب على القاعدة المتعلقة بالكيف وهي توجب أن «لا إنتاج من سالبتين كلية أو جزئية، إلا إذا كانت السالبتان في حكم الموجبة، كما صرح به السهروردي في منطق التلويحات (٤٨) . وذلك لأن الحد الأوسط حينئذ لا يربط بين المقدمتين أبدا ؛ لأن الصغرى - لكونها سالبة - تفصل بين الأصغر والأوسط، والكبرى أيضا - لكونها سالبة كذلك - تفصل بين الأوسط والأكبر. وهذا يعني أن حدود القياس الثلاثة مفصول بعضها عن بعض فصلا ،تاما، فلا يمكن استنباط نتيجة من هذه الحدود ـ أو بالأحرى القضايا ـ التي لا ترابط بينها أصلا .. (۲) وهو أيضا ما يترتب على قاعدة الاستغراق، وذلك لأن النتيجة لو لم تتبع أخس المقدمتين كما تكون كلية سالبة أو موجبة، فلا محالة يكون موضوعها مستغرقا، وقد يكون محمولاها أيضا مسغرقا وهي تتصور في عشرة ضروب من الأشكال الأربعة، تركت ذكرها لضيق المقام. وهذا الحد الذي استغرق في النتيجة - موضوعا أو محمولا ـ لم يستغرق في موضعه من المقدمة في تلك الضروب والقاعدة توجب كون الحد الذي استغرق في النتيجة مستغرقا في المقدمة التي هو فيها، وبعبارة أخرى: إذا جاء حد من حدي النتيجة مستغرقا فيجب أن يكون نفس هذا الحد قد استغرق من قبل في المقدمة التي اشتملت عليه وذلك لأن النتيجة إذا أخذ فيها أحد حديها بصورة كلية ـ موضوعا أو محمولا - ولم يؤخذ هذا الحد في المقدمة كذلك، بل أخذ بصورة جزئية ، وهذا يؤدي إلى أن تكون النتيجة أكبر من المقدمتين؛ لأن العقل لا يبرر الحكم الكلي في هذه الحالة .
And two negative premises [do not produce], and the conclusion follows the weaker of the two premises, so a composite syllogism does not produce. Consequently, it is not necessary that the individuals referred to in the minor premise are the individuals referred to in the major premise; thus the two premises are separate from each other, and therefore there is no basis for deducing a conclusion from two propositions each separate from the other. This is the secret of the condition that the middle term be distributed in one of the two premises. Among the effects of this rule—the rule of distribution—is what the author stated, namely: that no syllogism is produced from two particular premises, affirmative or negative, because the middle term in that case is not distributed in either of the two premises. (1) And this ruling depends on the rule concerning quality, which requires that 'no syllogism is produced from two negative premises, universal or particular, unless the two negatives are in the position of an affirmative,' as stated by al-Suhrawardi in Mantiq al-Talwihat (48). That is because the middle term then never connects the two premises; for the minor premise—being negative—separates the minor term from the middle, and the major premise—also being negative—separates the middle from the major. This means that the three terms of the syllogism are completely separated from each other, so no conclusion can be deduced from these terms—or rather propositions—which have no relation at all. (2) This also follows from the rule of distribution, because if the conclusion did not follow the weaker premise, but were universal negative or affirmative, then its subject would necessarily be distributed, and its predicate might also be distributed; and this is conceivable in ten moods of the four figures, which I have omitted due to lack of space. And this term which is distributed in the conclusion—subject or predicate—is not distributed in its place in the premise in those moods, while the rule requires that a term distributed in the conclusion be distributed in the premise in which it occurs. In other words: if a term of the two terms of the conclusion appears as universal—subject or predicate—and this term was not taken in the premise similarly, but was taken as particular, then this leads to the conclusion being larger than the premises, because the intellect does not justify the universal judgment in this case.
Source pages p. 499

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the general conditions for a valid syllogism. The author begins by affirming two key rules: (1) a syllogism cannot consist of two negative premises, and (2) the conclusion always follows the weaker premise in both quantity and quality. He explains the logic behind these: if both premises are negative, the middle term fails to connect the minor and major terms, effectively isolating all three terms. The same applies when both premises are particular—the middle term is not distributed, so no universal conclusion can be drawn. This leads to the fundamental principle that the middle term must be distributed in at least one premise. The author then relates this to the rule of distribution: a term distributed in the conclusion must have been distributed in its premise, otherwise the conclusion would exceed the premises. He illustrates this with a hypothetical case where the conclusion is universal but the terms were not universally taken in the premises, which would lack rational justification.

Key Terms
الحد الأوسط
الاستغراق
النتيجة تتبع أخس المقدمتين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why do two negative premises fail to produce a conclusion?
A) Because the middle term is distributed in both premises.
B) Because the middle term connects the extremes only through affirmation.
C) Because the conclusion automatically becomes affirmative.
D) Because the minor premise is always particular.

Key Takeaway
A valid syllogism requires that the middle term be distributed in at least one premise, and the conclusion must match the weaker premise in quality and quantity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: Consider the following syllogism: 'No khamr is halal. Every nabidh is khamr. Therefore, no nabidh is halal.' Is this syllogism valid? Apply the rule about two negatives and explain.
?
Task 2: Given the premises 'Some animals are permitted for sacrifice' and 'Every goat is an animal,' what conclusion, if any, follows? Check the rule about the conclusion following the weaker premise.

The Syllogism
beginner

The Two Conditions for First Figure Syllogisms
Author specifies conditions for first figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and verify the two necessary conditions (affirmative minor and universal major) for a valid first-figure categorical syllogism.

Core Idea
The first figure demands affirmative minor and universal major to ensure subsumption.

The first figure is the most powerful in yielding certain conclusions, but it comes with strict conditions. The author lays out two key requirements: the minor premise must be affirmative, and the major premise must be universal. Without these, subsumption fails and the syllogism is invalid. Let's examine why.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions
Find in the passage the explicit conditions for the first figure: affirmation of the minor and universality of the major.
The author states: 'For [the first figure] is affirmation of the minor and universality of the major'.
2
Understand the role of subsumption
The author says 'so that subsumption is necessary'. Subsumption (al-indirāj) means the minor term falls under the middle term, which then connects to the major via the universal major.
If the middle term is completely within the major (universal major), and the minor is within the middle (affirmative minor), then the minor must be within the major.
3
Test each violation
What happens if the minor is negative? The middle term is separated from the minor, breaking the link. What if the major is particular? Then the middle may not be fully contained in the major, so the conclusion does not necessarily follow.
The passage explains that if the minor is negative, the judgment is a negation, so no positive connection.
4
Connect to the distribution rule
The commentary explains that a negative minor leads to a distributed major term in the conclusion that was not distributed in the premise, violating the distribution rule.
This is a rational justification for why the conditions are necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الحجة من كلية وجزئية إلا جزئية، وكيفا )) ، فلا ينتج قياس مركب من موجبة وسالبة إلا سالبة ، ( بالاستقراء) التام ، فإنا تتبعنا جميع الأقيسة [ف]وجدناها كذلك. ثم لكل من الأشكال شروط كما وكيفا وجهةً، وبدأ المصنف بالشروط شرط إنتاج الشكل الأول الكم والكيف، وذكر الشروط في الجهة في المختلطات، وقال: ویست و بیان ضروبه في الشكل (الْأَوَّلِ إيجاب الصغرى وكلية الكبرى : لبلزم ) الاندراح ) . أي اندراج الأصغر تحت الأوسط، فيلزم النتيجة، وإذا اختل أحدهما فات الاندراج . أما إذا كان الصغرى سالبة فلأن الحكم فيها حينئذ بسلب الأوسط عن (۱) وذلك لأن الحد الأوسط مفصول عن الأصغر أو الأكبر حال كون إحدى المقدمتين سالبة، ومعناه أن النتيجة لا تربط الأصغر والأكبر ربطا إيجابيا، وبالتالي تصير النتيجة سالبة حتما، وهو معنى تبعية النتيجة لأخص المقدمتين . (٢) وأضاف السهروردي في منطق التلويحات (٤٨) إلى هذه القواعد العامة قاعدة أخرى. هي أنه لا إنتاج عن صغرى سالبة وكبرى جزئية. والحق أن ما ذكره من القواعد ثابتة بتبريرات عقلية، فصلناها لك سابقا ، وليس العمدة فيه الاستقراء، خلافا لما صنيع المصنف، تبعا لما قاله شارح المطالع (١٨٤)، والله أعلم. (۳) كذا في حمد الله ، وفي بحر العلوم «ليستلزم». وهذا يوحي (٤) وأيضا إذا كانت الصغرى سالبة وجب أن تكون الكبرى موجبة؛ لاستحالة الإنتاج من سالبتين ، وبالتالي لزم أن تكون النتيجة سالبة ؛ لكونها تابعة لأخس المقدمتين في الكيف، يعني أن الحد الأكبر في النتيجة مستغرق ؛ لكونه محمولا في نتيجة سالبة ، بينما هو غير مستغرق في المقدمة التي جاء فيها - وهي الكبرى - حيث كان محمولا في كلية موجبة ، ومحمول الكلية الموجبة غير مستغرق. وهذا يخل بقاعدة الاستغراق ، وبالتالي يفسد القياس، وبعبارة موجزة: لو لم تكن الصغرى موجبة لاستغرق حد في النتيجة لم يستغرق في مقدمته. وهذا ما يعنيه المناطقة بقولهم إذا نفيت شيئا عن شيء لم يكن المنتجة
Chapter on Argument: From a universal and particular [premise] only a particular [conclusion], and from quality: a syllogism composed of affirmative and negative only produces a negative [conclusion], by complete induction, for we have examined all syllogisms and found them thus. Then each of the figures has conditions in quantity, quality, and modality. The author began with the conditions for production of the first figure in quantity and quality, and mentioned the conditions in modality regarding mixed [premises]. He said: 'And [the condition] for [the first figure] is affirmation of the minor and universality of the major, so that subsumption is necessary.' That is, the subsumption of the minor under the middle, so the conclusion follows. If either is violated, subsumption fails. As for when the minor is negative, because the judgment then is a negation of the middle from [the minor]... (1) Because the middle term is separated from the minor or the major when one of the premises is negative, meaning the conclusion does not connect the minor and major positively, so the conclusion is necessarily negative, which is the meaning of the conclusion following the weaker premise. (2) Al-Suhrawardi added in Mantiq al-Talwihat (48) to these general rules another rule: that no syllogism is produced from a negative minor and a particular major. The truth is that what he mentioned of rules is established by rational justifications, which we have detailed for you earlier, and reliance is not on induction, contrary to what the author did, following the commentator of al-Matali' (184). And Allah knows best. (3) Thus in Hamdallah, and in Bahr al-Ulum 'laysatalzim' (does not necessitate). This suggests... (4) Also, if the minor is negative, then the major must be affirmative, because production from two negatives is impossible; consequently the conclusion must be negative, since it follows the weaker premise in quality. That means the major term in the conclusion is distributed because it is the predicate of a negative conclusion, while it is not distributed in the premise in which it occurs—namely the major premise—where it was a predicate of a universal affirmative, and the predicate of a universal affirmative is not distributed. This violates the rule of distribution, and thus corrupts the syllogism. In brief: if the minor were not affirmative, a term would be distributed in the conclusion that was not distributed in its premise. And this is what the logicians mean by their saying: 'If you negate something from something...'
Source pages p. 500

Commentary
This passage presents the specific conditions for the first figure of the categorical syllogism. It begins with a general rule: from a universal and a particular premise, only a particular conclusion follows; and from an affirmative and a negative premise, only a negative conclusion follows. These are established by complete induction. Then the author turns to the first figure, stating that it requires an affirmative minor premise and a universal major premise. The reason is that the minor term must be subsumed under the middle term, which is secured by an affirmative minor (affirming the minor's inclusion in the middle) and a universal major (so that the middle entirely lies within the major). If either condition fails, subsumption is lost. For example, if the minor is negative, the middle term is separated from the minor, destroying the link. The commentary then mentions al-Suhrawardi's addition: no syllogism from a negative minor and a particular major. The author critiques the reliance on induction for these rules, arguing they are rationally justified. Finally, a further explanation is given: if the minor is negative and the major must be affirmative (to avoid two negatives), then the major term ends up distributed in the negative conclusion but undistributed in its premise (since it is the predicate of a universal affirmative). This violates the distribution rule and invalidates the syllogism.

Key Terms
الشكل الأول
الاندراج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the conditions of the first figure, what happens if the minor premise is negative?
A) The syllogism produces a negative conclusion.
B) The syllogism becomes invalid because the middle term is separated from the minor.
C) The syllogism requires the major to be particular.
D) The syllogism remains valid if the major is universal.

Key Takeaway
The first figure's validity hinges on an affirmative minor (to ensure subsumption) and a universal major (to cover the middle).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the following syllogism: 'Every act of worship is an act of obedience. Some acts of obedience are not prescribed. Therefore, some acts of worship are not prescribed.' Is this a valid first-figure syllogism? Check the conditions and explain.
?
Construct a valid first-figure syllogism on a tema from Fiqh (e.g., obligations) meeting the two conditions.

Why Induction is Not the Foundation: The Rational Basis for Syllogistic Rules
Author critiques induction as basis for syllogistic rules.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between inductive and deductive justification of logical rules, and evaluate the author's critique of reliance on induction.

Core Idea
Logical rules are ultimately rational, not merely inductive.

The author, commenting on the conditions, points out that the rules are established by rational justifications, not merely by complete induction. This is a subtle but important point: while induction (surveying all cases) can confirm the rules, the real foundation is a priori reasoning. This lesson unpacks that critique.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim about induction
Find where the author says the rules are established by complete induction and then criticizes reliance on it. The passage says: 'by complete induction, for we have examined all syllogisms and found them thus' and later 'the truth is that what he mentioned of rules is established by rational justifications... and reliance is not on induction'.
2
Understand the critique
Why is induction insufficient? Induction merely observes that all examined cases fit a pattern, but it does not provide the necessity required for logical rules. Rational justification, like the distribution argument, shows why the rule must hold.
The author says 'ليس العمدة فيه الاستقراء' (reliance is not on induction).
3
Examine al-Suhrawardi's additional rule
Al-Suhrawardi adds that no syllogism is produced from a negative minor and a particular major. This is an extension of the conditions, and the author notes it is also rationally justified.
This rule is not derived from induction but from the same rational principles.
4
Reconstruct the rational justification
How would you prove the necessity of affirmative minor and universal major without induction? The passage provides a proof: if the minor is negative, then the major term becomes distributed in the conclusion but not in the premise, violating the distribution rule.
This is a deductive argument, not an inductive one.

Source Text

(4) Also, if the minor is negative, then the major must be affirmative, because production from two negatives is impossible; consequently the conclusion must be negative, since it follows the weaker premise in quality. That means the major term in the conclusion is distributed because it is the predicate of a negative conclusion, while it is not distributed in the premise in which it occurs—namely the major premise—where it was a predicate of a universal affirmative, and the predicate of a universal affirmative is not distributed. This violates the rule of distribution, and thus corrupts the syllogism. In brief: if the minor were not affirmative, a term would be distributed in the conclusion that was not distributed in its premise. And this is what the logicians mean by their saying: 'If you negate something from something...'
Source pages p. 500

Commentary
This passage presents the specific conditions for the first figure of the categorical syllogism. It begins with a general rule: from a universal and a particular premise, only a particular conclusion follows; and from an affirmative and a negative premise, only a negative conclusion follows. These are established by complete induction. Then the author turns to the first figure, stating that it requires an affirmative minor premise and a universal major premise. The reason is that the minor term must be subsumed under the middle term, which is secured by an affirmative minor (affirming the minor's inclusion in the middle) and a universal major (so that the middle entirely lies within the major). If either condition fails, subsumption is lost. For example, if the minor is negative, the middle term is separated from the minor, destroying the link. The commentary then mentions al-Suhrawardi's addition: no syllogism from a negative minor and a particular major. The author critiques the reliance on induction for these rules, arguing they are rationally justified. Finally, a further explanation is given: if the minor is negative and the major must be affirmative (to avoid two negatives), then the major term ends up distributed in the negative conclusion but undistributed in its premise (since it is the predicate of a universal affirmative). This violates the distribution rule and invalidates the syllogism.

Key Terms
الاستقراء التام
الاندراج
الحد الأوسط

The Syllogism

Conditions for a Valid First-Figure Syllogism
Author explains conditions for first figure productivity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to verify whether a first-figure categorical syllogism is valid by checking the affirmation of the minor and the universality of the major.

Core Idea
The first figure requires an affirmative minor and a universal major for the conclusion to follow.

In this lesson, we examine the two essential conditions that a first-figure syllogism must meet to produce a necessary conclusion. The passage explains why these conditions are necessary and how they ensure that the minor term is properly subsumed under the middle term. Understanding these conditions will enable you to test any first-figure syllogism for validity before drawing a conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the major and minor premises
In any categorical syllogism, locate the premise that contains the major term (the predicate of the conclusion) and the premise that contains the minor term (the subject of the conclusion).
2
Check the minor premise for affirmation
The minor premise must be affirmative. If it is negative (e.g., 'No nabidh is an intoxicant'), the middle term is denied of the minor, so the major's judgment cannot extend to it.
3
Check the major premise for universality
The major premise must be universal (either universal affirmative or universal negative). If it is particular, the judgment applies only to part of the middle term, and the minor may not be included.
4
Verify subsumption (indirāj)
Ensure the minor term is subsumed under the middle term as affirmed in the major premise. Subsumption is necessary for the conclusion to follow.
5
Evaluate validity
If both conditions are met and the syllogism is properly formed, it is valid. If either condition is violated, the syllogism is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم لكل من الأشكال شروط كما وكيفا وجهةً، وبدأ المصنف بالشروط شرط إنتاج الشكل الأول الكم والكيف، وذكر الشروط في الجهة في المختلطات، وقال: ويست وبيان ضروبه في الشكل الأول إيجاب الصغرى وكلية الكبرى : لبلزم الاندراح. أي اندراج الأصغر تحت الأوسط، فيلزم النتيجة، وإذا اختل أحدهما فات الاندراج. أما إذا كان الصغرى سالبة فلأن الحكم فيها حينئذ بسلب الأوسط عن الأصغر، والحكم في الكبرى على ما ثبت له الأوسط، فلا يدخل فيه الأصغر، فيجوز أن لا ينجر إليه حكم الكبرى. وأما إذا كانت الكبرى جزئية فلأن الحكم فيها حينئذ على بعض ما ثبت له الأوسط، ويجوز أن يكون ذلك البعض غير الأصغر، فلا ينجر إليه الحكم. واحتمال الضروب في كل شكل ستة عشر، حاصلة من ضرب المحصورات الأربع الصغريات في أنفسها الكبريات، وأسقط ههنا شرط الإيجاب ثمانية، حاصلة من ضرب السالبتين الصغريين مع الكبريات الأربع، وشرط الكلية أربعة، حاصلة من ضرب الموجبتين الصغريين في الجزئيتين الكبريين، بقي أربعة: الموجبتين الصغريين مع الكبريين الكليتين السالبة الكلية والموجبة الكلية، منتجة لمطالب أربعة، وهي المحصورات الأربع بالضرورة.
Then for each figure there are conditions in quantity, quality, and modality. The author began with the conditions for the production of the first figure in quantity and quality, and mentioned the conditions in modality in the mixed syllogisms. He said: 'And its conditions and the explanation of its moods in the first figure are: the affirmation of the minor and the universality of the major, so that subsumption is necessary.' That is, the subsumption of the minor term under the middle term, thus the conclusion follows. If one of them is violated, subsumption fails. As for if the minor is negative, because the judgment in it then is the negation of the middle term from the minor, and the judgment in the major is about that which the middle term is affirmed of, so the minor is not included in it, and thus it is possible that the judgment of the major does not extend to it. And as for if the major is particular, because the judgment in it then is about some of that which the middle term is affirmed of, and it is possible that that part is other than the minor, so the judgment does not extend to it. And the possibility of moods in every figure is sixteen, resulting from multiplying the four quantified minor premises by the four quantified major premises. Here the condition of affirmation removes eight, resulting from multiplying the two negative minors with the four majors. And the condition of universality removes four, resulting from multiplying the two affirmative minors with the two particular majors. There remain four: the two affirmative minors with the two universal majors (the universal negative and the universal affirmative), productive for four demands, which are the four quantified propositions necessarily.
Source pages p. 500

Commentary
This passage explains the conditions for a valid syllogism in the first figure and then shows how these conditions reduce the possible moods to exactly four. The author states that each figure has conditions in quantity (universal/particular), quality (affirmative/negative), and modality (necessary/contingent). For the first figure, the condition in quantity and quality is that the minor premise must be affirmative and the major premise must be universal. The reason given is that only then does the minor term properly subsume under the middle term, allowing the conclusion to follow. If the minor is negative, the middle term is denied of the minor, so the major cannot be applied. If the major is particular, the judgment applies only to part of the middle term, not necessarily including the minor. Then the author calculates the total possible moods in any figure as 16 (4 types of minor × 4 types of major). Applying the condition of affirmation (eliminates all moods with a negative minor, which is 2 possibilities for minor × 4 for major = 8) and the condition of universality (eliminates moods with a particular major, which are the 2 affirmative minors × 2 particular majors = 4) leaves 4 productive moods. These four moods pair an affirmative minor with a universal major (either universal affirmative or universal negative), and they necessarily produce one of the four quantified propositions as conclusion. For example, in Fiqh: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' (major universal affirmative), 'Every nabidh is an intoxicant' (minor affirmative) yields 'Every nabidh is forbidden'. If the major were particular, e.g., 'Some intoxicants are forbidden', the conclusion would not follow because not all intoxicants are covered. This passage is a standard exposition of the foundation of categorical syllogistic.

Deriving the Four Productive Moods of the First Figure
Author derives four valid moods from 16 possibilities

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the four valid moods of the first figure from the 16 possible premise combinations by applying the conditions of affirmation and universality.

Core Idea
From 16 possibilities, only four moods survive: those pairing an affirmative minor with a universal major.

This lesson examines the systematic elimination process that yields exactly four productive moods in the first figure. The passage calculates the total possible premise combinations (16) and shows how the conditions reduce them to four. Understanding this derivation will help you recognize the only valid mood patterns in first-figure reasoning.

Guided Path
1
List all 16 premise combinations
Consider the four quantified minor premises (universal affirmative A, universal negative E, particular affirmative I, particular negative O) and the four quantified major premises (A, E, I, O). Multiply them to get 16 possible pairs.
2
Apply the condition of affirmation
Eliminate all pairs where the minor is negative (E or O). Since there are two negative minors and four majors, this removes 8 pairs. Remaining: 8 pairs with affirmative minors (A or I).
3
Apply the condition of universality
From the remaining 8, eliminate all pairs where the major is particular (I or O). There are 2 particular majors and 2 affirmative minors, so this removes 4 pairs. Remaining: 4 pairs.
4
Identify the four moods
The remaining four pairs are: minor A with major A (Barbara), minor A with major E (Celarent), minor I with major A (Darii), minor I with major E (Ferio). Each produces a conclusion: A, E, I, O respectively.
5
Verify with Islamic examples
For each mood, construct a real fiqh or kalam example. For instance, Barbara: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden; every nabidh is an intoxicant; therefore every nabidh is forbidden.' Check that the conditions hold.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Four Productive Moods of the First Figure
Author calculates the 16 possible mood combinations and reduces them to 4 productive moods in the first figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the four productive moods of the first figure and justify why the other twelve combinations are invalid based on the conditions of affirmation and universality.

Core Idea
Only 4 of 16 first-figure moods produce valid conclusions; conditions eliminate the rest.

In the first figure of the categorical syllogism, not every combination of premises yields a valid conclusion. This passage shows you how to systematically reduce the 16 possible mood combinations to just four that are productive (produce necessary conclusions) in the first figure. The key conditions are that the minor premise must be affirmative and the major premise must be universal. By the end of this lesson, you will master this reduction and know exactly which moods work.

Guided Path
1
Understand the 16 possible mood combinations
There are four types of categorical propositions (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative). With two premises, the total combinations are 4×4 = 16. These are the moods to be evaluated.
The minor and major premises each can be any of the four types.
2
Apply the condition of affirmative minor
In the first figure, the minor premise must be affirmative. This eliminates any mood where the minor is negative. Since there are 2 negative minors (universal negative and particular negative), each can combine with 4 majors, giving 2×4 = 8 invalid moods.
The text says 'condition of affirmation drops eight'.
3
Apply the condition of universal major
After the first condition, 8 moods remain. The major premise must be universal. This eliminates moods where the major is particular. Among the remaining 8, the minors are always affirmative. The two particular majors (particular affirmative and particular negative) each combine with the two affirmative minors, giving 2×2 = 4 more invalid moods.
The text says 'condition of universality drops four'.
4
Identify the four remaining productive moods
After both conditions, exactly four moods remain: (1) minor universal affirmative + major universal affirmative → universal affirmative (Barbari). (2) minor universal affirmative + major universal negative → universal negative (Celarent). (3) minor particular affirmative + major universal affirmative → particular affirmative (Darii). (4) minor particular affirmative + major universal negative → particular negative (Ferio). These four are productive in the first figure.
The text lists these four explicitly.
5
Recognise the special property of the first figure
The first figure uniquely produces all four types of categorical conclusions, especially the universal affirmative, which no other figure can produce. This makes it the most perfect and demonstrative figure.
This property is mentioned at the end of the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأصغر، والحكم في الكبرى على ما ثبت له الأوسط، فلا يدخل فيه الأصغر، فيجوز أن لا ينجر إليه حكم الكبرى. وأما إذا كانت الكبرى جزئية فلأن الحكم فيها حينئذ على بعض ما ثبت له الأوسط، ويجوز أن يكون ذلك البعض غير الأصغر، فلا ينجر إليه الحكم. (وَاحْتِمَالُ الضُّرُوبِ فِي كُلِّ شَكل سِتَّةَ عَشَرَ)، حاصلة من ضرب المحصورات الأربع الصغريات في أنفسها الكبرياتِ، وَأَسْقَطَ هَهُنَا شَرْطُ الْإِيجَاب ثَمَانِيَةً)، حاصلة من ضرب السالبتين الصغريين مع الكبريات الأربع، (وَشَرْطُ الْكُلَّيَّةِ أَرْبَعَةً)، حاصلة من ضرب الموجبتين الصغريين في الجزئيتين الكبريين، (بَقِيَ أَرْبَعَةُ : الْمُوجَبَتَيْنِ الصغريين مَعَ الْكُبْرَيَيْنِ الْكُلِّيتَيْنِ السالبة الكلية والموجبة الكلية، مُنْتِجَةً لِمَطَالِبَ أَرْبَعَةٍ، وهي المحصورات الأربع بِالضَّرُورَةِ . فالحاصل من موجبة كلية صغرى وكبرى الضرب الأول، المنتج للموجبة الكلية والحاصل من صغرى كلية وكبرى سالبة الضرب الثاني، المنتج للسالبة الكلية والحاصل من جزئية صغرى، وموجبة كلية كبرى الضرب الثالث، المنتج للموجبة الجزئية والحاصل من جزئية صغرى وسالبة كبرى الضرب الرابع، المنتج للسالبة الجزئية. (وَذَلِكَ)، أي إنتاج النتائج الأربع مِنْ خَاصَّتِهِ، كَالْإِيجَابِ الْكُلِّي. لأنه غيره من الأشكال لا ينتج الإيجاب الكلي.
The minor, and the judgment in the major is on what the middle term is established for, so the minor does not enter it; thus it is possible that the judgment of the major does not extend to it. And if the major is particular, then the judgment therein is on some of what the middle term is established for, and it is possible that that part is other than the minor, so the judgment does not extend to it. (And the possibility of moods in every figure is sixteen), resulting from multiplying the four categorical minors by themselves as majors. (And the condition of affirmation drops eight here), resulting from multiplying the two negative minors by the four majors. (And the condition of universality drops four), resulting from multiplying the two affirmative minors by the two particular majors. (Four remain: the two affirmative minors with the two universal majors, the universal negative and universal affirmative, productive necessarily for four demands, namely the four categoricals. (And that is), meaning the production of the four conclusions, from its properties, like the universal affirmative. Because other figures do not produce the universal affirmative.

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the first figure of the categorical syllogism. It begins by explaining a condition: the judgment in the major premise applies to what the middle term is established for, without including the minor directly. If the major were particular, the judgment would only cover part of what the middle term applies to, and that part might not be the minor, so the conclusion would not follow. Thus the major must be universal. The author then calculates the possible moods: there are 16 possible combinations from the four types of categorical propositions (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative) as both minor and major. However, conditions restrict them. The condition of affirmation eliminates 8 moods where the minor is negative, because in the first figure the minor must be affirmative. The condition of universality eliminates 4 moods where the major is particular. This leaves 4 productive moods. The four moods are listed: (1) affirmative universal minor and major → universal affirmative conclusion; (2) universal minor and negative major → universal negative; (3) particular minor and universal affirmative major → particular affirmative; (4) particular minor and negative major → particular negative. The author then notes that producing all four conclusions is a special property of the first figure, especially the universal affirmative, which no other figure can produce. This property makes the first figure essential for demonstration.

Resolving the Circularity Doubt in the First Figure
Author presents the famous doubt about circularity in the first figure and solves it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the apparent circularity in the first figure and resolve it by distinguishing undetailed and detailed knowledge.

Core Idea
The circle dissolves when the major is known undetailed before the conclusion.

This passage addresses a famous objection to the first figure of the categorical syllogism: that it involves circular reasoning. At first glance, the major premise and the conclusion seem to depend on each other, creating a vicious circle. However, the author shows that the circle is only apparent by distinguishing between two kinds of knowledge: undetailed (ijmāl) and detailed (tafsīl). Understanding this distinction is crucial for defending the first figure as a valid form of reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Doubt
Read the passage and locate the two dependencies: the conclusion depends on the universality of the major, and the universality of the major depends on the truth of the conclusion. Understand why this creates an apparent circle.
The author says: 'al-natīja mawqūfa 'alā kulliyya al-kubrā' and 'bi-l-'aks, ay kulliyya al-kubrā mawqūfa 'alā ṣidq al-natīja'.
2
Understand the Dependency
The universality of the major depends on the conclusion because the minor is part of the middle term. If the judgment does not hold for the minor, it cannot be universal for all of the middle. So the major's universality requires the conclusion to be true.
The passage explains: 'li-anna al-aṣghar min jumla al-awsaṭ' – the minor is part of the middle.
3
Recognize the Solution
The author proposes that detailed knowledge (the conclusion) depends on undetailed knowledge (the major), but undetailed knowledge is independent of the detailed. Thus, there is no mutual dependence; it is one-way.
Key statement: 'al-tafṣīl huwa al-natīja mawqūf 'alā al-ijmāl alladhī huwa al-kubrā, wa 'ilmuhu ghayr mawqūf 'alā al-tafṣīl'.
4
Apply the Distinction
Consider an example from Islamic law: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' (major – undetailed). 'Nabidh is an intoxicant' (minor). 'Therefore nabidh is forbidden' (conclusion – detailed). The general rule is known first as an undetailed principle; the conclusion applies it to a specific case. The general rule does not require the conclusion to be true in advance.
This shows how the dependency is only from conclusion to major, not vice versa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَهَهُنَا شَكٍّ مَشْهُورٌ مِنْ وَجْهَيْن : الْأَوَّلُ أَنَّ النَّتِيجَةَ مَوْقُوفَةٌ عَلَى كُلِّيَّة الْكُبْرَى، وذلك ضروري، وَبِالْعَكْسِ، أي كلية الكبرى موقوفة على صدق النتيجة؛ لِأَنَّ الْأَصْغَرَ مِنْ جُمْلَةِ الْأَوْسَطِ، فَدَارَ، فما لم يعلم ثبوت الحكم للأصغر لا يعلم ثبوته للأوسط، وثبوت الحكم للأصغر هو النتيجة، فحينئذ يشتمل هذا الشكل على الدور. وَحَلُّهُ: أَنَّ التَّفْصِيلَ هو النتيجة مَوْقُوفٌ عَلَى الْإِجْمَالِ الذي هو الكبرى، وعلمه غير موقوف على التفصيل؛ إذ باختلاف الشيء إجمالا
And here is a famous doubt from two aspects: the first is that the result is dependent on the universality of the major, and that is necessary; and conversely, i.e., the universality of the major is dependent on the truth of the result, because the minor is part of the middle, so there is circularity. So unless knowledge of the judgment for the minor is established, knowledge for the middle is not established, and knowledge of the judgment for the minor is the result, so this figure involves circularity. And its solution: that the detailed, which is the result, is dependent on the undetailed, which is the major; but knowledge of it is not dependent on the detailed, because the thing differs as undetailed and detailed.
Source pages p. 502

Commentary
This passage presents a famous dialectical doubt concerning the first figure and its solution. The doubt arises from two reciprocal dependencies: (1) the conclusion depends on the major being universal (since the first figure requires a universal major to extend the judgment to the minor), and (2) the universality of the major depends on the truth of the conclusion, because the minor is part of the middle term, so establishing the universal judgment for all of the middle requires that it holds for the minor, which is the conclusion. This creates a circle (dawr). However, the author provides a solution by distinguishing between undetailed (ijmālī) and detailed (tafsīlī) knowledge. The major premise is known in an undetailed manner—as a general principle—without requiring explicit knowledge of every instance. The conclusion is the detailed application of that general principle. The undetailed knowledge does not presuppose the detailed knowledge; rather, the detailed depends on the undetailed. Thus, the dependency is one-way: the conclusion depends on the major, but not vice versa. The solution resolves the apparent circularity by showing that the major is known first in a general sense, and the conclusion is derived from it later.

Key Terms
دور
إجمال
تفصيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

According to the solution, upon which kind of knowledge does the conclusion depend?
A) Detailed knowledge (tafṣīl)
B) Undetailed knowledge (ijmāl)
C) Neither
D) Both equally

Key Takeaway
The apparent circularity in the first figure is resolved by recognizing that the major premise is known in an undetailed (general) manner independently of the conclusion, while the conclusion is the detailed application of that general knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Identify a real case in Kalam (e.g., the argument for the existence of God from contingency) where the first figure is used, and evaluate whether the circularity doubt applies and how the solution works.

The Syllogism

The First Figure's Exclusive Universal Affirmative
Enumeration of the four moods and exclusive universal affirmative

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four moods of the first figure and explain why the universal affirmative conclusion is exclusive to it.

Core Idea
The first figure alone yields the universal affirmative — the gold standard of demonstrative proof.

The first figure of the categorical syllogism is distinguished by its ability to produce all four types of conclusions, most notably the universal affirmative. This lesson unpacks that exclusive property and examines the four moods that generate them.

Guided Path
1
Locate the four moods
In the passage, find the description of the third and fourth moods. Note their premise combinations and the resulting conclusions.
The third mood: particular minor + universal negative major → particular affirmative conclusion. The fourth: particular minor + particular negative major → particular negative conclusion.
2
Identify the exclusive property
The text states that the first figure has the characteristic of producing the universal affirmative conclusion, which other figures cannot produce directly.
This property is called 'al-ījāb al-kullī' (الإيجاب الكلي).
3
Verify with other figures
Consider why the second, third, or fourth figures cannot produce a universal affirmative. For example, in the second figure the middle is predicate in both premises, leading to negative conclusions or particular affirmatives only.
The first figure's middle term position (subject in major, predicate in minor) enables subsumption of the minor under the major completely.
4
Apply to a real argument
Take a universal affirmative claim from Islamic theology, e.g., 'All temporally originated things have a cause.' Construct a first-figure syllogism that proves it.
Example: Everything temporal is originated; the world is temporal; so the world is originated. But to get a universal affirmative, you need universal major and minor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الحجة
الضرب الثالث ، المنتج للموجبة الجزئية والحاصل من جزئية صغرى وسالبة كبرى الضرب الرابع ، المنتج للسالبة الجزئية .
(وَذَلِكَ)، أي إنتاج النتائج الأربع مِنْ خَوَاصهِ ، كَالْإِيجَابِ الْكُلِّي .
لأن غيره من الأشكال لا ينتج الإيجاب الكلي.
وَهَهُنَا شَكٍّ مَشْهُورٌ مِنْ وَجْهَيْن : الْأَوَّلُ أَنَّ النَّتِيجَةَ مَوْقُوفَةٌ عَلَى كُلِّيَّة الْكُبْرَى)، وذلك ضروري ، (وَبِالْعَكْسِ)، أي كلية الكبرى موقوفة على صدق النتيجة ؛ لِأَنَّ الْأَصْغَرَ مِنْ جُمْلَةِ الْأَوْسَطِ، فَدَارَ) ، فما لم يعلم ثبوت الحكم للأصغر لا يعلم ثبوته للأوسط، وثبوت الحكم للأصغر هو النتيجة، فحينئذ يشتمل هذا الشكل على الدور .
(وَحَلُّهُ: أَنَّ التَّفْصِيلَ) هو النتيجة (مَوْقُوفٌ عَلَى الْإِجْمَالِ) الذي هو الكبرى، وعلمه غير موقوف على التفصيل ؛ إذ باختلاف الشيء إجمالا
وتفصيلا يختلف العنوان، (وَالْحُكْمُ يَخْتَلِفُ بِاخْتِلَافِ الْأَوْصَافِ) البديهية والنظرية، فيجوز أن يكون الإجمال ضروريا والتفصيل نظريا موقوفا
عليه ، (فَلَا إِشْكَالَ.
الثَّانِي: أَنَّ قَوْلَنَا الْخَلَأُ لَيْسَ بِمَوْجُودٍ، وَكُلُّ مَا لَيْسَ بِمَوْجُودٍ لَيْسَ بِمَحْسُوس يُنْتِجُ) «الخلاء ليس بمحسوس»، (مَعَ أَنَّ الصُّغْرَى سَالِبَةٌ، بَلْ كُلَّمَا تَكَرَّرَتِ النِّسْبَةُ السَّلْبِيَّةُ أَنْتَجَتْ .
وَحَلُّهُ كَمَا قِيلَ : إِنَّهَا مُوجَبَةٌ سَالِبَةُ الْمَحْمُولِ)، لا أنها سالبة، حتى يتوجه الإيراد، (يَدُلُّ عَلَى ذَلِكَ جَعْلُ النِّسْبَةِ السَّلْبِيَّةِ مِرْأَةَ لِلْأَفْرَادِ فِي الْكُبْرَى). وتفصيل هذا الجواب أن الكبرى حكم فيها بأن ما ثبت له ليس بموجود ليس
بمحسوس، فإن حكم في الصغرى بأن ليس بموجود ثابت للخلاء فحينئذ صارت موجبة سالبة المحمول، أو معدولة لا سالبة، وإلا فلا اندراج فلا إنتاج ، اللهم إلا بالعرض ؛ لملازمتها سالبة المحمول.
(أَقُولُ: وَلَكَ أَنْ تَسْتَدِلَّ مِنْ هَهُنَا عَلَى عَدَمِ اسْتِدْعَاءِ تِلْكَ الْمُوجَبَةِ الْوُجُودَ للموضوع ؛ فإن ما موضوعه معدوم إن تجعلها صغرى، وتجعل النسبة السلبية موضوعة في الكبرى أنتجت ألبتة، فلو لم يكن موجبة سالبة المحمول لزم إنتاج سالبة الصغرى فيجب كونها سالبة المحمول مع عدم الموضوع، ( فَتَدَبَّرْ ) ؛ فإن فيه غلطا فاحشا ؛ فإن غاية ما لزم إنتاج الموجبة السالبة المحمول مع عدم الموضوع نتيجة صادقة، ولا يلزم منه صدقها ؛ فإن المقدمة الكاذبة ربما تنتج نتيجة صادقة. هذا والله أعلم ، وهو العاصم.
Section on Proof
The third mood produces the particular affirmative and is composed of a particular minor and a negative major. The fourth mood produces the particular negative. 
(That is), meaning producing the four conclusions is among its [the first figure's] characteristics, like the universal affirmative. Because other figures do not produce the universal affirmative. 
And here is a famous doubt from two aspects: The first is that the conclusion depends on the universality of the major premise, and that is necessary, and conversely, i.e., the universality of the major premise depends on the truth of the conclusion, because the minor is part of the middle [term], so there is circularity. For unless the affirmation of the judgment for the minor is known, its affirmation for the middle is not known, and the affirmation of the judgment for the minor is the conclusion. Hence this figure involves circularity. 
(And its resolution: that the detailed [knowledge]) is the conclusion (depends on the summary [knowledge]) which is the major premise, but the knowledge of it does not depend on the detailed [knowledge]; since with the difference of the thing in summary and in detail the designation differs, (and the judgment differs with the difference of the descriptions) — the intuitive and the theoretical. So it is possible that the summary is necessary and the detailed is theoretical, depending on it, (so there is no problem). 
The second: that our saying "The void is not existent" and "Everything not existent is not sensed" produces "The void is not sensed", even though the minor is negative. Rather, whenever the negative relation is repeated, it produces. 
And its resolution is as has been said: it is an affirmative with a negative predicate, not a negative, so that the objection does not apply. (This is indicated by making the negative relation a mirror for the individuals in the major premise). The details of this answer: the major premise rules that whatever has been established as not existent is not sensed. Then the minor rules that not existent is established for the void. Hence it becomes an affirmative with a negative predicate, or a modified [proposition], not a negative. Otherwise there is no subsumption and no production, except accidentally due to its being concomitant with the negative predicate. 
(I say: and you may argue from this that such an affirmative does not require existence of the subject. For if you make its subject non-existent as the minor, and make the negative relation the subject in the major, it produces absolutely. If it were not an affirmative with a negative predicate, then the production from a negative minor would be necessary, so it must be an affirmative with a negative predicate despite the non-existence of the subject. So ponder, for there is a gross error in that; for the most that is required is that the affirmative with a negative predicate produces a true conclusion despite the non-existence of the subject, but its truth does not follow; for a false premise may produce a true conclusion. And God knows best, and He is the Protector.)
Source pages p. 502

Commentary
This passage from a commentary on the Sullam al-Ulum discusses the distinctive features of the first figure of the categorical syllogism and resolves two famous objections. First, the author enumerates the third and fourth moods: the third mood produces a particular affirmative conclusion from a particular minor and a universal negative major; the fourth mood produces a particular negative conclusion from a particular minor and a particular negative major. The key point is that the first figure alone can produce a universal affirmative conclusion; other figures cannot, which gives it a special status in demonstrative proof. Then two objections are raised. The first objection alleges circularity: the conclusion of the first figure depends on the universality of the major premise, but that universality itself seems to depend on the truth of the conclusion because the minor term is included in the middle term. The resolution distinguishes between summary (ijmāl) and detailed (tafsīl) knowledge: the major premise is summary knowledge, which is immediate and necessary, while the conclusion is detailed knowledge, which is theoretical and depends on the summary. Since the description differs, the circularity is broken. The second objection involves a syllogism with two seemingly negative premises: 'The void is not existent' and 'Everything not existent is not sensed' leading to 'The void is not sensed.' In a proper categorical syllogism, two negatives do not produce a conclusion. The commentator resolves this by reinterpreting the minor as an affirmative proposition with a negative predicate (مُوجَبَةٌ سَالِبَةُ الْمَحْمُولِ), i.e., 'The void is non-existent', which is affirmative in quality but has a negative predicate. The major is also analyzed as containing a negative relation as a predicate. Thus, the premises are actually affirmative, and the conclusion follows validly. The author adds a further reflection: this analysis shows that an affirmative proposition does not require the existence of its subject, as the void is non-existent yet the argument works. He warns against an error: a false premise can produce a true conclusion, so one must not conclude that the premises are true from the truth of the conclusion. All Islamic examples are drawn from logical discourse (void, existence, sensation) which were standard in medieval Islamic philosophy.

Key Terms
الضرب
الشكل الأول
الموجبة الكلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is a characteristic exclusive to the first figure of the categorical syllogism?
A) It can produce a particular affirmative conclusion.
B) It can produce a universal affirmative conclusion.
C) It can produce a particular negative conclusion.
D) It can produce a universal negative conclusion.

Key Takeaway
The first figure uniquely yields a universal affirmative conclusion directly, making it the foundation of demonstrative proof in logic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Construct a first-figure categorical syllogism in the Barbara mood (AAA) on a fiqh issue. For example, use the premise 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' and 'Wine is an intoxicant' to conclude 'Wine is forbidden.' Identify the mood and verify that it uses the first figure.

The Syllogism

Resolving the Circularity Objection in the First Figure
Resolution of the circularity objection via summary and detailed knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the distinction between summary and detailed knowledge to resolve the circularity objection in a first-figure syllogism.

Core Idea
Circularity is avoided because summary knowledge is immediate while detailed knowledge is derived — they are different cognitive acts.

A famous objection claims that the first figure involves circular reasoning: the conclusion depends on the universality of the major premise, and that universality depends on the truth of the conclusion. This lesson explores the resolution which distinguishes between summary (ijmāl) and detailed (tafsīl) knowledge.

Guided Path
1
State the circularity objection
The objection: the conclusion (that the minor is a subset of the major) depends on the major premise being universal. But the major premise's universality itself depends on that conclusion, because the minor is part of the middle term. This creates a circle.
read the passage: 'the conclusion depends on the universality of the major... and conversely.'
2
Identify the two kinds of knowledge
The resolution distinguishes between summary knowledge (ijmāl) and detailed knowledge (tafsīl). The major premise is summary; the conclusion is detailed.
Summary: general, immediate. Detailed: specific, derived.
3
Explain why no circle occurs
The summary knowledge is known independently (e.g., through intuition or axiom). The detailed knowledge depends on the summary, but the summary does not depend on the detailed. Since the descriptions (alwāṣif) differ, the epistemic dependence is one-way.
The passage says: 'the judgment differs with the difference of the descriptions.'
4
Apply to an Islamic example
Consider the universal premise 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' as summary knowledge from revelation. The conclusion 'Wine is forbidden' is detailed knowledge derived from that universal plus the minor 'Wine is an intoxicant.' The universal does not depend on the conclusion.
The summary is known independently (Quran/Sunnah), so no circle.

Source Text

Section on Proof
The third mood produces the particular affirmative and is composed of a particular minor and a negative major. The fourth mood produces the particular negative. 
(That is), meaning producing the four conclusions is among its [the first figure's] characteristics, like the universal affirmative. Because other figures do not produce the universal affirmative. 
And here is a famous doubt from two aspects: The first is that the conclusion depends on the universality of the major premise, and that is necessary, and conversely, i.e., the universality of the major premise depends on the truth of the conclusion, because the minor is part of the middle [term], so there is circularity. For unless the affirmation of the judgment for the minor is known, its affirmation for the middle is not known, and the affirmation of the judgment for the minor is the conclusion. Hence this figure involves circularity. 
(And its resolution: that the detailed [knowledge]) is the conclusion (depends on the summary [knowledge]) which is the major premise, but the knowledge of it does not depend on the detailed [knowledge]; since with the difference of the thing in summary and in detail the designation differs, (and the judgment differs with the difference of the descriptions) — the intuitive and the theoretical. So it is possible that the summary is necessary and the detailed is theoretical, depending on it, (so there is no problem). 
The second: that our saying "The void is not existent" and "Everything not existent is not sensed" produces "The void is not sensed", even though the minor is negative. Rather, whenever the negative relation is repeated, it produces. 
And its resolution is as has been said: it is an affirmative with a negative predicate, not a negative, so that the objection does not apply. (This is indicated by making the negative relation a mirror for the individuals in the major premise). The details of this answer: the major premise rules that whatever has been established as not existent is not sensed. Then the minor rules that not existent is established for the void. Hence it becomes an affirmative with a negative predicate, or a modified [proposition], not a negative. Otherwise there is no subsumption and no production, except accidentally due to its being concomitant with the negative predicate. 
(I say: and you may argue from this that such an affirmative does not require existence of the subject. For if you make its subject non-existent as the minor, and make the negative relation the subject in the major, it produces absolutely. If it were not an affirmative with a negative predicate, then the production from a negative minor would be necessary, so it must be an affirmative with a negative predicate despite the non-existence of the subject. So ponder, for there is a gross error in that; for the most that is required is that the affirmative with a negative predicate produces a true conclusion despite the non-existence of the subject, but its truth does not follow; for a false premise may produce a true conclusion. And God knows best, and He is the Protector.)
Source pages p. 502

Commentary
This passage from a commentary on the Sullam al-Ulum discusses the distinctive features of the first figure of the categorical syllogism and resolves two famous objections. First, the author enumerates the third and fourth moods: the third mood produces a particular affirmative conclusion from a particular minor and a universal negative major; the fourth mood produces a particular negative conclusion from a particular minor and a particular negative major. The key point is that the first figure alone can produce a universal affirmative conclusion; other figures cannot, which gives it a special status in demonstrative proof. Then two objections are raised. The first objection alleges circularity: the conclusion of the first figure depends on the universality of the major premise, but that universality itself seems to depend on the truth of the conclusion because the minor term is included in the middle term. The resolution distinguishes between summary (ijmāl) and detailed (tafsīl) knowledge: the major premise is summary knowledge, which is immediate and necessary, while the conclusion is detailed knowledge, which is theoretical and depends on the summary. Since the description differs, the circularity is broken. The second objection involves a syllogism with two seemingly negative premises: 'The void is not existent' and 'Everything not existent is not sensed' leading to 'The void is not sensed.' In a proper categorical syllogism, two negatives do not produce a conclusion. The commentator resolves this by reinterpreting the minor as an affirmative proposition with a negative predicate (مُوجَبَةٌ سَالِبَةُ الْمَحْمُولِ), i.e., 'The void is non-existent', which is affirmative in quality but has a negative predicate. The major is also analyzed as containing a negative relation as a predicate. Thus, the premises are actually affirmative, and the conclusion follows validly. The author adds a further reflection: this analysis shows that an affirmative proposition does not require the existence of its subject, as the void is non-existent yet the argument works. He warns against an error: a false premise can produce a true conclusion, so one must not conclude that the premises are true from the truth of the conclusion. All Islamic examples are drawn from logical discourse (void, existence, sensation) which were standard in medieval Islamic philosophy.

Key Terms
الدور
التفصيل
الإجمال

Key Takeaway
The circularity objection is resolved by recognizing that the major premise is summary knowledge, which is independent, while the conclusion is detailed knowledge derived from it.

Negative Premises Reinterpreted: Affirmative with Negative Predicate
Reinterpretation of negative premises as affirmatives with negative predicate

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reinterpret a seemingly negative minor premise as an affirmative with a negative predicate to validate a first-figure syllogism.

Core Idea
A negative minor can be recast as an affirmative with a negative predicate — saving the syllogism from invalidity.

The second objection to the first figure involves a syllogism that appears to have two negative premises: 'The void is not existent' and 'Everything not existent is not sensed' leading to 'The void is not sensed.' Since two negatives normally do not produce a conclusion in categorical logic, the commentator resolves this by showing the premises are actually affirmative with negative predicates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the apparent problem
The syllogism has a minor premise 'The void is not existent' (negative) and a major 'Everything not existent is not sensed' (negative). According to the rules of categorical syllogism, two negatives yield no conclusion. So how does the conclusion follow?
The commentator calls this a 'famous doubt'.
2
Examine the commentator's solution
The solution is to reinterpret the minor premise as an affirmative proposition with a negative predicate (mūjaba sāliba al-maḥmūl), i.e., 'The void is non-existent.' This makes it affirmative in quality, though the predicate includes a negation.
The major premise can also be seen as 'Everything not existent is not sensed' — which is a universal affirmative with a negative predicate: 'All not-existent is not-sensed.'
3
Verify the validity
Now both premises are affirmative (though with negative predicates). In the first figure, an affirmative minor and affirmative major produce a valid conclusion. The minor term 'void' falls under the middle 'not-existent', which falls under the major 'not-sensed', so the conclusion follows.
The commentator notes that this is indicated by 'making the negative relation a mirror for the individuals in the major premise.'
4
Consider the truth condition
The author adds a caution: an affirmative with negative predicate does not require the existence of the subject. The void is non-existent, but the argument still works. However, a true conclusion does not guarantee true premises; a false premise can yield a true conclusion.
This addresses a potential error in the reasoning.

الضرب الثالث ، المنتج للموجبة الجزئية والحاصل من جزئية صغرى وسالبة كبرى الضرب الرابع ، المنتج للسالبة الجزئية .

لأن غيره من الأشكال لا ينتج الإيجاب الكلي.
وَهَهُنَا شَكٍّ مَشْهُورٌ مِنْ وَجْهَيْن : الْأَوَّلُ أَنَّ النَّتِيجَةَ مَوْقُوفَةٌ عَلَى كُلِّيَّة الْكُبْرَى)، وذلك ضروري ، (وَبِالْعَكْسِ)، أي كلية الكبرى موقوفة على صدق النتيجة ؛ لِأَنَّ الْأَصْغَرَ مِنْ جُمْلَةِ الْأَوْسَطِ، فَدَارَ) ، فما لم يعلم ثبوت الحكم للأصغر لا يعلم ثبوته للأوسط، وثبوت الحكم للأصغر هو النتيجة، فحينئذ يشتمل هذا الشكل على الدور .
(وَحَلُّهُ: أَنَّ التَّفْصِيلَ) هو النتيجة (مَوْقُوفٌ عَلَى الْإِجْمَالِ) الذي هو الكبرى، وعلمه غير موقوف على التفصيل ؛ إذ باختلاف الشيء إجمالا

الثَّانِي: أَنَّ قَوْلَنَا الْخَلَأُ لَيْسَ بِمَوْجُودٍ، وَكُلُّ مَا لَيْسَ بِمَوْجُودٍ لَيْسَ بِمَحْسُوس يُنْتِجُ) «الخلاء ليس بمحسوس»، (مَعَ أَنَّ الصُّغْرَى سَالِبَةٌ، بَلْ كُلَّمَا تَكَرَّرَتِ النِّسْبَةُ السَّلْبِيَّةُ أَنْتَجَتْ .
وَحَلُّهُ كَمَا قِيلَ : إِنَّهَا مُوجَبَةٌ سَالِبَةُ الْمَحْمُولِ)، لا أنها سالبة، حتى يتوجه الإيراد، (يَدُلُّ عَلَى ذَلِكَ جَعْلُ النِّسْبَةِ السَّلْبِيَّةِ مِرْأَةَ لِلْأَفْرَادِ فِي الْكُبْرَى). وتفصيل هذا الجواب أن الكبرى حكم فيها بأن ما ثبت له ليس بموجود ليس

Section on Proof
The third mood produces the particular affirmative and is composed of a particular minor and a negative major. The fourth mood produces the particular negative. 
(That is), meaning producing the four conclusions is among its [the first figure's] characteristics, like the universal affirmative. Because other figures do not produce the universal affirmative. 
And here is a famous doubt from two aspects: The first is that the conclusion depends on the universality of the major premise, and that is necessary, and conversely, i.e., the universality of the major premise depends on the truth of the conclusion, because the minor is part of the middle [term], so there is circularity. For unless the affirmation of the judgment for the minor is known, its affirmation for the middle is not known, and the affirmation of the judgment for the minor is the conclusion. Hence this figure involves circularity. 
(And its resolution: that the detailed [knowledge]) is the conclusion (depends on the summary [knowledge]) which is the major premise, but the knowledge of it does not depend on the detailed [knowledge]; since with the difference of the thing in summary and in detail the designation differs, (and the judgment differs with the difference of the descriptions) — the intuitive and the theoretical. So it is possible that the summary is necessary and the detailed is theoretical, depending on it, (so there is no problem). 
The second: that our saying "The void is not existent" and "Everything not existent is not sensed" produces "The void is not sensed", even though the minor is negative. Rather, whenever the negative relation is repeated, it produces. 
And its resolution is as has been said: it is an affirmative with a negative predicate, not a negative, so that the objection does not apply. (This is indicated by making the negative relation a mirror for the individuals in the major premise). The details of this answer: the major premise rules that whatever has been established as not existent is not sensed. Then the minor rules that not existent is established for the void. Hence it becomes an affirmative with a negative predicate, or a modified [proposition], not a negative. Otherwise there is no subsumption and no production, except accidentally due to its being concomitant with the negative predicate. 
(I say: and you may argue from this that such an affirmative does not require existence of the subject. For if you make its subject non-existent as the minor, and make the negative relation the subject in the major, it produces absolutely. If it were not an affirmative with a negative predicate, then the production from a negative minor would be necessary, so it must be an affirmative with a negative predicate despite the non-existence of the subject. So ponder, for there is a gross error in that; for the most that is required is that the affirmative with a negative predicate produces a true conclusion despite the non-existence of the subject, but its truth does not follow; for a false premise may produce a true conclusion. And God knows best, and He is the Protector.)

The Syllogism

Resolving the Problem of Two Negative Premises
Author resolves an objection about a syllogism with two negative premises by analyzing it as affirmative with negative predicate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to transform an apparent negative premise into an affirmative with a negative predicate in a categorical syllogism to preserve validity.

Core Idea
A proposition with a negative predicate is affirmative, not negative, saving the syllogism from the rule against two negative premises.

In classical Islamic logic, a categorical syllogism cannot have two negative premises. But what happens when a premise seems negative yet the syllogism is valid? The author of this passage tackles exactly that puzzle: the famous 'void syllogism' appears to have two negatives but actually works because one is an affirmative with a negative predicate. Let's learn this subtle but powerful distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the apparent problem
Examine the given syllogism: 'The void is not existent; everything not existent is not sensible; therefore the void is not sensible.' It seems both premises are negative (the major is a universal negative, the minor a particular negative). According to logic rules, two negative premises should yield no conclusion. Yet we intuitively know this conclusion is correct.
The 'void' (al-khala') is a concept in Kalam referring to empty space.
2
Analyze the minor premise's logical form
Read the minor premise 'The void is not existent' more closely. Is it asserting that the void lacks existence, or that non-existence is a characteristic of the void? The Arabic phrasing 'ليس بموجود' can be interpreted as an affirmative proposition with a negative predicate: 'The void is a non-existent thing'. In this reading, the proposition is actually affirmative (it affirms the predicate 'non-existent' of the subject).
This reinterpretation is key: the predicate is negative, but the copula is positive, making it a 'mūjiba sāliba al-maḥmūl' (affirmative with negative predicate).
3
Recognize the structural consequence
With the minor now affirmative, the syllogism becomes: major (universal negative) and minor (affirmative). This is the first figure (Celarent mood: No M is P, All S is M, therefore No S is P). The middle term 'non-existent' (or 'what is not existent') connects the premises. The negative relation in the major is simply a universal negative, while the minor's negative predicate does not count as a negative proposition.
The author notes that the 'negative relation' (al-nisba al-salbiyya) in the major serves as a mirror for individuals, making the inclusion possible.
4
Verify validity and generalize
Now test: All non-existent is not sensible; the void is non-existent; therefore the void is not sensible. This is valid. The general rule: whenever a premise appears negative but the negation is actually part of the predicate, it is an affirmative with negative predicate, not a true negative. This avoids the prohibition of two negative premises.
The author mentions that if not interpreted this way, there would be no inclusion (indiraj) and no production (intaj), except accidentally.

الثَّانِي: أَنَّ قَوْلَنَا الْخَلَأُ لَيْسَ بِمَوْجُودٍ، وَكُلُّ مَا لَيْسَ بِمَوْجُودٍ لَيْسَ بِمَحْسُوس يُنْتِجُ «الخلاء ليس بمحسوس»، مَعَ أَنَّ الصُّغْرَى سَالِبَةٌ، بَلْ كُلَّمَا تَكَرَّرَتِ النِّسْبَةُ السَّلْبِيَّةُ أَنْتَجَتْ. وَحَلُّهُ كَمَا قِيلَ: إِنَّهَا مُوجَبَةٌ سَالِبَةُ الْمَحْمُولِ، لا أنها سالبة، حتى يتوجه الإيراد، يَدُلُّ عَلَى ذَلِكَ جَعْلُ النِّسْبَةِ السَّلْبِيَّةِ مِرْأَةَ لِلْأَفْرَادِ فِي الْكُبْرَى. وَتَفْصِيلُ هَذَا الْجَوَابِ أَنَّ الْكُبْرَى حُكْمٌ فِيهَا بِأَنَّ مَا ثَبَتَ لَهُ لَيْسَ بِمَوْجُودٍ لَيْسَ بِمَحْسُوسٍ، فَإِنْ حُكْمٌ فِي الصُّغْرَى بِأَنَّ لَيْسَ بِمَوْجُودٍ ثَابِتٌ لِلْخَلَاءِ فَحِينَئِذٍ صَارَتْ مُوجَبَةً سَالِبَةَ الْمَحْمُولِ، أَوْ مَعْدُولَةً لَا سَالِبَةً، وَإِلَّا فَلَا انْدِرَاجَ فَلَا إِنْتَاجَ، اللَّهُمَّ إِلَّا بِالْعَرَضِ لِمُلَازَمَتِهَا سَالِبَةَ الْمَحْمُولِ.
The second: that our statement 'The void is not existent, and everything not existent is not sensible' produces 'The void is not sensible', even though the minor premise is negative; rather, whenever the negative relation is repeated, it produces. And its solution, as it is said: it is an affirmative with a negative predicate, not a negative, so that the objection is not directed. That is indicated by making the negative relation a mirror for individuals in the major premise. The detail of this answer is that the major premise judges that whatever is not existent is not sensible. Then the minor premise judges that 'not existent' is affirmed of the void; consequently it becomes an affirmative with a negative predicate, or a modified (معدولة) not a negative. Otherwise there is no inclusion and no production, except accidentally, because of its entailment of the negative predicate.

Commentary
This passage discusses a syllogism that appears to have two negative premises but is actually an affirmative with a negative predicate. The author explains that the so-called 'negative relation' (النسبة السلبية) when used as a predicate in the major premise makes the proposition affirmative with a negative predicate, not a true negative. The key point is that the minor premise 'الخلاء ليس بموجود' is analyzed as 'الخلاء هو ليس بموجود' (the void is a thing not existent), which is affirmative with a negative predicate. Therefore the syllogism is valid because both premises are actually affirmative (one with a negative predicate). The condition of two negative premises not producing is avoided.

Key Takeaway
An apparent negative premise can be an affirmative with a negative predicate, preserving syllogistic validity when the negative relation is treated as a predicate.

Conditions of the Second Figure: Quality Difference and Universal Major
Author states conditions for second figure productivity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a given second figure syllogism satisfies the conditions of differing quality and universal major, and explain why violation leads to sterility.

Core Idea
Second figure requires one affirmative and one negative premise, with a universal major.

In this passage, the author establishes the two key conditions for a valid second figure syllogism: the premises must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative) and the major premise must be universal. He then demonstrates why any deviation—such as both premises being affirmative or both negative—makes the syllogism sterile. Understanding these conditions is crucial for evaluating arguments in Islamic sciences like kalam and fiqh, where such syllogisms are frequently employed.

Guided Path
1
Identify premises and their quality
Locate the two premises of the syllogism. Determine whether each is affirmative (positive) or negative (negative).
In the second figure, the middle term is the predicate in both premises.
2
Check the major premise for universality
The major premise (the one containing the major term) must be universal (kulliyya). Identify the major term (the predicate of the conclusion) and check the quantifier of its premise.
Major term is the subject of the major premise in the second figure?
3
Verify difference in quality
The two premises must differ: one affirmative, one negative. If both are affirmative or both negative, the condition is violated.
4
Understand the reduction proof
The author reduces the second figure to the first by converting one premise and swapping roles. Trace why agreement in quality makes this reduction fail: if both affirmative, the converted premise is particular and cannot be a universal major; if both negative, the minor becomes negative in the first figure, preventing conclusion.
Reduction is a method to verify validity.
5
Apply to an example
Test a concrete syllogism: 'All beneficial innovations are commendable. No sinful act is commendable. Therefore, no sinful act is a beneficial innovation.' Check that premises differ in quality (affirmative and negative) and major premise is universal (negative universal).
This is a valid second figure (Camestres).
