فَصْلٌ فِي الْحُجَّةِ الشَّكْلِ الثَّانِي وَضُرُوبِهِ. وَيُشْتَرَطُ فِي الشَّكْلِ الثَّانِي اخْتِلَافُ الْمُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ فِي الْكَيْفِ، وَكُلِّيَّةُ شَرْطِ إِنْتَاجِ الْكُبْرَى، وَإِلَّا يَلْزَمُ الِاخْتِلَافُ الْمُوجِبُ لِلْعُقْمِ، وَهُوَ صِدْقُ الْمُوجِبَةِ تَارَةً وَالسَّالِبَةِ أُخْرَى. أَمَّا فِي صُورَةِ اتِّفَاقِهِمَا فِي الْكَيْفِ فَلِأَنَّ الشَّيْءَ الْوَاحِدَ رُبَّمَا. وَذَلِكَ لِأَنَّهُ لَا يُثْبَتُ إِنْتَاجُهُ إِلَّا بِرَدِّهِ إِلَى الشَّكْلِ الْأَوَّلِ، وَإِذَا كَانَتْ مُخَالَفَتُهُ لِلْأَوَّلِ إِنَّمَا هُوَ فِي الْكُبْرَى وَجَبَ فِي رَدِّهِ إِلَيْهِ أَنْ تُعْكَسَ إِحْدَى الْمُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ وَتُجْعَلَ كُبْرَى، فَإِنْ كَانَتَا مُوجِبَتَيْنِ فَبَاطِلٌ، أَيْ لَا يُمْكِنُ فِيهِ ذَلِكَ؛ لِأَنَّ عَكْسَ مَا يُعْكَسُ مِنْهُمَا جُزْئِيَّةٌ لَا تَصْلُحُ كُبْرَى لِلْأَوَّلِ، وَإِنْ كَانَتَا سَالِبَتَيْنِ أَمْكَنَ فِيهِ ذَلِكَ، لَكِنْ لَا يُنْتِجُ؛ إِذْ تَصِيرُ الصُّغْرَى سَالِبَةً فِي الْأَوَّلِ، فَلَمْ يَتَلَاقَيَا.
Chapter on the proof: the second figure and its types. It is required in the second figure that the two premises differ in quality, and universality is a condition for the productivity of the major premise. Otherwise, the difference that necessitates sterility follows, which is that it is true at times as affirmative and at others as negative. As for the case of agreement in quality, it is because one thing perhaps... That is because its production is only established by reduction to the first figure, and since its difference from the first is only in the major premise, in reducing it to it, one of the two premises must be converted and made the major. If both are affirmative, it is false, i.e., that is not possible, because what is converted from them is particular and not suitable as a major for the first. If both are negative, it is possible, but it does not produce because the minor becomes negative in the first, so they do not meet.
Source pages p. 504

Commentary
This passage introduces the second figure of the categorical syllogism. The condition for a productive syllogism in the second figure is that the two premises differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative) and the major premise must be universal. The author explains why: the productivity of the second figure is proven by reduction to the first figure. In the second figure, the middle term is the predicate in both premises, so it is not distributed unless one premise is negative. The author shows that if both premises are affirmative, reducing to the first figure fails because the converted premise is particular and cannot serve as a universal major. If both are negative, reduction yields a negative minor in the first figure, which is also non-productive. Thus difference in quality is essential.

C) All innovations are misguidance. All misguidance is bad. Therefore all innovations are bad.

Key Takeaway

The Syllogism

Condition and Productive Moods of the Second Figure
Author explains the condition and four productive moods of the second figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the condition of the second figure and enumerate its four productive moods, and determine the conclusion of a given second-figure syllogism.

Core Idea
The second figure always yields a negative conclusion because premises must differ in quality and the major must be universal.

The second figure of the categorical syllogism has a special condition that sets it apart: the two premises must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative) and the major premise must be universal. This condition guarantees that the conclusion is always negative. In this lesson, you will learn why this condition is necessary and how to recognize the four valid mood combinations that produce necessary conclusions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the middle term's position
In the second figure, the middle term is the predicate of both premises. Locate the term that appears as the predicate in both the minor and major premises.
This differs from the first figure where the middle term is subject of major and predicate of minor.
2
Check the quality condition
The two premises must be different in quality: one affirmative and one negative. If both are affirmative or both negative, no necessary conclusion follows. The text explains why agreement in quality leads to sterility — the middle term may be related to the extremes in multiple ways.
3
Ensure the major premise is universal
The major premise must be universal (all or no). A particular major premise cannot yield a necessary conclusion. The text gives an example: if the major is particular, the middle term may be affirmed of some individuals of the subject and denied of others.
4
Enumerate the four productive moods
There are four valid combinations: (1) universal affirmative minor + universal negative major, (2) universal negative minor + universal affirmative major – both produce a universal negative conclusion. (3) particular affirmative minor + universal negative major, (4) particular negative minor + universal affirmative major – both produce a particular negative conclusion.
These are referred to as 'the two universals' and 'the two differing' in the matn.
5
Test with an Islamic example
Apply the second figure to a fiqh argument: minor 'Every wine is an intoxicant' (affirmative universal), major 'No permissible substance is an intoxicant' (negative universal). Conclusion: 'No wine is permissible.' This is a valid mood of the second figure.
The conclusion is universal negative because both premises are universal and differ in quality.

فصل في الحجة الشكل الثاني وضروبه ( و ) يشترط (في) الشكل (الثاني اختلاف المقدسين في الكيف، وكلية شرط إنتاج الكبرى (٢) ، وإلا يلزم الاختلاف الموجب للعقم، وهو صدق الموجبة تارة والسالبة أخرى، أما في صورة اتفاقهما في الكيف فلأن الشيء الواحد ربما يثبت لشيئين متباينين، فلا يصدق الإيجاب، وربما يثبت لمتساويين، فلا يصدق السلب، وربما يسلب عن متساويين، فلا يصدق السلب، وربما يسلب عن متباينين، فلا يصدق الإيجاب. أما في صورة جزئية الكبرى فلأن شيئا ربما يثبت لأفراد شيء، ويسلب عن بعض أفراد ما هو أعم منه، فلا يصدق السلب، أو يسلب عن مباينه، فيصدق السلب والضروب المنتجة في هذا الشكل أيضا أربعة: الأول موجبة كلية صغرى وسالبة كلية كبرى، والثاني سالبة كلية صغرى، وموجبة كلية كبرى، وهما ينتجان سالبة كلية وإليهما أشار بقوله: (فَيُنْتِجُ الْكُلِّيَّتَانِ سَالِبَةً كُلَّيَّة) الثالث موجبة جزئية صغرى، وسالبة كلية كبرى، الرابع سالبة جزئية صغرى، وموجبة كلية كبرى وهما ينتجان سالبة جزئية، وإليهما أشار بقوله: (وَالْمُخْتَلِفَانِ كَما سَالِبَةٌ جُزْئِيَّةٌ).
Chapter on the Proof: The Second Figure and Its Moods. It is required in the second figure that the two premises differ in quality, and the universality of the major premise is a condition for productivity, otherwise the difference causes sterility, which is the truth of the affirmative sometimes and the negative other times. As for the case of their agreement in quality, because one thing may be affirmed for two distinct things, so the negative is not true, and may be affirmed for two equal things, so the negative is not true, and may be denied for two equal things, so the negative is not true, and may be denied for two distinct things, so the affirmative is not true. As for the case of a particular major premise, because a thing may be affirmed for individuals of something and denied for some individuals of what is more general than it, so the negative is not true, or denied for what is distinct from it, so the negative is true. The productive moods in this figure are also four: First: universal affirmative minor and universal negative major; Second: universal negative minor and universal affirmative major; both produce a universal negative, and he referred to them by his saying: 'The two universals produce a universal negative.' Third: particular affirmative minor and universal negative major; Fourth: particular negative minor and universal affirmative major; both produce a particular negative, and he referred to them by his saying: 'The two differing produce a particular negative.'
Source pages p. 504

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the second figure of the categorical syllogism, explaining its essential condition and listing its four productive moods. The author first states the condition: the two premises must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative). Moreover, the major premise must be universal for the syllogism to yield a valid conclusion. If the premises agree in quality, the conclusion would not follow because the middle term could be affirmed or denied of extremes in various ways. For example, if both premises are affirmative, the middle term could belong to extremes that are distinct, making a negative conclusion false, or to extremes that are equal, making an affirmative conclusion false. Similarly, if both are negative, the same ambiguity arises. The author then addresses the case where the major is particular: it fails to give a universal conclusion and often yields no necessary inference. After establishing the condition, the passage enumerates the four valid moods: (1) universal affirmative minor + universal negative major, (2) universal negative minor + universal affirmative major — both produce a universal negative conclusion; (3) particular affirmative minor + universal negative major, (4) particular negative minor + universal affirmative major — both produce a particular negative conclusion. The key insight is that this figure always yields a negative conclusion, and its purpose is to separate and negate, not to affirm. The commentary quotes part of the matn: 'the two universals produce a universal negative' and 'the two differing produce a particular negative.' The author's explanation follows Imam al-Ghazali's comparison in al-Mustasfa, noting that this figure is called 'al-farq' (the distinction) by jurists because it is used to show separation or difference between two things, as in 'every intoxicant is unlawful, every nabidh is intoxicant, therefore nabidh is unlawful' does not apply here; rather, an example from fiqh: 'every sale with gharar is prohibited, every sale with gharar is a contract, therefore some contracts are not prohibited' is not valid in second figure. Instead, for second figure: 'every alcohol is intoxicant, no wine is alcohol, therefore no wine is intoxicant' is actually first figure? Wait, second figure: middle term is predicate in both premises. Example: 'every intoxicant is unlawful, nothing permissible is intoxicant, therefore nothing permissible is unlawful'? Better example from fiqh: 'every intoxicant is haram, no wine is intoxicant' is invalid. Actually, the example given in the text: 'every black is color, every white is color' does not entail 'every black is white'. So the second figure is useful for refutation: 'every intoxicant causes drunkenness, no water causes drunkenness, therefore no water is intoxicant' — that is second figure affirmative? No, that is actually first figure? I need a correct Islamic example: 'every intoxicant is prohibited, every substance that is not prohibited is not intoxicant'? Easier: 'Every intoxicant is legally impure (najis). No water is legally impure. Therefore, no water is intoxicant.' That is second figure: major premises universal negative, minor universal affirmative, conclusion universal negative. So the second figure is used to disprove a predicate of a subject. The commentary should clarify that the condition of differing quality ensures that one premise is negative, which together with the universality of the major yields a negative conclusion. This passage guards against the misunderstanding that the second figure can ever produce an affirmative conclusion.

الضروب المنتجة

Key Takeaway
The second figure requires premises of differing quality and a universal major, and always yields a negative conclusion—its four moods produce either universal or particular negatives.

The Four Valid Moods of the Second Figure
Enumerates the four productive moods of the second figure and their negative conclusions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the four valid moods of the second figure and produce the correct conclusion for each.

Core Idea
The second figure has exactly four moods, all yielding negative conclusions.

In the second figure, the middle term is the predicate in both premises. This lesson identifies the four premise combinations that yield valid conclusions—two universal and two mixed—as laid out in the passage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure
Recall that the second figure has the middle term as predicate in both premises. This arrangement forces one premise to be negative.
2
List the universal moods
The first two moods use two universal premises: (1) universal affirmative minor + universal negative major; (2) universal negative minor + universal affirmative major. Both produce a universal negative conclusion.
These correspond to the statement: 'the two universal ones produce a universal negative.'
3
List the mixed moods
The third and fourth moods mix a particular with a universal: (3) particular affirmative minor + universal negative major; (4) particular negative minor + universal affirmative major. Both produce a particular negative conclusion.
These correspond to: 'the two mixed ones produce a particular negative.'
4
Verify the negativity
Check that in all four moods, the conclusion is negative—either universal or particular. This is because one premise is negative, so the conclusion must be negative as per the rules.
This is a defining feature of the second figure.

يثبت لشيئين متباينين، فلا يصدق الإيجاب، وربما يثبت لمتساويين، فلا يصدق السلب ، وربما يسلب عن متساويين، فلا يصدق السلب، وربما يسلب عن متباينين ، فلا يصدق الإيجاب. أما في صورة جزئية الكبرى فلأن شيئا ربما يثبت لأفراد شيء، ويسلب عن بعض أفراد ما هو أعم منه ، فلا يصدق السلب، أو يسلب عن مباينه ، فيصدق السلب والضروب المنتجة في هذا الشكل أيضا أربعة: الأول موجبة كلية صغرى وسالبة كلية كبرى، والثاني سالبة كلية صغرى، وموجبة كلية كبرى، وهما ينتجان سالبة كلية وإليهما أشار بقوله: (فَيُنْتِجُ الْكُلِّيَّتَانِ سَالِبَةً كُلَّيَّة) الثالث موجبة جزئية صغرى، وسالبة كلية كبرى، الرابع سالبة جزئية صغرى، وموجبة كلية كبرى وهما ينتجان سالبة جزئية ، وإليهما أشار بقوله: (وَالْمُخْتَلِفَانِ كَما سَالِبَةٌ جُزْئِيَّةٌ (١) . = في المقدمتين في هذا الشكل، وذلك لكون إحدى المقدمتين سالبة في ضوء هذا الاشتراط . والسالبة موجبة أو سالبة تستغرق المحمول . انظر مدخل لدراسة المنطق القديم: ۱۲۹. أن هذا الشكل لا ينتج إلا نتيجة سالبة، سواء كانت جزئية أو كلية، وهذا أمر لم يكن منه بد؛ لأن من شروط هذا الشكل كما رأينا أن تكون إحدى المقدمتين سالبة، الأخرى سالبة. وما أدق تعبير حجة الإسلام في المستصفى (۱) يعني فالنتيجة لا بد أن تكون هي بنفيه يجب (٤٠/١) : كل شيئين - أراد بهما حدي النتيجة ـ أُخبر عن أحدهما بما يخبر عن الآخر أن يكون بينهما انفصال، وهو النفي». فقيمة هذا الشكل أنه إنما يستخدم في الإقصاء والإبعاد لا في الإثبات والبرهنة كما في الشكل الأول، فصح للفقهاء أن يطلق على هذا الشكل بـ«الفرق»، كما أشار إليه الإمام الغزالي نفسه. فعرفت أن كون نتائجه سالبة ما يمكن تبريره ،عقليا، بالإضافة إلى طريقة عقلية أخرى ذكرها العضد في شرح المختصر ،(۲۷) فلا أعرف معنى كلام السعد رَحِمَهُ اللهُ ؛ حين قال في حاشية. شرح المختصر (۱۰۱/۱): «العمدة فيه الاستقراء»، ولو قال مثل السيد في حاشيته عليه (۱۰۱/۱): «السر في هذا الحكم وإن كان معلوما استقراء .. كان أولى .
It applies to two distinct things, so the affirmative is not true, and sometimes it applies to two equivalent things, so the negative is not true, and sometimes it is negated from two equivalent things, so the negative is not true, and sometimes it is negated from two distinct things, so the affirmative is not true. As for the particular major case, because a thing sometimes applies to individuals of a thing and is negated from some individuals of what is more general than it, so the negative is not true, or is negated from what is distinct from it, so the negative is true. The productive moods in this figure are also four: the first is universal affirmative minor and universal negative major; the second is universal negative minor and universal affirmative major; both produce a universal negative conclusion, and the author pointed to them by his saying: 'the two universal ones produce a universal negative.' The third is particular affirmative minor and universal negative major; the fourth is particular negative minor and universal affirmative major; both produce a particular negative conclusion, and the author pointed to them by his saying: 'and the two mixed ones produce a particular negative.' In the two premises in this figure, because one of the two premises is negative in light of this condition. And the negative is either affirmative or negative and it subsumes the predicate. See: An Introduction to the Study of Ancient Logic: 129. That this figure produces only a negative conclusion, whether particular or universal, and this is inevitable; because from the conditions of this figure as we have seen is that one of the two premises is negative, and the other is negative. How precise is the expression of Hujjat al-Islam in al-Mustasfa: (1) meaning: the conclusion must be... by its negation ... (40/1): 'Every two things - he meant by them the two limits of the conclusion - one of them is informed of what the other is informed of, that there is a separation between them, which is negation.' So the value of this figure is that it is used only for exclusion and removal, not for affirmation and demonstration as in the first figure. Thus it was correct for the jurists to call this figure 'al-farq (difference)', as indicated by Imam al-Ghazali himself. So you know that its conclusions being negative can be justified rationally, in addition to another rational method mentioned by al-'Adud in Sharh al-Mukhtasar (27). So I do not know the meaning of the words of al-Sa'd, may Allah have mercy on him, when he said in his Hashiya on Sharh al-Mukhtasar (101/1): 'The mainstay in it is induction.' And if he had said like al-Sayyid in his hashya upon it (101/1): 'The secret in this ruling, though known by induction, it would have been better.'

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the second figure of categorical syllogism, enumerating its four valid moods and explaining why all conclusions are negative. The author begins by discussing the conditions under which terms apply or are negated in general, then focuses on the specific moods. The four moods are: (1) universal affirmative minor + universal negative major → universal negative; (2) universal negative minor + universal affirmative major → universal negative; (3) particular affirmative minor + universal negative major → particular negative; (4) particular negative minor + universal affirmative major → particular negative. The commentator then explains that the second figure necessarily has a negative conclusion because one premise must be negative, and reflects on the logical nature of the figure as used for exclusion (al-farq). He cites al-Ghazali and al-'Adud, and critiques al-Sa'd's reliance on induction.

Why the Second Figure Only Gives Negative Conclusions
Explains why second figure conclusions are negative and its use in differentiation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the logical reason why the second figure yields only negative conclusions and describe its value in juristic reasoning.

Core Idea
The second figure is used for exclusion and distinction, not for affirmation.

The passage explains that the second figure necessarily produces a negative conclusion because one premise must be negative. It then discusses how this figure is used for 'al-farq' (differentiation) in Islamic jurisprudence, as noted by al-Ghazali.

Guided Path
1
Recall the condition
The second figure requires one premise to be negative. This is because the middle term is predicate in both premises; if both premises were affirmative, the middle term would not be distributed properly.
A negative premise distributes its predicate, which is the middle term.
2
Infer the conclusion
With one negative premise, the conclusion must be negative (by syllogistic rules). Thus, all conclusions in the second figure are negative.
3
Identify the logical value
Al-Ghazali called this figure 'al-farq' (difference) because it is used to separate one concept from another, not to prove something positively.
The conclusion is always negative, affirming that one thing is not another.
4
Apply to juristic reasoning
In fiqh, when a jurist wants to distinguish between two categories (e.g., valid vs. invalid contracts), a second-figure syllogism shows that one is not the other.
This aligns with the passage's emphasis on exclusion and removal.

يثبت لشيئين متباينين، فلا يصدق الإيجاب، وربما يثبت لمتساويين، فلا يصدق السلب ، وربما يسلب عن متساويين، فلا يصدق السلب، وربما يسلب عن متباينين ، فلا يصدق الإيجاب. أما في صورة جزئية الكبرى فلأن شيئا ربما يثبت لأفراد شيء، ويسلب عن بعض أفراد ما هو أعم منه ، فلا يصدق السلب، أو يسلب عن مباينه ، فيصدق السلب والضروب المنتجة في هذا الشكل أيضا أربعة: الأول موجبة كلية صغرى وسالبة كلية كبرى، والثاني سالبة كلية صغرى، وموجبة كلية كبرى، وهما ينتجان سالبة كلية وإليهما أشار بقوله: (فَيُنْتِجُ الْكُلِّيَّتَانِ سَالِبَةً كُلَّيَّة) الثالث موجبة جزئية صغرى، وسالبة كلية كبرى، الرابع سالبة جزئية صغرى، وموجبة كلية كبرى وهما ينتجان سالبة جزئية ، وإليهما أشار بقوله: (وَالْمُخْتَلِفَانِ كَما سَالِبَةٌ جُزْئِيَّةٌ (١) . = في المقدمتين في هذا الشكل، وذلك لكون إحدى المقدمتين سالبة في ضوء هذا الاشتراط . والسالبة موجبة أو سالبة تستغرق المحمول . انظر مدخل لدراسة المنطق القديم: ۱۲۹. أن هذا الشكل لا ينتج إلا نتيجة سالبة، سواء كانت جزئية أو كلية، وهذا أمر لم يكن منه بد؛ لأن من شروط هذا الشكل كما رأينا أن تكون إحدى المقدمتين سالبة، الأخرى سالبة. وما أدق تعبير حجة الإسلام في المستصفى (۱) يعني فالنتيجة لا بد أن تكون هي بنفيه يجب (٤٠/١) : كل شيئين - أراد بهما حدي النتيجة ـ أُخبر عن أحدهما بما يخبر عن الآخر أن يكون بينهما انفصال، وهو النفي». فقيمة هذا الشكل أنه إنما يستخدم في الإقصاء والإبعاد لا في الإثبات والبرهنة كما في الشكل الأول، فصح للفقهاء أن يطلق على هذا الشكل بـ«الفرق»، كما أشار إليه الإمام الغزالي نفسه. فعرفت أن كون نتائجه سالبة ما يمكن تبريره ،عقليا، بالإضافة إلى طريقة عقلية أخرى ذكرها العضد في شرح المختصر ،(۲۷) فلا أعرف معنى كلام السعد رَحِمَهُ اللهُ ؛ حين قال في حاشية. شرح المختصر (۱۰۱/۱): «العمدة فيه الاستقراء»، ولو قال مثل السيد في حاشيته عليه (۱۰۱/۱): «السر في هذا الحكم وإن كان معلوما استقراء .. كان أولى .
It applies to two distinct things, so the affirmative is not true, and sometimes it applies to two equivalent things, so the negative is not true, and sometimes it is negated from two equivalent things, so the negative is not true, and sometimes it is negated from two distinct things, so the affirmative is not true. As for the particular major case, because a thing sometimes applies to individuals of a thing and is negated from some individuals of what is more general than it, so the negative is not true, or is negated from what is distinct from it, so the negative is true. The productive moods in this figure are also four: the first is universal affirmative minor and universal negative major; the second is universal negative minor and universal affirmative major; both produce a universal negative conclusion, and the author pointed to them by his saying: 'the two universal ones produce a universal negative.' The third is particular affirmative minor and universal negative major; the fourth is particular negative minor and universal affirmative major; both produce a particular negative conclusion, and the author pointed to them by his saying: 'and the two mixed ones produce a particular negative.' In the two premises in this figure, because one of the two premises is negative in light of this condition. And the negative is either affirmative or negative and it subsumes the predicate. See: An Introduction to the Study of Ancient Logic: 129. That this figure produces only a negative conclusion, whether particular or universal, and this is inevitable; because from the conditions of this figure as we have seen is that one of the two premises is negative, and the other is negative. How precise is the expression of Hujjat al-Islam in al-Mustasfa: (1) meaning: the conclusion must be... by its negation ... (40/1): 'Every two things - he meant by them the two limits of the conclusion - one of them is informed of what the other is informed of, that there is a separation between them, which is negation.' So the value of this figure is that it is used only for exclusion and removal, not for affirmation and demonstration as in the first figure. Thus it was correct for the jurists to call this figure 'al-farq (difference)', as indicated by Imam al-Ghazali himself. So you know that its conclusions being negative can be justified rationally, in addition to another rational method mentioned by al-'Adud in Sharh al-Mukhtasar (27). So I do not know the meaning of the words of al-Sa'd, may Allah have mercy on him, when he said in his Hashiya on Sharh al-Mukhtasar (101/1): 'The mainstay in it is induction.' And if he had said like al-Sayyid in his hashya upon it (101/1): 'The secret in this ruling, though known by induction, it would have been better.'
Source pages p. 505

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the second figure of categorical syllogism, enumerating its four valid moods and explaining why all conclusions are negative. The author begins by discussing the conditions under which terms apply or are negated in general, then focuses on the specific moods. The four moods are: (1) universal affirmative minor + universal negative major → universal negative; (2) universal negative minor + universal affirmative major → universal negative; (3) particular affirmative minor + universal negative major → particular negative; (4) particular negative minor + universal affirmative major → particular negative. The commentator then explains that the second figure necessarily has a negative conclusion because one premise must be negative, and reflects on the logical nature of the figure as used for exclusion (al-farq). He cites al-Ghazali and al-'Adud, and critiques al-Sa'd's reliance on induction.

الفرق

Reduction by Contradiction (Radd bi-l-Khalf)
Author explains reduction by contradiction for syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply reduction by contradiction to prove a categorical conclusion by showing its negation contradicts the premises.

Core Idea
Reduction by contradiction proves a conclusion by refuting its opposite.

This passage introduces a powerful method: to prove a conclusion, assume its opposite and derive a contradiction with the given premises. This is a cornerstone of logical proof in the Islamic tradition, used extensively in kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the desired conclusion
Determine what you need to prove from the given premises.
In the example, the conclusion is 'No J is A' from premises 'Every J is B' and 'No A is B'.
2
State the contradictory of the conclusion
Assume the opposite of the conclusion. This becomes a temporary premise.
The contradictory of 'No J is A' is 'Some J is A'.
3
Combine with the original major premise
Use the assumed contradictory as a minor premise with the original major (the universal premise) to form a new syllogism.
Combine 'Some J is A' with 'No A is B' to produce 'Some J is not B'.
4
Check for contradiction with the minor premise
If the new conclusion contradicts one of the original premises, then the assumption is false, so the original conclusion must be true.
'Some J is not B' contradicts 'Every J is B', so the assumption is invalid.
5
Affirm the original conclusion
Since the contradictory leads to a contradiction, the original conclusion is necessarily true.
Thus 'No J is A' is proven.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الحجة
طرق رد بالخلف)، وهو جعل نقيض النتيجة لإيجابه صغرى، وكبرى القياس الشكل الثاني إلى الأول لكليتها كبرى ; حتى ينتج ما تنافي الصغرى، فيبطل، ويصدق النتيجة، مثلا للبرهنة على «كل (ج) (ب)» و «لا شيء من (أ) (ب)» ، فلو لم يصدق لا شيء من (ج) (أ) لصدق «بعض (ج) (أ))، وهي مع الكبرى تنتج بعض (ج) ليس (ب)»، وقد كان كل (ج) (ب) ، فإذن صدق النتيجة واجب. أو بعكس الكبرى) ; ليرتد إلى الشكل الأول; لينتج النتيجة المطلوبة. وهذا إنما يجري في الأول والثالث ، وأما في الثاني والرابع فالكبرى لإيجابها إنما تنعكس جزئية، وهي غير صالحة لكبروية الشكل الأول، والصغرى أيضا سالبة ، لا يصلح الصغروية فيه. (أو) بعكس (الصغرى); ليرتد إلى الشكل الرابع ، (الترتيب) ; ليرتد إلى الشكل الأول، فينتج نتيجة ، ١٠٠ عكس لتحصيل النتيجة المطلوبة. وهذا إنما يجري في الثاني فقط، وأما في الأول والثالث فالكبرى سالبة، لا تصلح لصغروية الشكل الأول، وأيضا عكس الصغرى جزئية، لا تصلح لكبرويته، وأما في الرابع فالصغرى لكونها سالبة جزئية غير قابلة للانعكاس ولو فرض انعكاسها كما في الخاصتين فإنما تنعكس جزئية غير صالحة لكبرويته . ثم اعلم : أن الشيخ أجرى في الضرب الرابع الافتراض بأن فرض بعض (ج) (د) في بعض (ج) ليس (ب)، وكل (أ) (ب)، فلا شيء من (د) (ب)، وكل (أ) (ب)، فلا شيء من (د) (أ) من ثاني هذا الشكل. وأيضا بعض (ج) (د)، ولا شيء من (د) (أ) ، ينتج من الشكل الأول النتيجة المطلوبة. وهذا تصريح منه بجريان الافتراض في السالبة، ولزم منه استدعاء صحته
Section on Proof: Methods: Reduction by contradiction, which is taking the contradictory of the conclusion for its affirmation as minor premise, and the major of the syllogism of the second figure to the first, due to its universality as major, until it produces what contradicts the minor, so it is invalidated, and the conclusion is true. For example, to prove 'Every J is B' and 'No A is B', if 'No J is A' were not true, then 'Some J is A' would be true; it together with the major produces 'Some J is not B', while every J is B, so the conclusion is necessarily true. Or by conversion of the major, to return to the first figure to produce the desired conclusion. This applies only in the first and third figures. As for the second and fourth, the major, because of its affirmation, converts only particularly, and it is not suitable as a major in the first figure; also the minor is negative, not suitable as a minor therein. Or by conversion of the minor, to return to the fourth figure, or by rearrangement, to return to the first figure, producing a conclusion whose conversion yields the desired conclusion. This applies only in the second figure. As for the first and third, the major is negative, not suitable as a minor in the first figure; also the conversion of the minor is particular, not suitable as its major. As for the fourth, the minor, because it is particular negative, is not convertible; even if its conversion were supposed, as in the special cases, it converts only particularly, not suitable as its major. Then know: that the Shaykh (i.e., the author) applied in the fourth mood hypothetical supposition by supposing some J is D from 'some J is not B' and 'every A is B', so no D is B, and every A is B, so no D is A from the second of this figure. Also, some J is D and no D is A produce from the first figure the desired conclusion. This is his explicit statement on the application of hypothetical supposition to the negative, and it necessitates seeking its correctness.
Source pages p. 506

Commentary
This passage is a section on proof (al-hujja) explaining methods to reduce syllogisms from other figures to the first figure. The first method is reduction by contradiction (khalf): take the contradictory of the desired conclusion along with the original major to produce a contradiction with the minor, thereby proving the conclusion. An example is given using schematic variables: to prove 'Every J is B' and 'No A is B', assume 'No J is A' is false, so 'Some J is A' is true, which with the major yields 'Some J is not B', contradicting the minor, so the conclusion holds. The second method is conversion of the major (aks al-kubra): this works in figures 1 and 3 but not 2 and 4 due to quantity constraints. The third method is conversion of the minor (aks al-sughra): this works only in figure 2, with limitations in other figures. The last part discusses hypothetical supposition (iftirad) used in the fourth mood of figure 2: an extra term D is introduced to mediate a reduction to figure 1. The Shaykh's discussion shows that hypothetical supposition applies even to negative propositions, requiring careful justification.

Key Terms
رد بالخلف
نقيض
نتيجة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the reduction by contradiction example from the passage, what is the contradictory of the desired conclusion 'No J is A'?
A) Some J is A
B) Every J is A
C) No A is J
D) Some J is not A

Key Takeaway
Reduction by contradiction proves a conclusion by showing its negation leads to a contradiction with the premises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Apply reduction by contradiction to validate the following: Premises: 'Every obligatory act (wajib) is commanded.' and 'No commanded act is sinful (ma'siya).' Prove that 'No obligatory act is sinful.'

Reduction by Conversion of Major and Minor
Author explains conversion of major/minor for reduction to first figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to use conversion of the major or minor premise to reduce a syllogism to the first figure and identify its limitations based on figure and proposition type.

Core Idea
Conversion of premises can reduce syllogisms to the first figure, but only under strict conditions.

The second method discussed is converting one premise to bring the syllogism into the first figure. However, the passage stresses that conversion is not always applicable due to the quantity and quality of the premises. Understanding these limitations is crucial for correctly applying reduction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure of the original syllogism
Determine which figure the syllogism belongs to based on the position of the middle term.
For example, second figure: middle term is predicate in both premises.
2
Check if conversion of the major is allowed
Conversion of the major works only if the major is universal affirmative (A). It converts to particular affirmative (I), which can serve as a major in the first figure.
Passage says: conversion of major applies in figures 1 and 3, not in 2 and 4.
3
Check if conversion of the minor is allowed
Conversion of the minor works only in the second figure because the minor there is particular? Actually, passage says: conversion of minor applies only in the second figure; in other figures the minor is negative or converts to particular unintelligible.
In second figure, the minor is affirmative? Need to match conditions.
4
Perform the conversion and reconstruct the syllogism
Convert the chosen premise and rearrange to form a first-figure syllogism. Ensure the new premises yield the desired conclusion directly or after obversion.
After conversion, the conclusion may need to be converted back to the original form.
5
Verify validity and applicability
Ensure the converted syllogism is valid in the first figure and that the original conclusion follows.
If conversion is not allowed, other methods like hypothetical supposition are needed.

Hypothetical Supposition (Iftirad) for the Fourth Mood
Author explains hypothetical supposition used in the fourth mood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to use hypothetical supposition to reduce a syllogism with a particular negative premise to the first figure by introducing a new term.

Core Idea
When conversion fails, introduce a hypothetical term to mediate the reduction.

The most subtle method is hypothetical supposition, where we suppose an additional term (D) that serves as a bridge. This method is used for the fourth mood of the second figure, where other reductions are impossible. The passage gives an example with 'Some C is not B' and 'Every A is B' to prove 'Some C is not A'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problematic mood
Recognize a syllogism that cannot be reduced by contradiction or conversion, e.g., the fourth mood of the second figure with a particular negative premise.
Example: 'Some C is not B' and 'Every A is B'. Want to prove 'Some C is not A'.
2
Introduce a hypothetical term D
Assume there exists a term D such that 'Some C is D' holds, derived from the particular negative 'Some C is not B' (the C that is not B is D).
D is like a witness: it is a C that is not B.
3
Derive relationships using original premises
From 'No D is B' (since D is not B) and 'Every A is B', infer 'No D is A' via the second figure.
This uses the fact that if every A is B and no D is B, then no D is A.
4
Combine with the hypothetical minor
Now use 'Some C is D' (hypothetical) and 'No D is A' to form a first-figure syllogism.
That yields 'Some C is not A', which is the desired conclusion.
5
Confirm the reduction
The original conclusion is now proven using a first-figure syllogism with an intermediate term D.
This method is valid because D is only hypothetical; the truth of the conclusion does not depend on the reality of D.

فصل في الحجة
طرق رد بالخلف)، وهو جعل نقيض النتيجة لإيجابه صغرى، وكبرى القياس الشكل الثاني إلى الأول لكليتها كبرى ; حتى ينتج ما تنافي الصغرى، فيبطل، ويصدق النتيجة، مثلا للبرهنة على «كل (ج) (ب)» و «لا شيء من (أ) (ب)» ، فلو لم يصدق لا شيء من (ج) (أ) لصدق «بعض (ج) (أ))، وهي مع الكبرى تنتج بعض (ج) ليس (ب)»، وقد كان كل (ج) (ب) ، فإذن صدق النتيجة واجب. أو بعكس الكبرى) ; ليرتد إلى الشكل الأول; لينتج النتيجة المطلوبة. وهذا إنما يجري في الأول والثالث ، وأما في الثاني والرابع فالكبرى لإيجابها إنما تنعكس جزئية، وهي غير صالحة لكبروية الشكل الأول، والصغرى أيضا سالبة ، لا يصلح الصغروية فيه. (أو) بعكس (الصغرى); ليرتد إلى الشكل الرابع ، (الترتيب) ; ليرتد إلى الشكل الأول، فينتج نتيجة ، ١٠٠ عكس لتحصيل النتيجة المطلوبة. وهذا إنما يجري في الثاني فقط، وأما في الأول والثالث فالكبرى سالبة، لا تصلح لصغروية الشكل الأول، وأيضا عكس الصغرى جزئية، لا تصلح لكبرويته، وأما في الرابع فالصغرى لكونها سالبة جزئية غير قابلة للانعكاس ولو فرض انعكاسها كما في الخاصتين فإنما تنعكس جزئية غير صالحة لكبرويته . ثم اعلم : أن الشيخ أجرى في الضرب الرابع الافتراض بأن فرض بعض (ج) (د) في بعض (ج) ليس (ب)، وكل (أ) (ب)، فلا شيء من (د) (ب)، وكل (أ) (ب)، فلا شيء من (د) (أ) من ثاني هذا الشكل. وأيضا بعض (ج) (د)، ولا شيء من (د) (أ) ، ينتج من الشكل الأول النتيجة المطلوبة. وهذا تصريح منه بجريان الافتراض في السالبة، ولزم منه استدعاء صحته
Section on Proof: Methods: Reduction by contradiction, which is taking the contradictory of the conclusion for its affirmation as minor premise, and the major of the syllogism of the second figure to the first, due to its universality as major, until it produces what contradicts the minor, so it is invalidated, and the conclusion is true. For example, to prove 'Every J is B' and 'No A is B', if 'No J is A' were not true, then 'Some J is A' would be true; it together with the major produces 'Some J is not B', while every J is B, so the conclusion is necessarily true. Or by conversion of the major, to return to the first figure to produce the desired conclusion. This applies only in the first and third figures. As for the second and fourth, the major, because of its affirmation, converts only particularly, and it is not suitable as a major in the first figure; also the minor is negative, not suitable as a minor therein. Or by conversion of the minor, to return to the fourth figure, or by rearrangement, to return to the first figure, producing a conclusion whose conversion yields the desired conclusion. This applies only in the second figure. As for the first and third, the major is negative, not suitable as a minor in the first figure; also the conversion of the minor is particular, not suitable as its major. As for the fourth, the minor, because it is particular negative, is not convertible; even if its conversion were supposed, as in the special cases, it converts only particularly, not suitable as its major. Then know: that the Shaykh (i.e., the author) applied in the fourth mood hypothetical supposition by supposing some J is D from 'some J is not B' and 'every A is B', so no D is B, and every A is B, so no D is A from the second of this figure. Also, some J is D and no D is A produce from the first figure the desired conclusion. This is his explicit statement on the application of hypothetical supposition to the negative, and it necessitates seeking its correctness.
Source pages p. 506

Commentary
This passage is a section on proof (al-hujja) explaining methods to reduce syllogisms from other figures to the first figure. The first method is reduction by contradiction (khalf): take the contradictory of the desired conclusion along with the original major to produce a contradiction with the minor, thereby proving the conclusion. An example is given using schematic variables: to prove 'Every J is B' and 'No A is B', assume 'No J is A' is false, so 'Some J is A' is true, which with the major yields 'Some J is not B', contradicting the minor, so the conclusion holds. The second method is conversion of the major (aks al-kubra): this works in figures 1 and 3 but not 2 and 4 due to quantity constraints. The third method is conversion of the minor (aks al-sughra): this works only in figure 2, with limitations in other figures. The last part discusses hypothetical supposition (iftirad) used in the fourth mood of figure 2: an extra term D is introduced to mediate a reduction to figure 1. The Shaykh's discussion shows that hypothetical supposition applies even to negative propositions, requiring careful justification.

ضرب الرابع
شكل ثاني

Key Takeaway
Hypothetical supposition introduces a new term to bridge the inference when direct reduction is impossible.

Proof by Reductio ad Absurdum in Categorical Syllogisms
Author defines reductio ad absurdum with a categorical example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply reductio ad absurdum to prove a categorical conclusion in a syllogism.

Core Idea
Reductio proves a conclusion by showing its negation leads to contradiction.

This passage introduces an indirect proof method used in logic. You will learn how to assume the opposite of a conclusion and derive a contradiction with the premises. This method strengthens arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion to prove
Start with the original syllogism and clearly state the conclusion that needs to be proven. In the example, the conclusion is 'No C is A'.
2
Assume the contradictory of the conclusion
Take the contradictory (naqīḍ) of the desired conclusion as a new minor premise. For 'No C is A', the contradictory is 'Some C is A'.
The contradictory must be exactly the opposite truth value.
3
Combine with the original major premise in the first figure
Use the original major premise ('No A is B') together with the assumed minor ('Some C is A') to form a first-figure syllogism. This yields a new conclusion: 'Some C is not B'.
The first figure is chosen to ensure a valid inference.
4
Observe the contradiction with the original minor premise
Compare the newly derived conclusion ('Some C is not B') with the original minor premise ('Every C is B'). They are contradictory, so the assumption that led to them must be false.
5
Conclude the original conclusion is true
Since the assumption of the contradictory is false, the original conclusion ('No C is A') must be true.
This establishes the conclusion by reductio.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الحجة طرق رد بالخلف)، وهو جعل نقيض النتيجة لإيجابه صغرى، وكبرى القياس الشكل الثاني إلى الأول لكليتها كبرى ؛ حتى ينتج ما تنافي الصغرى، فيبطل، ويصدق النتيجة، مثلا للبرهنة على «كل (ج) (ب)» و «لا شيء من (أ) (ب)» ، فلو لم يصدق لا شيء من (ج) (أ) لصدق «بعض (ج) (أ))، وهي مع الكبرى تنتج بعض (ج) ليس (ب)»، وقد كان كل (ج) (ب) ، فإذن صدق النتيجة واجب.
Chapter on the proof. Methods: Reductio ad absurdum, which is making the contradictory of the conclusion to make it a minor premise, and the major premise of the syllogism of the second figure to the first because of its universality is a major; so that it yields what contradicts the minor premise, then it becomes false, and the conclusion is true. For example, to prove 'Every C is B' and 'No A is B', so if 'No C is A' were not true, then 'Some C is A' would be true, and it together with the major yields 'Some C is not B', whereas it was given that every C is B. Therefore the conclusion is necessarily true.
Source pages p. 506

Commentary
This passage defines the method of proof known as reductio ad absurdum (الرد بالخلف). The author begins by stating that this method involves taking the contradictory opposite (نقيض) of the desired conclusion and using it as a minor premise, while the major premise of the original syllogism is taken from the second figure and converted to the first figure, provided it is universal. The goal is to produce a conclusion that contradicts the original minor premise, thereby invalidating it and establishing the truth of the original conclusion. An example is given: to prove 'Every C is B' and 'No A is B', the reductio works by assuming the opposite of 'No C is A', i.e., 'Some C is A'. Combining this with the major 'No A is B' in the first figure yields 'Some C is not B', which contradicts the original minor 'Every C is B'. Hence the original conclusion 'No C is A' must be true. All examples are from categorical logic using terms from the Islamic sciences, such as the relations between categories.

كبرى

Key Takeaway
Reductio ad absurdum assumes the contradictory of the conclusion to derive a contradiction with a premise, thereby proving the conclusion.

When Conversion of Major or Minor Works: Conditions in Each Figure
Author explains conditions for using conversion of major, conversion of minor, and arrangement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine which reduction method (conversion of major, conversion of minor, or arrangement) is applicable to a given syllogistic figure and justify your choice using the logical conditions.

Core Idea
Each figure permits only one premise conversion to reach the first figure.

The first figure is the ideal form for categorical syllogisms. To bring syllogisms from other figures into the first figure, logicians developed three methods: conversion of the major premise, conversion of the minor premise, and reordering of premises (tartīb). However, these methods are not universally applicable; each figure imposes restrictions based on the quantity and quality of the premises. In this lesson, you will learn exactly when each method can be used.

Guided Path
1
Identify the method
The passage discusses three methods: conversion of major, conversion of minor, and arrangement. Each serves a different purpose: conversion of major reduces to first figure, conversion of minor reduces to fourth figure, and arrangement reduces to first figure.
2
Check conversion of major
Conversion of major works only in first and third figures because the major premise in these figures is affirmative. In second and fourth figures, the major is also affirmative but converts to a particular, which cannot serve as the universal major required in the first figure.
Also the minor in second and fourth is negative, unsuitable as a minor in first figure.
3
Check conversion of minor
Conversion of minor reduces to the fourth figure, but it only works in the second figure. In first and third figures, the major is negative, which cannot become a minor, and the converse of the minor is particular, not universal. In fourth figure, the minor is negative particular, which cannot convert.
4
Check arrangement
Arrangement (reordering premises) reduces to first figure and works only in the second figure. In first and third, the major is negative and the minor's converse is particular; in fourth, the minor is not convertible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(أو) بعكس (الصغرى)؛ ليرتد إلى الشكل الرابع ، (الترتيب)؛ ليرتد إلى الشكل الأول، فينتج نتيجة ، ١٠٠ عكس لتحصيل النتيجة المطلوبة. وهذا إنما يجري في الثاني فقط، وأما في الأول والثالث فالكبرى سالبة، لا تصلح لصغروية الشكل الأول، وأيضا عكس الصغرى جزئية، لا تصلح لكبرويته، وأما في الرابع فالصغرى لكونها سالبة جزئية غير قابلة للانعكاس ولو فرض انعكاسها كما في الخاصتين فإنما تنعكس جزئية غير صالحة لكبرويته . ثم اعلم : أن الشيخ أجرى في الضرب الرابع الافتراض بأن فرض بعض (ج) (د) في بعض (ج) ليس (ب)، وكل (أ) (ب)، فلا شيء من (د) (ب)، وكل (أ) (ب)، فلا شيء من (د) (أ) من ثاني هذا الشكل. وأيضا بعض (ج) (د)، ولا شيء من (د) (أ) ، ينتج من الشكل الأول النتيجة المطلوبة. وهذا تصريح منه بجريان الافتراض في السالبة، ولزم منه استدعاء صحته. شرح بحر العلوم على سلم العلوم عقد الوضع وجود الموصوف . واعتذر عنه المتأخرون بالتخصيص بما إذا كان الصغرى مركبة، وهذا الدليل لا يجري في الضرب الثاني ، وإلا دار.
Or by conversion of the major: to reduce it to the first figure, so that it yields the desired conclusion. This only applies in the first and third figures. As for the second and fourth, the major, because of its affirmation, only converts as a particular, and that is not suitable as a major in the first figure. Also the minor is negative, not suitable as a minor in it. Or by conversion of the minor: to reduce it to the fourth figure; or by arrangement (tartīb): to reduce to the first figure, then it produces a conclusion, then its converse to obtain the desired conclusion. That only applies in the second figure. As for the first and third, the major is negative, not suitable as a minor in the first figure, and also the converse of the minor is particular, not suitable as a major in it. As for the fourth, the minor, being negative and particular, is not subject to conversion. Even if its conversion were assumed as in the two special cases, it would only convert as a particular, not suitable as a major in it. Then know that the Master (al-Shaykh) executed in the fourth mood the hypothesis by assuming some C is D in some C is not B, and every A is B, so no D is B, and every A is B, so no D is A from the second of this figure. And also some C is D, and no D is A, yields from the first figure the desired conclusion. This is a clear statement from him that the hypothesis applies in the negative, and it requires its correctness. Commentary of Bahr al-Ulum on Sullam al-Ulum: the act of positing the existence of the subject. And the later scholars apologized by specifying it to the case where the minor is composite, and this proof does not apply in the second mood, otherwise it would be circular.

Commentary
This passage discusses various methods to reduce syllogisms to the first figure using conversion (عكس) and hypothesis (افتراض). The author first explains the conversion of the major premise. This works only in the first and third figures because in the second and fourth, the major is affirmative and converts to a particular, which cannot serve as a major in the first figure. Similarly, the minor is negative, unsuitable as a minor. Then conversion of the minor is discussed, which reduces to the fourth figure, and arrangement (ترتيب) reduces to the first figure. These apply only in the second figure. The author then details the conditions for each figure. Next, the hypothesis method (افتراض) is introduced, attributed to 'the Master' (الشيخ, likely Avicenna). The example involves assuming a term D to resolve the syllogism. The passage includes a note from the commentator (Bahr Ulum) about the act of positing (عقد الوضع) and the existence of the subject. The later scholars specified that hypothesis applies only when the minor is composite, and it does not work in the second mood of the third figure, lest it leads to circularity. All examples are rooted in categorical logic.

The Hypothesis Method (Iftirad) by Ibn Sina
Author explains Ibn Sina's hypothesis method with a concrete example using terms A, B, C, D.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Sina's hypothesis method for reducing a fourth-mood syllogism to the first figure and explain the role of the assumed term D.

Core Idea
Hypothesis introduces a dummy term to bridge premises when direct conversion fails.

When conversion or arrangement cannot reduce a syllogism to the first figure, Ibn Sina employed the hypothesis method (iftirāḍ). This involves assuming a new term D that stands in a specific relation to the existing terms, allowing the deduction of needed intermediate conclusions. In this lesson, you will see the precise steps of his example and understand how the hypothesis circumvents conversion limitations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original syllogism
The original syllogism is in the fourth mood of a figure (likely the fourth figure). It has a minor: some C is not B, and a major: all A is B. The desired conclusion is some C is not A.
The exact mood is not specified, but it involves a negative premise.
2
Introduce the hypothesis
Assume some C is D, where D is a new term. This assumption is the 'hypothesis' that will help link the premises.
D is a dummy term, not present in original syllogism.
3
First deduction
From the assumption (some C is D) and the original minor (some C is not B), we cannot directly combine. But the passage shows: using all A is B and the assumption, we get no D is B, then from all A is B and no D is B, we get no D is A via the second figure.
The deduction uses the second figure of the same shape.
4
Second deduction
Now we have some C is D (from assumption) and no D is A (from above). These two premises form a first-figure syllogism (some C is D, no D is A) yielding some C is not A, which is the desired conclusion.
This shows the hypothesis method successfully reduces to the first figure.
5
Note on conditions
The commentator notes that later scholars restricted the hypothesis method to cases where the minor premise is composite. Also, it fails in the second mood of the fourth figure to avoid circularity.

Source Text

Conditions and Demonstrative Proofs of the Third Figure
Author discusses posterior and demonstrative proofs and states the condition of the minor premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the innī and limī proofs for the third figure, identify the condition that the minor premise must be affirmative and universal, and apply the hypothesis method using variables.

Core Idea
The third figure's validity is demonstrated through a limī proof and requires the minor to be affirmative and universal.

This passage deepens our understanding of the third figure by distinguishing posterior (innī) and demonstrative (limī) proofs. The limī proof shows the essential necessity of the conclusion, while the condition for the minor ensures reducibility to the first figure. The author also provides a hypothesis method that avoids figure conversion.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish innī and limī proofs
Note that innī proofs show that the conclusion follows, while limī proofs show why it follows essentially. Find where the author marks this distinction.
The author states these proofs are all innī, then introduces the limī proof.
2
Understand the limī proof
The author explains that the third figure reduces to the middle being affirmed of the minor and denied of the major, so the minor and major must be mutually exclusive; otherwise, a contradiction arises.
This shows the necessity of the conclusion essentially.
3
Analyze the objection and response
Avicenna objected that 'mutual exclusiveness' is ambiguous. The author clarifies it means negation from the other, which is precisely the claim.
This resolves the ambiguity and defends the limī proof.
4
Construct the hypothesis method
Using variables: let D be the essence from which B is negated. Then D is different from that to which B is affirmed. So some C is not A. This works without reference to a figure.
The method uses 'act of positing' to define D.
5
Apply the condition for the third figure
The passage states that in the third figure, the minor premise must be affirmative and universal. This is because reduction to the first figure requires converting one premise to a universal affirmative minor.
Check that the minor is affirmative and universal for validity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن هذه الدلائل كلها إنيات، إنما يظهر منها صدق النتيجة مع المقدمتين ، ولا يظهر منها أن بأي سبب سبب لازمناها. والبرهان اللمي أن حاصل هذا الشكل يرجع إلى ثبوت الأوسط للأصغر ، ونقيضه للأكبر كلا، وبالعكس، فلا بد أن لا يكون أفراد الأصغر من أفراد الأكبر ، وإلا يلزم اجتماع متنافيين فقد ظهر منه أن مقدمات هذا الشكل مستلزمة للنتيجة بالذات. ولعل ما نقل من المعلم الأول: أن شيئا إذا أثبت لشيء وسلب عن آخر، فالمثبت له والمسلوب عنه متباينان فيلزم سلب أحدهما عن الآخر محمول على ما قلنا، وسقط اعتراض الشيخ عليه بأن التباين يدل على معان كثيرة، والمراد ههنا سلب عن الآخر، وهو بعينه الدعوى. ثم يمكن تقرير الافتراض من غير رجوع إلى شكل أصلا ؛ فإن الذات التي سلب عنها (ب) نسميها (د)، والتي ثبت لها (ب) غيرها، وإلا لزم اجتماع المتنافييْنِ ، فقد وجب سلب (أ) عنه، سلب (أ) عنه، و(د) هو الذي كان (ج) بعقْدِ الوضع، سواء كان موجودا أو معدوما، ويصح انعقاد الموجبة أو لا، فبعض (ج) ليس (أ)، وهو المطلوب. وهذا يجري في كل ضرب، وغير مختص بضرب دون ضرب ، والعلم التام عند واهب العلوم. (و) يشترط (في) الشكل (الثَّالِثِ إِيجَابُ الصُّغْرَى(۱) مَعَ كلتة وضروبه (۱) وذلك لأنه إنما يرتد إلى الأول بعكس إحداهما وجعلها صغرى ؛ لموافقته له في الكبرى، فالتي تعكسها إما الصغرى أو الكبرى، فإن كانت الصغرى فإذا عكستها كانت الصغرى= المنتجة
Then, all these proofs are posterior (inniyāt), they only show the truth of the conclusion with the premises, but they do not show by what reason we are necessitated by them. The demonstrative proof (burhān limī) is that the outcome of this figure reduces to the subsistence of the middle term for the minor, and its contradictory for the major both, and vice versa. So it is necessary that the individuals of the minor are not among the individuals of the major, otherwise contradictory would combine. It has thus become clear that the premises of this figure necessitate the conclusion essentially. Perhaps what has been transmitted from the First Teacher (al-Muʿallim al-Awwal) - that if something is affirmed of something and negated of another, then that which is affirmed of and that which is negated from are mutually exclusive, so it is necessary to negate one from the other - is to be understood as what we have said. And the objection of the Master (al-Shaykh) that mutual exclusiveness indicates many meanings falls away; what is meant here is negation from the other, which is precisely the claim. Then the hypothesis can be established without any reference to a figure, for the essence from which B is negated we call D, and that to which B is affirmed is other than it, otherwise the two contradictories would combine. So it is necessary to negate A from it. D is that which was C by the act of positing, whether existent or non-existent, and whether the affirmative can be formed or not. So some C is not A, which is the desired conclusion. This applies to every mood and is not specific to any mood. And complete knowledge is from the Bestower of knowledge. (And) it is required (in) the third figure that the minor is affirmative with universality and its moods. That is because it only reduces to the first by converting one of them and making it a minor, due to its agreement with it in the major. So whichever you convert, either the minor or the major, if you convert the minor, then when you convert it, the minor becomes (the productive minor?)
Source pages p. 507

Commentary
This passage discusses the nature of the proofs previously given for the third figure. The author distinguishes between 'innī' (posterior) proofs, which show that the conclusion follows but not why, and 'limī' (demonstrative) proof, which explains the essential reason. The limī proof for the third figure is that the middle term is affirmed of the minor and denied of the major, so the minor and major must be mutually exclusive, else a contradiction would arise. The author then references the First Teacher (Aristotle) and responds to an objection from al-Shaykh (Avicenna) about the ambiguity of 'mutual exclusiveness'. The passage then presents a version of the hypothesis method that does not rely on conversion to another figure, using a term D to show that some C is not A. Finally, the condition for the third figure is stated: the minor must be affirmative and universal, because the reduction to the first figure requires converting one premise and making it a minor, and this conversion yields a universal affirmative only if the minor is affirmative and universal. The explanation continues, but the text cuts off.

Key Takeaway

Conditions and Productive Moods of the Second Figure
Author states the second figure's conditions and enumerates its six productive moods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two necessary conditions for a valid second-figure syllogism and recognize its six productive moods as enumerated by the author.

Core Idea
The second figure requires one affirmative minor and one universal premise.

This passage from Bahr al-'Ulum lays out the foundational rules for the second figure of the categorical syllogism. The author first argues why the minor must be affirmative and one premise must be universal, then lists the six specific mood combinations that yield valid conclusions. Understanding these conditions is crucial for constructing sound arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the structure of the second figure
In the second figure, the middle term is the predicate in both premises. This means the middle term appears in the predicate position of both the minor and major premises.
Contrast this with the first figure where the middle term is subject in one and predicate in the other.
2
Analyze the first condition: minor must be affirmative
The author argues that if the minor were negative, it would state that the minor is not an individual of the middle term. Combined with the major premise (which judges individuals of the middle), no conclusion follows about the minor being judged by the major. So the minor must be affirmative to establish a connection.
This condition ensures the minor term is related to the middle term.
3
Analyze the second condition: one premise must be universal
If both premises were particular, the judgment could apply to different subsets, and no necessary connection holds. For example, 'Some A are B' and 'Some B are C' does not guarantee that any A is C. Hence, at least one premise must be universal to distribute the middle term properly.
The author implies that the middle term must be distributed at least once, though not stating it explicitly.
4
Memorize the six productive moods
The author lists six moods: (1) both universal affirmative, (2) universal affirmative minor + universal negative major, (3) particular affirmative minor + universal affirmative major, (4) particular affirmative minor + universal negative major, (5) universal affirmative minor + particular affirmative major, (6) universal affirmative minor + particular negative major.
Note: This list includes some moods not standardly considered valid (e.g., AA). The text is taken as given; further commentary may clarify.
5
Evaluate the non-standard list
Compare with standard second-figure moods (EAE, AEE, EIO, AOO). The author's list differs, particularly in including AA and IA without a negative premise. This suggests either a different interpretation or a variant tradition. The exercise is to accept the text literally and recognize its divergence.
This step highlights critical thinking: logical texts must be examined against known rules.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الحجة
إِحْدَاهُمَا (۱) ) ، أما الأول (۲) فلأن الصغرى لو كانت سالبة لكان الحكم فيها بأن الأصغر ليس فردا للأوسط، والكبرى حاكمة بأن أفراده محكوم عليها بالأكبر، فلا يلزم الحكم بالأكبر عليه. وأما الثاني فلأنها لو كانتا جزئيتين جاز أن يكون بعض المحكوم عليه بالأصغر غير البعض الذي حكم عليه بالأكبر، فلا يلزم كونه محكوما عليه بالأكبر.
والضروب المنتجة في هذا الشكل ستة: الأول من موجبتين كليتين، الثاني من موجبةٍ كلية صغرى وسالبة كليةٍ كبرى الثالث من موجبة جزئية صغرى وموجبة كلية كبرى الرابع من موجبةٍ جزئية صغرى وسالبة كلية كبرى الخامس من موجبةٍ كلية صغرى وموجبة جزئية كبرى، السادس من موجبة كلية صغرى وسالبة جزئية كبرى.
Section on the Proof. One of them (1): as for the first (2), because if the minor premise were negative, the judgment in it would be that the minor is not an individual of the middle, and the major premise judges that its individuals are judged by the major, so the judgment of the major upon it does not follow. As for the second, because if both were particular, it is possible that some of those judged by the minor are not those judged by the major, so being judged by the major does not follow. The productive moods in this figure are six: first from two universal affirmatives, second from a universal affirmative minor and a universal negative major, third from a particular affirmative minor and a universal affirmative major, fourth from a particular affirmative minor and a universal negative major, fifth from a universal affirmative minor and a particular affirmative major, sixth from a universal affirmative minor and a particular negative major.
Source pages p. 508

Commentary
This passage defines the conditions and productive moods of the second figure of the categorical syllogism. It begins by stating two conditions: first, the minor premise must be affirmative (otherwise the middle term is not properly related); second, one premise must be universal (to ensure distribution of the middle term). Then it enumerates all six valid moods: (1) AAA, (2) AEE, (3) IAI, (4) IEO? Actually check: The fourth is IEO? No, list: 1. AA, 2. AE, 3. IA, 4. IE, 5. AI, 6. AO. In traditional notation, second figure moods are: EAE, AEE, EIO, AOO, etc. But here they list AA? That would be invalid because both premises affirmative would have no negative. Wait, the second figure requires one premise negative to conclude negatively. So mood (1) of two universal affirmatives seems problematic. However, this text may have a different interpretation or the 'second figure' here might refer to something else. Given the ambiguity, we proceed with the text as given. The author lists six moods with specific premise combinations. For example, the first mood is both premises universal affirmative, which would yield a particular affirmative conclusion in the second figure? Actually standard second figure valid moods are: EAE, AEE, EIO, AOO. So this list is non-standard. Perhaps the text is discussing a different classification. The commentary should note this discrepancy but not add outside information. Since the lesson must only explain the text, we describe the list as given. The conditions imply that the minor must be affirmative and one premise universal, so the moods listed satisfy those conditions.

Third Figure: Moods and Reduction to First Figure
Author explains third-figure moods and reduction methods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the six moods of the third figure and apply conversion and contradiction methods to reduce them to the first figure.

Core Idea
The third figure yields only particular conclusions — it refutes universal claims.

We have seen the perfect first figure. Now the third figure, though imperfect, is uniquely suited for refuting universal propositions. This lesson unpacks its six moods and the three reduction strategies used to demonstrate validity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the six moods by premise types
List each mood's minor and major premises (affirmative/negative, universal/particular). The passage gives combinations for moods 1–6.
Moods 1,3,5 produce particular affirmative; 2,4,6 produce particular negative.
2
Understand why conclusions are always particular
The subject of the conclusion is the predicate of the minor premise. Since the minor must be affirmative, its predicate is undistributed, forcing the conclusion to be particular.
Read the footnote carefully.
3
Master the khulf (contradiction) method
Assume the contradictory of the desired conclusion, combine with the minor to derive something that contradicts the major. This proves the original conclusion.
Work through the example: Every J is B, every J is A, therefore some B is A.
4
Apply conversion of the minor for moods 1–4
Convert the minor premise (swap subject and predicate) to obtain a first-figure syllogism. This works only when the major is universal.
Moods 5 and 6 have particular majors, so this method fails.
5
Apply conversion of major plus transposition
Convert the major, then transpose the premises to get a first-figure syllogism via the fourth figure.
This works for all moods.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشكل الثالث شرح بحر العلوم على سلم العلوم
فالضرب الأول والثالث والخامس ينتج موجبة جزئية، وإليها أشار بقوله: (لِيُنْتِجَ الْمُوجَبَتَانِ): الكلية والجزئية الصغريان، كما في الأول والثالث، (مع الْمُوجَبَةِ الْكُلِّيَّةِ الكبرى، (أو) الموجبة (الْكُلَيَةُ) الصغرى (مع الموجبة الْجُزْئِيَّةِ الكبرى، كما في الخامس (مُوجَبَةً جُزْئِيَّةً). والبواقي تنتج سالبة جزئية ، وإليها أشار بقوله: (وَمَعَ السَّالِبَةِ الكَليَّةِ)، أي ينتج الموجبتان: الكلية والجزئية الصغريان مع السالبة الكلية الكبرى كما في الثاني والرابع، (أو الْكُلَّيَّةُ) الموجبة الصغرى (مَعَ السَّالِبَةِ الْجُزْئِيَّةِ) الكبرى، كما في السادس ( سَالِبَةً جُزْئِيَّة (١) ،
الصغرى ينتج لا شيء بالْخُلْفِ). وهو ههنا جعل نقيض النتيجة لكليته كبرى، وصغرى القياس طرق رد إلى الأول صغرى ؛ لينتج ما ينافي الكبرى، مثلا كل (ج) (ب)، وكل (ج) (أ)، فبعض (ب) (أ)) ، وإلا فـــلا شيء من (ب) (أ)»، وهو مع من (ج) (أ)»، وقد كان في الكبرى كل (ج) (أ) . وقس عليه بواقي الأمور. أَوْ عَكس الصُّغْرَى) ؛ ليرتد إلى الشكل الأول، فينتج النتيجة المطلوبة، وهذا إنما يجري في الضروب الأربعة الأولِ ، وأما الخامس والسادس فلكون الكبرى جزئية غير صالحة لكبروية الشكل الأول، (أَوْ) عکس (الْكُبْرَى)؛ ليرتد إلى الشكل الرابع ، (ثُمَّ) عکس (التَّرْتِيبِ)، فيصير شكلا أول، فينتج
(۱) من الملاحظ أن هذا الشكل لا ينتج إلا نتيجة جزئية، سواء كانت سالبة أو موجبة، وهذا أمر لم يكن منه بد؛ وذلك لأن موضوع النتيجة دائما هو محمول الصغرى، ومن شرط هذا الشكل أن تكون الصغرى موجبة ، فمحمولها سيكون غير مستغرق باستمرار، ويجب أن يكون في النتيجة أيضا غير مستغرق كذلك ، وهذا أن تكون النتيجة يوجب الأخرى جزئية دائما. وانظر لتبرير آخر في شرح العضد لمختصر ابن الحاجب: ٢٨. وقيمة هذا الشكل أنه إنما يستخدم في رد حكم كلي أو تفنيد قضية كلية.
The Third Figure. Commentary of Bahr al-Ulum on Sullam al-Ulum. The first, third, and fifth moods produce a particular affirmative; and it is indicated by his saying: 'The two affirmatives produce' — the universal and particular minors, as in the first and third, with the universal affirmative major, or the universal affirmative minor with the particular affirmative major, as in the fifth, produce a particular affirmative. The remaining ones produce a particular negative, indicated by his saying: 'And with the universal negative' — that is, the two affirmatives, universal and particular minors, with the universal negative major produce, as in the second and fourth; or the universal affirmative minor with the particular negative major, as in the sixth, produce a particular negative (1). The minor produces nothing by the method of contradiction (khulf). It is here to make the contradictory of the conclusion, as a universal major, and the minor premise of the syllogism, as a method of reduction to the first figure as a minor, so as to produce what contradicts the major. For example: Every J is B, and every J is A, therefore some B is A. Otherwise, no B is A, and that together with every J is A, while the major was every J is A. And analogize the rest. Or conversion of the minor: to reduce to the first figure, so it produces the desired conclusion. This applies only to the first four moods; as for the fifth and sixth, because the major is particular, it is not suitable as a major for the first figure. Or conversion of the major: to reduce to the fourth figure, then conversion of the order: so it becomes a first figure, and produces. (1) It is observed that this figure produces only a particular conclusion, whether negative or affirmative, and this is inevitable; because the subject of the conclusion is always the predicate of the minor, and the condition of this figure is that the minor be affirmative, so its predicate will always be undistributed, and it must also be undistributed in the conclusion, which necessitates that the conclusion always be particular. For another justification, see Sharh al-Adud on Mukhtasar Ibn al-Hajib: 28. The value of this figure is that it is used to refute a universal judgment or falsify a universal proposition.
Source pages p. 508

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the third figure of the categorical syllogism. It first identifies which moods yield a particular affirmative conclusion: the first, third, and fifth moods. The first mood has a universal affirmative minor and a universal affirmative major; the third has a particular affirmative minor and a universal affirmative major; the fifth has a universal affirmative minor and a particular affirmative major. All produce a particular affirmative. The remaining moods (second, fourth, sixth) produce a particular negative conclusion: the second with universal affirmative minor and universal negative major; the fourth with particular affirmative minor and universal negative major; the sixth with universal affirmative minor and particular negative major. The passage then explains three methods of reducing third-figure moods to the first figure: (1) by contradiction (khulf) — assume the contradictory of the conclusion, which with the minor yields a conclusion that contradicts the major, proving the original conclusion; (2) by conversion of the minor — for the first four moods only, since in the fifth and sixth the major is particular and cannot serve as major in the first figure; (3) by conversion of the major followed by conversion of the order (i.e., transposition of premises) to convert to the fourth figure and then to the first. A footnote explains why the third figure yields only particular conclusions: the subject of the conclusion is the predicate of the minor, and since the minor must be affirmative, its predicate is undistributed, so the conclusion's subject must also be undistributed, forcing a particular judgment. This figure is valuable for refuting universal claims.

Key Terms
شكل ثالث
الخلف
عكس
رد إلى الأول

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a third-figure categorical syllogism on a Fiqh topic. For example, using the premise 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' (major) and 'Every intoxicant is a beverage' (minor), state the conclusion and identify the mood and figure. Then reduce it to the first figure using conversion of the minor.

Third Figure: Moods, Conclusions, and Reduction Methods
Author enumerates moods, conclusions, and reduction methods for third figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the six moods of the third figure, determine their conclusion types, and apply reduction methods (contradiction, conversion, assumption) to convert third-figure syllogisms to the first figure.

Core Idea
The third figure always yields a particular conclusion; its reduction methods guarantee all valid moods are provable.

This passage expands on the third figure of the categorical syllogism, explaining its six moods, their particular conclusions, and the techniques for reducing them to the first figure. Mastering these reductions is essential for transforming any third-figure argument into the most transparent syllogistic form.

Guided Path
1
Identify the six moods
From the enumeration, note that moods 1, 3, 5 produce affirmative particular conclusions, while moods 2, 4, 6 produce negative particular conclusions. The pattern depends on the quantity and quality of the premises.
The author refers to the original text's phrasing: 'liyuntija al-mūjibatān' etc.
2
Learn reduction by contradiction (al-radd bi'l-khalf)
Take the contradictory of the desired conclusion as a premise. Combine it with one original premise to derive a contradiction with the other original premise. This proves the conclusion must hold.
Example: If conclusion 'some B is A' is denied ('no B is A'), with 'every C is A' we get 'no C is B' contradicting 'every C is B'.
3
Learn conversion methods (al-aks)
Convert the minor premise to fit the first figure (only for moods 1-4). Alternatively, convert the major (to fourth figure), then transpose premises and convert the conclusion. This works for moods 1 and 5.
Conversion preserves quantity and quality for universal negatives and particulars.
4
Learn assumption (al-iftirāḍ) for particular premises
When the minor or major is particular affirmative, assume an individual (د) that falls under that term. Then construct a universal syllogism with د as subject, deriving the desired particular conclusion.
This method does not apply to moods 1 and 2 to avoid circularity.
5
Distinguish causal from non-causal demonstration
The above methods show only that the conclusion follows (luzum). The causal demonstration (bayān limmī) shows that the conclusion holds essentially because the middle term's individuals are shared between premises.
The causal demonstration applies to all moods by interpreting the assumption method in terms of shared individuals.
