الشكل الثالث. فالضرب الأول والثالث والخامس ينتج موجبة جزئية، وإليها أشار بقوله: (لِيُنْتِجَ الْمُوجَبَتَانِ): الكلية والجزئية الصغريان، كما في الأول والثالث، (مع الْمُوجَبَةِ الْكُلِّيَّةِ الكبرى، (أو) الموجبة (الْكُلَيَةُ) الصغرى (مع الموجبة الْجُزْئِيَّةِ الكبرى، كما في الخامس (مُوجَبَةً جُزْئِيَّةً). والبواقي تنتج سالبة جزئية، وإليها أشار بقوله: (وَمَعَ السَّالِبَةِ الكَليَّةِ)، أي ينتج الموجبتان: الكلية والجزئية الصغريان مع السالبة الكلية الكبرى كما في الثاني والرابع، (أو الْكُلَّيَّةُ) الموجبة الصغرى (مَعَ السَّالِبَةِ الْجُزْئِيَّةِ) الكبرى، كما في السادس (سَالِبَةً جُزْئِيَّةً). وطرق ردها إلى الأول: أَوِ الرَّدَّ بِالْخُلْفِ). وهو ههنا جعل نقيض النتيجة كبرى، وصغرى القياس طرق رد إلى الأول صغرى؛ لينتج ما ينافي الكبرى، مثلا كل (ج) (ب)، وكل (ج) (أ)، فبعض (ب) (أ))، وإلا فـــلا شيء من (ب) (أ)»، وهو مع من (ج) (أ)»، وقد كان في الكبرى كل (ج) (أ). وقس عليه بواقي الأمور. أَوْ عَكْس الصُّغْرَى)؛ ليرتد إلى الشكل الأول، فينتج النتيجة المطلوبة، وهذا إنما يجري في الضروب الأربعة الأولِ، وأما الخامس والسادس فلكون الكبرى جزئية غير صالحة لكبروية الشكل الأول، (أَوْ) عکس (الْكُبْرَى)؛ ليرتد إلى الشكل الرابع، (ثُمَّ) عکس (التَّرْتِيبِ)، فيصير شكلا أول، فينتج النتيجة، (ثُمَّ) عكس (النَّتيجة)؛ ليحصل النتيجة المطلوبة. وهذا إنما يجري في الأول والخامس، دون البواقي، كما لا يخفى. أَوِ الرَّدَّ إِلَى الثَّانِي بِعَكْسِهِمَا)، فينتج النتيجة المطلوبة، وهذا إنما يجري في الثاني والرابع، دون الأول والثالث؛ لإيجابهما، والسادس؛ لجزئية الكبرى، والخامس؛ لإيجابهما ولجزئية الكبرى معا. وربما يبين في الافتراض في الصغرى إذا كانت موجبة جزئية، مثلا بعض (ج) (ب)، وكل (ج) (أ)) فيفرض ذلك البعض (د)، فكل (د) (ج)، وكل (ج) (أ)، فكل (د) (أ)، فنقول (د) (ب) و(د) (أ) ، فبعض (ب) (أ). وفي الكبرى إذا كانت جزئية، مثلا كل (ج) (ب)، وبعض (ج) (أ))، فنفرضه (د)، فكل (د) (ج)، وكل (ج) (ب)، فكل (د) (ب) وكل (د) (أ)، فبعض (ب) (أ)، وهذا لا يجري في الأولين، وإلا دار. وإنما لم ينتج الأولان كلية؛ لأن الأصغر يجوز عمومه من الأكبر، نحو كل إنسان حيوان، وكل إنسان ناطق، أو لا شيء من الإنسان بفرس. ثم هذه البيانات لا تفيد إلا لزوم النتيجة لمقدمتي هذا الشكل، ولا تفيد اللمية، أو أن اللزوم بالذات. وأما البيان اللمي فهو أن الصغرى قد حكمت أن أفراد الأوسط اد الأصغر، والكبرى قد حكمت أن تلك الأفراد مما يثبت له الأكبر، أو هي أفراد يسلب عنه، واشترك أفراد الأوسط في الصغرى والكبرى؛ لكون إحداهما كلية، فبعض أفراد الأصغر ما ثبت له الأكبر أو سلب عنه، وقد ظهر بهذا أن النتيجة لازمة لهذا الشكل لزوما بالذات. وإن شئت فأرجع حاصل الافتراض إلى هذا؛ ليصير برهانا لميا جاريا في جميع الضرورب. والعلم الحق عند علام الغيوب. ولما كان لمتوهم أن يتوهم أن هذين الشكلين لا ينتجان إلا بالإرجاع.
The third figure. The first, third, and fifth moods produce an affirmative particular conclusion, and to that the author pointed by his saying: 'The two affirmatives produce' — the universal and particular minor, as in the first and third, with the universal affirmative major, or the universal affirmative minor with the particular affirmative major, as in the fifth, [produce] an affirmative particular. And the remaining moods produce a negative particular, and to that the author pointed by his saying: 'And with the universal negative' — that is, the two affirmatives, the universal and particular minor, with the universal negative major, as in the second and fourth, or the universal affirmative minor with the particular negative major, as in the sixth, [produce] a negative particular. And the methods of reducing it to the first [figure]: either reduction by contradiction. And that here is making the contradictory of the conclusion a major [premise], and a minor [premise] of the syllogism as a method of reduction to the first [figure] as a minor, so that it yields what contradicts the major. For example, 'Every (j) is (b) and every (j) is (a)' so 'Some (b) is (a)', otherwise 'No (b) is (a)', and that together with 'Some (j) is (a)', while in the major [original] it was 'Every (j) is (a)'. And analogize to that the remaining cases. Or conversion of the minor, so that it returns to the first figure, and then it yields the desired conclusion. And this applies only to the first four moods; as for the fifth and sixth, because the major is particular, it is not suitable to be a major in the first figure. Or conversion of the major, so that it returns to the fourth figure, then conversion of the order, so it becomes a first figure, then it yields the conclusion, then conversion of the conclusion, so that the desired conclusion is obtained. And this applies only to the first and fifth, not to the remaining, as is not hidden. Or reduction to the second figure by converting both premises, so it yields the desired conclusion. And this applies only to the second and fourth, not to the first and third because they are affirmative, nor to the sixth because the major is particular, nor to the fifth because they are affirmative and because of the particularity of the major together. And sometimes it is explained by assumption for the minor when it is affirmative particular, for example 'Some (j) is (b) and every (j) is (a)', so that particular is assumed to be (d), so 'Every (d) is (j) and every (j) is (a)', so 'Every (d) is (a)', so we say '(d) is (b) and (d) is (a)', so 'Some (b) is (a)'. And for the major when it is particular, for example 'Every (j) is (b) and some (j) is (a)', so we assume it (d), so 'Every (d) is (j) and every (j) is (b)', so 'Every (d) is (b) and every (d) is (a)', so 'Some (b) is (a)'. And this does not apply to the first two [moods], otherwise there would be circularity. And the first two [moods] do not produce a universal because the minor term may be more general than the major, such as 'Every human is an animal, and every human is rational', or 'No human is a horse'. Then these demonstrations do not give anything except the necessitation of the conclusion from the two premises of this figure, and they do not give the causal [demonstration], or that the necessitation is essential. As for the causal demonstration, it is that the minor premise has ruled that the individuals of the middle [term] are included in the minor, and the major premise has ruled that those individuals are such that the major is affirmed of them, or are individuals from which it is negated, and the individuals of the middle term are shared in the minor and major because one of them is universal, so some individuals of the minor are those for which the major is affirmed or negated. And by this it has become evident that the conclusion is necessitated for this figure by an essential necessitation. And if you wish, refer the result of assumption to this, so that it becomes a causal demonstration applying to all moods. And true knowledge is with the Knower of the unseen. And since it might be thought by one who would think that these two figures produce [conclusions] only through reduction.
Source pages p. 509

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the third figure of the categorical syllogism (al-shakl al-thalith), explaining its six moods, their conclusions, and the various methods of reducing them to the first figure or others. The author first enumerates the six moods: first, third, and fifth produce an affirmative particular conclusion (مُوجَبَة جُزْئِيَّة), while the remaining moods (second, fourth, sixth) produce a negative particular conclusion (سَالِبَة جُزْئِيَّة). The text uses a commentary style, referring to an original author's phrasing (e.g., 'ليُنتج الموجبتان'). Next, the author lists the methods of reduction (طرق الرد إلى الأول). The first method is reduction by contradiction (الرد بالخلف): taking the contradictory of the desired conclusion as a premise and showing it leads to a contradiction with the original major. The second is conversion of the minor premise (عكس الصغرى) to fit the first figure, but this works only for the first four moods because in the fifth and sixth the major is particular and cannot serve as a major in the first figure. The third is conversion of the major premise (عكس الكبرى) to the fourth figure, then converting the order (عكس الترتيب) to make it a first figure, then converting the conclusion (عكس النتيجة) to get the desired conclusion; this works only for the first and fifth moods. The fourth is reduction to the second figure by converting both premises (الرد إلى الثاني بعكسهما), which works for the second and fourth moods. The fifth is the method of assumption (الافتراض) for moods where the minor or major is particular: assuming that particular instance as a distinct term (د) and constructing a syllogism with universal premises to produce the desired conclusion. This method does not apply to the first two moods (which have universal majors and affirmative minors) to avoid circularity. The author then explains why the first two moods (with universal major and affirmative minor) do not produce universal conclusions: because the minor term (al-asghar) may be more general than the major (al-akbar), as in 'Every human is an animal' and 'Every human is rational'—the conclusion 'Some animal is rational' is particular, not 'Every animal is rational'. Finally, the author distinguishes between demonstrations that only prove the necessitation (luzum) of the conclusion from the premises and those that prove it essentially (bil-dhat) or causally (al-bayan al-linniyy). The causal demonstration shows that the middle term's individuals are included in the minor and are affirmed/negated of the major, leading to a particular conclusion. The passage closes with a note that the reduction methods might lead some to think these figures only produce conclusions via reduction, but that is not the case.

Key Terms
الشكل الثالث
الرَّدَّ بِالْخُلْفِ
عَكْس الصُّغْرَى
الافتراض
البيان اللمي

Which reduction method is explicitly stated to work for the fifth mood of the third figure?

Key Takeaway
The third figure's six moods all yield particular conclusions, and each mood can be reduced to the first figure via a specific method (contradiction, conversion, assumption), ensuring all valid arguments are provable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a valid third-figure syllogism using fiqh terms: 'Every intoxicant is haram, every intoxicant is a drink, therefore some drink is haram.' Identify its mood (first mood of third figure) and conclusion type (affirmative particular).
?
Reduce the following third-figure syllogism to the first figure using conversion of the minor: 'No human (ج) is a horse (ب), every human (ج) is an animal (أ), therefore some animal (أ) is not a horse (ب).' Show the steps.

The Syllogism
intermediate
6 min
Methods of Syllogistic Reduction: Conversion and Reductio
Author lists and explains conversion and reductio methods for reducing syllogisms to first figure, with mood applicability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which moods can be reduced to the first figure by conversion of the conclusion and which by reductio to the second figure, and explain the reasons for exclusion.

Core Idea
Not all syllogisms can be reduced by the same method; each reduction method has its own domain of moods.

In this lesson, we explore the first two methods introduced by the author for reducing syllogisms to the first figure: conversion of the conclusion and reductio to the second figure. You will learn which moods each method applies to and why other moods are excluded. This understanding is crucial for transforming any valid syllogism into the demonstrative first figure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two reduction methods
The author mentions two methods: (1) converting the conclusion of a syllogism, and (2) using reductio by converting both premises to yield the conclusion in the second figure.
2
List the moods each method applies to
For conversion of conclusion: only the first and fifth moods. For reductio: only the second and fourth.
3
Understand the exclusions
The author explains why other moods are excluded: first and third are excluded from reductio because they are affirmative; sixth because the major is particular; fifth because of both affirmation and particularity.
4
Reflect on why reduction is useful
Reduction to the first figure standardizes the syllogism for clear demonstration.

فصل في الحجة
النتيجة ، (ثُمَّ) عكس (النَّتيجة) ؛ ليحصل النتيجة المطلوبة. وهذا إنما يجري في الأول والخامس، دون البواقي ، كما لا يخفى.
أَوِ الرَّدَّ إِلَى الثَّانِي بِعَكْسِهِمَا)، فينتج النتيجة المطلوبة، وهذا إنما يجري في الثاني والرابع، دون الأول والثالث ؛ لإيجابهما، والسادس؛ لجزئية الكبرى ، والخامس ؛ لإيجابهما ولجزئية الكبرى معا. وربما يبين في الافتراض في الصغرى إذا كانت موجبة جزئية ، مثلا بعض (ج) (ب)، وكل (ج) (أ)) فيفرض ذلك البعض (د)، فكل (د) (ج) ، وكل (ج) (أ)، فكل (د) (أ)، فنقول (د) (ب) و(د) (أ) ، فبعض (ب) (أ) . وفي الكبرى إذا كانت جزئية ، مثلا كل (ج) (ب) ، وبعض (ج) (أ)) ، فنفرضه (د)، فكل (د) (ج)، وكل (ج) (ب) ، فكل (د) (ب) وكل (د) (أ) ، فبعض (ب) (أ)، وهذا لا يجري في الأولين، وإلا دار.
وإنما لم ينتج الأولان كلية ؛ لأن الأصغر يجوز عمومه من الأكبر، نحو كل إنسان حيوان، وكل إنسان ناطق، أو لا شيء من الإنسان بفرس. ثم هذه البيانات لا تفيد إلا لزوم النتيجة لمقدمتي هذا الشكل، ولا تفيد اللمية، أو أن اللزوم بالذات. وأما البيان اللمي فهو أن الصغرى قد حكمت أن أفراد الأوسط اد الأصغر ، والكبرى قد حكمت أن تلك الأفراد مما يثبت له الأكبر، أو هي أفراد يسلب عنه، واشترك أفراد الأوسط في الصغرى والكبرى ؛ لكون إحداهما كلية ، فبعض أفراد الأصغر ما ثبت له الأكبر أو سلب عنه، وقد ظهر بهذا أن النتيجة لازمة لهذا الشكل لزوما بالذات. وإن شئت فأرجع حاصل الافتراض إلى هذا؛ ليصير برهانا لميا جاريا في جميع الضرورب. والعلم الحق عند علام الغيوب.
Chapter on the argument. The conclusion, then convert the conclusion to obtain the desired conclusion. This applies only to the first and fifth [moods], not the rest, as is clear. Or reductio to the second by converting both, thus producing the desired conclusion. This applies only to the second and fourth, not the first and third because of their affirmatives, nor the sixth because of the particularity of the major, nor the fifth because of both their affirmatives and the particularity of the major. And it may be shown by hypothesis: in the minor if it is affirmative particular, e.g., 'Some (J) is (B)' and 'Every (J) is (A)', then we hypothesize that particular (J) as (D), so every (D) is (J) and every (J) is (A), therefore every (D) is (A). Then we say (D) is (B) and (D) is (A), so some (B) is (A). And in the major if particular, e.g., 'Every (J) is (B)' and 'Some (J) is (A)', we hypothesize it as (D), so every (D) is (J) and every (J) is (B), therefore every (D) is (B) and every (D) is (A), so some (B) is (A). This does not apply to the first two [moods] otherwise it would be circular. The first two [moods] do not produce a universal [conclusion] because the minor may be more general than the major, e.g., 'Every human is an animal', 'Every human is rational', or 'No human is a horse'. Then these demonstrations only yield the entailment of the conclusion from the premises of this figure, not the causal explanation (al-lamiyyah) or that the entailment is essential. As for the causal demonstration, it is that the minor premise has judged that the individuals of the middle are [individuals] of the minor, and the major premise has judged that those individuals are such that the major is affirmed of them or negated of them. And the individuals of the middle are shared between the minor and major because one of them is universal, so some individuals of the minor have the major affirmed or negated of them. By this it becomes clear that the conclusion necessarily follows from this figure essentially. And if you wish, refer the gist of the hypothesis to this so that it becomes a causal proof for all moods. And true knowledge is with the Knower of the Unseen.
Source pages p. 510

Commentary
This passage is an exposition on methods for reducing syllogisms in other figures to the first figure. The author begins by listing two methods: conversion (عكس) of the conclusion and reductio (رد) to the second figure by converting both premises. He specifies which moods these apply to. Then he introduces a hypothetical method (افتراض) for handling particular premises, providing detailed examples using variables (ج, ب, أ) and a hypothesized term (د). He explains why the first two moods do not produce universal conclusions, citing examples like 'Every human is an animal' and 'Every human is rational'. The author then distinguishes between demonstrations that merely show entailment (لزوم) and those that show the causal reason (البيان اللمي). He argues that the hypothetical method can be recast as a causal proof. Throughout, the commentary follows the author's order, clarifying that reduction to the first figure does not negate the independent value of other figures—they have their own natural arrangements.

رد

Conversion of the conclusion reduces first and fifth moods; reductio to second reduces second and fourth moods.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Syllogism
intermediate
7 min
The Hypothetical Method (Iftirad) for Handling Particular Premises
Author explains how to reduce syllogisms with particular premises using a hypothetical individual.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the hypothetical method to reduce syllogisms with particular premises to the first figure, using a hypothesized term.

Core Idea
Particular premises can be handled by assuming an individual that instantiates the particular term, enabling a universal syllogism.

When a syllogism contains a particular premise, the standard reduction methods may not suffice. The author introduces a hypothetical method (iftirad) in which we postulate a specific individual (D) that stands for the particular term, allowing us to construct a universal syllogism. This method is applied to cases where the minor or major premise is particular.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premise that is particular
The author gives two cases: minor premise particular (Some J is B) or major premise particular (Some J is A).
2
Introduce the hypothesized term D
We assume a new term D that represents the particular J in question.
3
Construct a universal syllogism using D
Since D is an individual of J, we have 'Every D is J' and the given universal premise (e.g., 'Every J is A'), yielding 'Every D is A'.
4
Combine with the other premise
For the minor case, we have 'D is B' and 'D is A', so 'Some B is A'. For the major case, similarly.
5
Check the limitation
This method does not work for the first two moods because it would lead to circularity.

Or reductio to the second by converting both, thus producing the desired conclusion. This applies only to the second and fourth, not the first and third because of their affirmatives, nor the sixth because of the particularity of the major, nor the fifth because of both their affirmatives and the particularity of the major. And it may be shown by hypothesis: in the minor if it is affirmative particular, e.g., 'Some (J) is (B)' and 'Every (J) is (A)', then we hypothesize that particular (J) as (D), so every (D) is (J) and every (J) is (A), therefore every (D) is (A). Then we say (D) is (B) and (D) is (A), so some (B) is (A). And in the major if particular, e.g., 'Every (J) is (B)' and 'Some (J) is (A)', we hypothesize it as (D), so every (D) is (J) and every (J) is (B), therefore every (D) is (B) and every (D) is (A), so some (B) is (A). This does not apply to the first two [moods] otherwise it would be circular. The first two [moods] do not produce a universal [conclusion] because the minor may be more general than the major, e.g., 'Every human is an animal', 'Every human is rational', or 'No human is a horse'. Then these demonstrations only yield the entailment of the conclusion from the premises of this figure, not the causal explanation (al-lamiyyah) or that the entailment is essential. As for the causal demonstration, it is that the minor premise has judged that the individuals of the middle are [individuals] of the minor, and the major premise has judged that those individuals are such that the major is affirmed of them or negated of them. And the individuals of the middle are shared between the minor and major because one of them is universal, so some individuals of the minor have the major affirmed or negated of them. By this it becomes clear that the conclusion necessarily follows from this figure essentially. And if you wish, refer the gist of the hypothesis to this so that it becomes a causal proof for all moods. And true knowledge is with the Knower of the Unseen.
Source pages p. 510

Commentary
This passage is an exposition on methods for reducing syllogisms in other figures to the first figure. The author begins by listing two methods: conversion (عكس) of the conclusion and reductio (رد) to the second figure by converting both premises. He specifies which moods these apply to. Then he introduces a hypothetical method (افتراض) for handling particular premises, providing detailed examples using variables (ج, ب, أ) and a hypothesized term (د). He explains why the first two moods do not produce universal conclusions, citing examples like 'Every human is an animal' and 'Every human is rational'. The author then distinguishes between demonstrations that merely show entailment (لزوم) and those that show the causal reason (البيان اللمي). He argues that the hypothetical method can be recast as a causal proof. Throughout, the commentary follows the author's order, clarifying that reduction to the first figure does not negate the independent value of other figures—they have their own natural arrangements.

Key Terms
افتراض
لزوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the hypothetical method for a particular minor premise, what is the first universal premise we affirm about D?
A) Every D is B
B) Every D is J
C) Every D is A
D) Some D is J

Key Takeaway
The hypothetical method introduces a placeholder individual to convert a particular premise into a universal one, enabling reduction to the first figure.

Distinguishing Demonstrations of Entailment from Causal Demonstrations
Author distinguishes between demonstration of entailment and causal demonstration, and suggests the hypothetical method can be seen as causal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between a demonstration that merely shows entailment (luzum) and a causal demonstration (bayan lami) in a syllogism, and identify which is used in a given argument.

Core Idea
A syllogism can prove that the conclusion follows, but a causal demonstration explains why it follows by revealing the essential connection of terms.

After discussing reduction methods, the author reflects on the nature of the demonstrations provided. He distinguishes between proofs that only show entailment (luzum) and those that show the causal reason (lami). The earlier reduction methods, including the hypothetical method, only demonstrate that the conclusion necessarily follows from the premises. A causal demonstration, however, reveals that the conclusion is essentially necessary by showing how the terms are connected.

Guided Path
1
Understand the distinction
The author says that the previous demonstrations only give entailment, not the reason why (lamiyyah).
2
Identify the causal demonstration
The author describes a causal demonstration: the minor premise states that individuals of the middle are individuals of the minor; the major states that those individuals are determined by the major. Because one premise is universal, the individuals of the middle are shared, so some individuals of the minor have the major affirmed or negated.
3
See how this reveals essential connection
The causal demonstration shows that the conclusion follows because of the intrinsic relationship between the terms, not just because of the form.
4
Relate back to the hypothetical method
The author suggests that the hypothetical method can be recast as a causal proof if one understands it as sharing of individuals.

The Independent Value of the Second and Third Figures
Author argues that second and third figures have independent value due to natural order of terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to defend the independent value of the second and third figures using the concept of natural order and the argument from al-Shifa.

Core Idea
Natural order gives the second and third figures unique value despite reducibility.

In this lesson, we examine why the second and third figures of the categorical syllogism are not dispensable even though they can be reduced to the first figure. The author cites Ibn Sina's al-Shifa to argue that these figures have a 'natural order' of terms that would be lost upon conversion. Understanding this helps us appreciate the full power of syllogistic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection being refuted
What is the potential misconception about the second and third figures?
The objection is that they are unnecessary because they can be reduced to the first figure.
2
Find the key term 'natural order' (طبعي)
How does the author define natural order in the example 'Man is a writer'?
Man is fixed as subject, writer as predicate; conversion would make it unnatural.
3
Analyze the argument from al-Shifa
Why does al-Shifa claim that these figures have a special property (خاصية)?
Because the natural composition of premises may only be possible in these figures.
4
Explain why reduction is not sufficient
If conversion makes premises unnatural, why are the second and third figures needed?
Reduction would destroy the natural order, requiring extra demonstration.

And since a conceiver might conceive that these two figures [second and third] do not produce except by reduction to the first figure, so there is no need to consider them, he refutes it by saying: 'And in al-Shifa it is stated that these two figures, even if they are reduced to the first, have a special property, namely that the natural [order] in some premises is that one of the two terms is fixed for subjecthood or predicatehood,' as in our saying 'Man is a writer', so man is fixed for subjecthood and writer for predicatehood, 'such that if it were converted it would be unnatural', and needs a demonstration for its proof. 'So the natural composition of premises may not be arranged except in one of these two [figures],' and if one of the premises were converted it would become unnatural, 'so there is no being free from need of them.' The condition for its production, and God knows best, and it is stipulated in the fourth figure one of two things: and explanation of its moods: both affirmative with the universality of the minor, or their difference in quality with the universality of one of them. And if neither of these two conditions is met, but they are either both affirmative with particularity of the minor, or both negative, or differing with both particular, then difference [of quantity or quality] in the conclusion necessitates barrenness. As for the first form, it is because of the truth of 'Some animal is human, and every horse is animal', or 'Every rational is animal', and the true [conclusion] in the first is negative and in the second affirmative. And as for the second form, it is because of the truth of 'No stone is human', and ...

Commentary
This passage defends the independent value of the second and third figures by citing al-Shifa, which argues that these figures have a 'natural' order of terms that is lost if converted to the first figure. The author uses the example 'Man is a writer' to show that the subject and predicate are naturally fixed. He then turns to the fourth figure, stipulating two conditions for its productivity: either both premises are affirmative with a universal minor, or they differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative) with at least one universal. If the conditions are violated—e.g., both affirmative with particular minor, both negative, or differing quality with both particular—the result is barren. The author provides examples: for the first violated condition, 'Some animal is human, every horse is animal' would yield a negative in the first case and affirmative in the second, showing inconsistency. This commentary follows the author's order, explaining each condition and its rationale.

D) They are the only figures that yield universal conclusions

Key Takeaway
The second and third figures have independent value because they accommodate the natural order of terms, which would be disrupted by reduction to the first figure.

Conditions for a Productive Fourth Figure
Author states the two conditions for a productive fourth figure and gives counterexamples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a given fourth figure syllogism is productive by checking the two stated conditions: both affirmative with universal minor, or differing quality with at least one universal.

Core Idea
The fourth figure is productive only under specific conditions of quality and quantity.

The author now turns to the fourth figure, outlining exactly when a fourth figure syllogism yields a valid conclusion and when it does not. Two conditions are given, followed by counterexamples. Understanding these conditions is essential for using the fourth figure correctly in Islamic logical analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions
What are the two conditions for productivity?
Both affirmative with universal minor, or differing quality with at least one universal.
2
Examine the first condition
If both premises are affirmative, what must be true of the minor premise?
The minor must be universal.
3
Examine the second condition
If premises differ in quality, what must be true?
At least one premise must be universal.
4
Analyze the violated cases
Look at the examples: 'Some animal is human, every horse is animal' and 'No stone is human...' What conditions do they violate?
First violates condition 1 (both affirmative but minor particular); second violates condition 2 (both negative or differing with both particular).
5
Understand the result
Why do these violations lead to barrenness?
Because the conclusion would be inconsistent—sometimes negative, sometimes affirmative.

Why the Fourth Figure is Not Redundant
Defense of the fourth figure's necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the rationale behind the fourth figure's necessity using the concept of natural term ordering.

Core Idea
The fourth figure preserves natural subject-predicate order that the first figure loses when terms are reversed.

Some critics argue that the fourth figure of the categorical syllogism is superfluous because it can be reduced to the first figure. The commentator, drawing on Ibn Sina's al-Shifa, refutes this by showing that each figure has a natural arrangement of terms that cannot always be replicated naturally in the first figure. Understanding this defense helps you appreciate why classical logicians maintained four distinct figures.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection being refuted
What is the claim that the fourth figure is unnecessary? Look for the phrase 'defence of the fourth figure' in the commentary.
The objectors say that the fourth figure reduces to the first, so there is no need for separate treatment.
2
Extract the concept of 'natural ordering' (tabi‘i)
The commentator cites al-Shifa: in some premises, one term is naturally suited to be subject and the other predicate. Find where he states that reversing would be 'unnatural'.
Natural order is based on linguistic or epistemic priority.
3
Apply the concept to the example 'Man is a writer'
Why is 'Man is a writer' natural? If reversed to 'A writer is man', it becomes unnatural. Explain how this relates to the fourth figure.
In the fourth figure, the middle term is naturally subject in the major premise and predicate in the minor, which the first figure would invert.
4
Connect the fourth figure's utility
How does this show that the fourth figure is not redundant? Summarize the commentator's final point about the natural composition of premises.
The natural arrangement may only fit one of the figures, so we cannot dispense with either.

وَهِيَ أَنَّ الطَّبَعِي فِي بَعْضِ الْمُقَدِّمَاتِ أَنَّ أَحَدَ الطَّرَفَيْنِ مُتَعَيِّنْ لِلْمَوْضُوعِيَّةِ أَوِ الْمَحْمُولِيَّةِ)، كما في قولنا الإنسان كاتب، فالإنسان متعين بالموضوعية والكاتب بالمحمولية، (حَتَّى لَوْ عُكِسَ كَانَ غَيْرَ طَبَعِي)، ويحتاج في إثباتهما إلى بيان، (فَالتَّأْليف الطَّبَعِيُّ) للمقدمات رُبَّمَا لَمْ يَنْتَظِمْ إِلَّا عَلَى أَحَدِ هَذَيْنِ)، ولو عكس إحدى المقدمات صارت غير طبعية ، (فَلَيْسَ عَنْهُمَا غُنْيَةٌ . شرط إنتاج (هَذَا)، والله أعلم ، (وَ) يشترط (في) الشكل (الرابع) أحد الأمرين: وبيان ضروبة (إيجَابُهُمَا مَعَ كُليّةِ الصُّغْرَى، أَوِ اخْتِلَافُهُمَا في الكيف (مَعَ كُلِّيَّةِ إِحْدَاهُمَا ، وَإِلَّا يكن أحد هذين الأمرين ، بل يكوناً إما موجبتين مع جزئية الصغرى، أو سالبتين ، أو مختلفتين مع جزئيتهما (لَزِمَ الِاخْتِلَافُ) في النتيجة الموجِبُ للعقمِ. أما في الصورة الأولى (١ وهي أن تكون الصغرى جزئية والصغرى والكبرى موجبتين، والقياس في هذه الحالة فاسد ؛ لأن الكبرى إذا كانت موجبة فمحمولها - وهو الحد الأوسط ـ غير مستغرق ، ولما كان الحد الأوسط موضوعا في الصغرى وجب أن تكون هذه الصغرى كلية ؛ حتى يمكن استغراقه فيها ؛ لأن الجزئية - موجبة أو سالبة - لا تستغرق الموضوع. انظر مدخل لدراسة المنطق القديم: ١٣٣) فَلِصِدْقِ بعض الحيوان إنسان، وكل فرس حيوان»، أو «كل ناطق حيوان»، والصادق في الأول السلب، وفي الثاني الإيجاب. وأما في الصورة الثانية (٢ وهي الخسة أن تكون الصغرى والكبرى سالبتين. وفساد القياس هنا واضح؛ حيث وجدت وهي السلب ـ في كلتا المقدمتين، وقد سبق أن قال المصنف لا إنتاج من سالبتين، وبيناه أحسن بيان هناك) فَلِصِدْقِ لا شيء من الحجر بإنسان، ولا شيء من الناطق بحجر والصادق فيه الإيجاب، أو «لا شيء من الفرس بحجر»، والصادق السلب. وأما في الصورة الثالثة (۱ وهي أن تكون إحدى المقدمتين موجبة والأخرى سالبة وأن تكونا جزئيتين. وهذا القياس فاسد ؛ لأن النتيجة سالبة مستغرقة المحمول، وهذا المحمول هو موضوع الكبرى الجزئية ، فلكي نضمن له الاستغراق لزم كون الكبرى كلية . انظر مدخل لدراسة المنطق القديم: ١٣٤) فَلِصِدْقِ «بعض الحيوان غُرَاب ، وبعض الأسود ليس بحيوان ، والصادق الإيجاب، أو بعض الأبيض ليس بحيوان والصادق السلب . والضروب في هذا الشكل ثمانية: الأول من موجبتين كليتين، الثاني من موجبة كلية صغرى وموجبة جزئية كبرى الثالث من سالبة كلية صغرى وموجبة كلية كبرى ، الرابع عكسُه ، الخامس من موجبة جزئية صغرى وسالبة كلية كبرى السادس من سالبة جزئية صغرى وموجبة كلية كبرى، السابع من موجبة كلية صغرى وسالبة جزئية كبرى الثامن من سالبة كلية صغرى وموجبة جزئية كبرى (۲ اعلم أن هذا التفصيل على رأي المتأخرين ، وأما على رأي المتقدمين فالضروب المنتجة لهذا الشكل خمسة، وعليه ابن الحاجب؛ فإن المتقدمين أسقطوا أيضا إنتاج الصغرى السالبة الجزئية مع الكبرى الموجبة الكلية، والصغرى الموجبة الكلية مع الكبرى السالبة الجزئية، والصغرى السالبة الكلية مع الكبرى الموجبة الجزئية، فيكون الساقط مجموع إحدى عشرة ؛ لأن بيان هذه الثلاثة على انعكاس السالبة الجزئية والمتقدمون لما اعتقدوا عدم انعكاسها حصروا الضروب المنتجة في الخمسة، وبينوا عقم هذه الثلاثة بالاختلاف في النتيجة. وأجاب المتأخرون بأن بيان الاختلاف في هذه الضروب إنما يتم إذا كان القياس مركبا من المقدمات البسيطة، لكنه يشترط في إنتاجها أن تكون السالبة المستعملة فيها من إحدى الخاصتين راجع الرسالة الشمسية مع شرح القطب: ٢٠٩/٢، ٢١٠، مختصر المنتهى مع شرح العضد ۲۹ ، ۳۰ ، حاشية العطار على الخبيصي: ٤٠٢ ، مناهج البحث عند مفكري الإسلام د/ على سامي النشار: ٦٨ - ٠٧٠)
The fourth productive figure. Commentary of Bahr al-Ulum on Sullam al-Ulum. (He) refuted (the claim) that they return to the first figure, so there is no need to consider them, by his statement: 'In the Shifa, these two figures, even if they reduce to the first, have a special characteristic, which is that the natural (order) in some premises is that one of the two terms is determined for subjecthood or predication,' as in our statement 'Man is a writer'; man is determined for subjecthood and writer for predication, 'so that if reversed it would be unnatural,' and proving them requires demonstration, 'so the natural composition of premises may not be arranged except in one of these two (ways),' and if one of the premises were reversed it would become unnatural, 'so there is no dispensation from them.' The condition for productivity of this (figure), and God knows best: it is required in the fourth figure one of two things: and the exposition of its moods: 'Both (premises) affirmative with the universality of the minor, or their difference in quality with the universality of one of them.' And if neither of these two conditions obtains, but they are either both affirmative with the minor particular, or both negative, or different in quality with both particular, then 'difference (in the conclusion) occurs,' which necessitates sterility. As for the first form (1: which is that the minor is particular and both minor and major are affirmative; the syllogism in this case is invalid because if the major is affirmative, its predicate—the middle term—is not distributed, and since the middle term is subject in the minor, the minor must be universal so that distribution can occur in it, because the particular—affirmative or negative—does not distribute the subject. See: Introduction to the Study of Ancient Logic, p. 133), then (he says) because of the truth of 'Some animal is man' and 'Every horse is animal' or 'Every speaker is animal,' and the true (conclusion) in the first is negative, and in the second affirmative. As for the second form (2: which is lowliness: that the minor and major are both negative. The invalidity of the syllogism here is clear, because negativity is found in both premises, and the author has already said there is no production from two negatives, and we explained it well there), then because of the truth of 'No stone is man' and 'No speaker is stone' and the true (conclusion) is affirmative, or 'No horse is stone' and the true (conclusion) is negative. As for the third form (1: which is that one premise is affirmative and the other negative, and both are particular. This syllogism is invalid because the conclusion is negative with distributed predicate, and this predicate is the subject of the major premise which is particular, so to guarantee its distribution the major must be universal. See: Introduction to the Study of Ancient Logic, p. 134), then because of the truth of 'Some animal is a crow' and 'Some black is not animal' and the true (conclusion) is affirmative, or 'Some white is not animal' and the true (conclusion) is negative. The moods in this figure are eight: first from two universal affirmatives, second from universal affirmative minor and particular affirmative major, third from universal negative minor and universal affirmative major, fourth its reverse, fifth from particular affirmative minor and universal negative major, sixth from particular negative minor and universal affirmative major, seventh from universal affirmative minor and particular negative major, eighth from universal negative minor and particular affirmative major. (2 Know that this detail is according to the later logicians. As for the early logicians, the productive moods of this figure are five, and Ibn al-Hajib follows them. The early logicians also dropped the productivity of the particular negative minor with universal affirmative major, and the universal affirmative minor with particular negative major, and the universal negative minor with particular affirmative major. So the dropped (moods) total eleven because the demonstration of these three depends on the conversion of the particular negative, and the early logicians, believing it does not convert, restricted the productive moods to five and demonstrated the sterility of these three by difference in conclusion. The later logicians replied that the demonstration of difference in these moods is only complete if the syllogism is composed of simple premises, but its productivity requires that the negative used in them be one of the two special (types). Refer to al-Risala al-Shamsiyya with Sharh al-Qutb: 2/209, 210; Mukhtasar al-Muntaha with Sharh al-Adud: 29, 30; Hashiyat al-Attar on al-Khubaysi: 402; Manahij al-Bahth 'inda Mufakkiri al-Islam by Dr. Ali Sami al-Nashshar: 68-70.)
Source pages p. 511

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the matn of Sullam al-Ulum, explaining the fourth figure of the categorical syllogism. The commentator begins by defending the fourth figure against those who argue it reduces to the first and is therefore superfluous. He cites Ibn Sina's al-Shifa, noting that these two figures (the second and third? the text says 'these two figures' but the context is the fourth figure and another, perhaps the third? Actually the commentator is referring to the fourth and something else. The point is that despite reducibility, each figure has a natural ordering of terms: in some premises, a term is naturally subject and the other predicate, as in 'Man is a writer' where reversing would be unnatural. Thus the natural syllogistic arrangement may require one of these figures, so they are not dispensable.

Next, the commentator states the condition for the fourth figure's productivity: either both premises are affirmative with the minor premise universal, or the premises differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative) with at least one premise universal. If neither condition is met, three problematic cases arise:
1. Both premises affirmative with a particular minor: example given 'Some animal is man, every horse is animal' yields no necessary conclusion.
2. Both premises negative: example 'No stone is man, no speaker is stone' yields no conclusion.
3. Mixed quality with both particular: example 'Some animal is crow, some black is not animal' yields ambiguous conclusion.

The commentator then lists the eight moods of the fourth figure as per later logicians: (1) AA (universal affirmative minor, universal affirmative major), (2) A with I (universal affirmative minor, particular affirmative major), (3) EA (universal negative minor, universal affirmative major), (4) its reverse AE? Actually 'its reverse' likely means (4) from universal affirmative minor and universal negative major, (5) IA (particular affirmative minor, universal negative major), (6) OA (particular negative minor, universal affirmative major), (7) AO (universal affirmative minor, particular negative major), (8) EO (universal negative minor, particular affirmative major).

Finally, the commentator notes a difference between early and later logicians. Early logicians (like Ibn al-Hajib) recognized only five productive moods, rejecting three that depend on the conversion of the particular negative proposition, which they did not accept. Later logicians defended these moods by arguing that the negative in those cases is of a special type that does allow conversion. This shows how logical doctrines evolved.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the fourth figure not considered redundant?
A) Because it produces conclusions that the first figure cannot produce at all.
B) Because it has a natural term ordering that the first figure sometimes cannot replicate naturally.
C) Because it was explicitly mentioned by Aristotle.
D) Because it allows more moods than other figures.

Key Takeaway
The fourth figure is not redundant; it maintains the natural order of terms that the first figure would invert.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the proposition: 'The world is created'. If you were to construct a syllogism where the natural term order demands that 'the world' be subject in the conclusion, which figure would you use? Explain using the concept of natural ordering.
?

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Conditions for a Valid Fourth Figure Syllogism
Conditions for productivity in the fourth figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a fourth figure syllogism is productive by checking the quantity and quality of its premises.

Core Idea
A fourth figure syllogism is productive only if either both premises are affirmative with a universal minor, or they differ in quality with at least one universal.

In the fourth figure, productivity depends on specific conditions regarding the universality and quality of the premises. The commentator enumerates three invalid forms that violate these conditions. By mastering these conditions, you can quickly evaluate validity of syllogisms encountered in Islamic legal reasoning and theology.

Guided Path
1
State the two productivity conditions
From the Arabic: 'Both premises affirmative with the universality of the minor, or their difference in quality with the universality of one of them.' Write them in your own words.
2
Test the first violation: both affirmative with particular minor
Example: 'Some animal is man' (I) and 'Every horse is animal' (A). Why does this fail? Analyze distribution of the middle term.
The middle term 'animal' is not distributed in either premise because the particular affirmative does not distribute its subject.
3
Test the second violation: both premises negative
Example: 'No stone is man' (E) and 'No speaker is stone' (E). Two negatives never produce a conclusion.
This is a general rule applicable to all figures.
4
Test the third violation: mixed quality with both particular
Example: 'Some animal is crow' (I) and 'Some black is not animal' (O). Why does a particular major cause indeterminacy?
The conclusion would be negative with a distributed predicate, but the predicate in the major is not distributed.
5
Summarize the rule in a decision table
Create a table listing the conditions and invalid forms for quick reference.

الجزئية، فيكون الساقط مجموع إحدى عشرة ؛ لأن بيان هذه الثلاثة على انعكاس السالبة الجزئية والمتقدمون لما اعتقدوا عدم انعكاسها حصروا الضروب المنتجة في الخمسة، وبينوا عقم هذه الثلاثة بالاختلاف في النتيجة. القطب: ٢٠٩/٢، ٢١٠، مختصر المنتهى مع شرح العضد ۲۹ ، ۳۰ ، حاشية العطار على الخبيصي: ٤٠٢ ، مناهج البحث عند مفكري الإسلام د/ على سامي النشار: ٦٨ - ٠٧٠)
The fourth productive figure. Commentary of Bahr al-Ulum on Sullam al-Ulum. (He) refuted (the claim) that they return to the first figure, so there is no need to consider them, by his statement: 'In the Shifa, these two figures, even if they reduce to the first, have a special characteristic, which is that the natural (order) in some premises is that one of the two terms is determined for subjecthood or predication,' as in our statement 'Man is a writer'; man is determined for subjecthood and writer for predication, 'so that if reversed it would be unnatural,' and proving them requires demonstration, 'so the natural composition of premises may not be arranged except in one of these two (ways),' and if one of the premises were reversed it would become unnatural, 'so there is no dispensation from them.' The condition for productivity of this (figure), and God knows best: it is required in the fourth figure one of two things: and the exposition of its moods: 'Both (premises) affirmative with the universality of the minor, or their difference in quality with the universality of one of them.' And if neither of these two conditions obtains, but they are either both affirmative with the minor particular, or both negative, or different in quality with both particular, then 'difference (in the conclusion) occurs,' which necessitates sterility. As for the first form (1: which is that the minor is particular and both minor and major are affirmative; the syllogism in this case is invalid because if the major is affirmative, its predicate—the middle term—is not distributed, and since the middle term is subject in the minor, the minor must be universal so that distribution can occur in it, because the particular—affirmative or negative—does not distribute the subject. See: Introduction to the Study of Ancient Logic, p. 133), then (he says) because of the truth of 'Some animal is man' and 'Every horse is animal' or 'Every speaker is animal,' and the true (conclusion) in the first is negative, and in the second affirmative. As for the second form (2: which is lowliness: that the minor and major are both negative. The invalidity of the syllogism here is clear, because negativity is found in both premises, and the author has already said there is no production from two negatives, and we explained it well there), then because of the truth of 'No stone is man' and 'No speaker is stone' and the true (conclusion) is affirmative, or 'No horse is stone' and the true (conclusion) is negative. As for the third form (1: which is that one premise is affirmative and the other negative, and both are particular. This syllogism is invalid because the conclusion is negative with distributed predicate, and this predicate is the subject of the major premise which is particular, so to guarantee its distribution the major must be universal. See: Introduction to the Study of Ancient Logic, p. 134), then because of the truth of 'Some animal is a crow' and 'Some black is not animal' and the true (conclusion) is affirmative, or 'Some white is not animal' and the true (conclusion) is negative. The moods in this figure are eight: first from two universal affirmatives, second from universal affirmative minor and particular affirmative major, third from universal negative minor and universal affirmative major, fourth its reverse, fifth from particular affirmative minor and universal negative major, sixth from particular negative minor and universal affirmative major, seventh from universal affirmative minor and particular negative major, eighth from universal negative minor and particular affirmative major. (2 Know that this detail is according to the later logicians. As for the early logicians, the productive moods of this figure are five, and Ibn al-Hajib follows them. The early logicians also dropped the productivity of the particular negative minor with universal affirmative major, and the universal affirmative minor with particular negative major, and the universal negative minor with particular affirmative major. So the dropped (moods) total eleven because the demonstration of these three depends on the conversion of the particular negative, and the early logicians, believing it does not convert, restricted the productive moods to five and demonstrated the sterility of these three by difference in conclusion. The later logicians replied that the demonstration of difference in these moods is only complete if the syllogism is composed of simple premises, but its productivity requires that the negative used in them be one of the two special (types). Refer to al-Risala al-Shamsiyya with Sharh al-Qutb: 2/209, 210; Mukhtasar al-Muntaha with Sharh al-Adud: 29, 30; Hashiyat al-Attar on al-Khubaysi: 402; Manahij al-Bahth 'inda Mufakkiri al-Islam by Dr. Ali Sami al-Nashshar: 68-70.)
Source pages p. 511

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the matn of Sullam al-Ulum, explaining the fourth figure of the categorical syllogism. The commentator begins by defending the fourth figure against those who argue it reduces to the first and is therefore superfluous. He cites Ibn Sina's al-Shifa, noting that these two figures (the second and third? the text says 'these two figures' but the context is the fourth figure and another, perhaps the third? Actually the commentator is referring to the fourth and something else. The point is that despite reducibility, each figure has a natural ordering of terms: in some premises, a term is naturally subject and the other predicate, as in 'Man is a writer' where reversing would be unnatural. Thus the natural syllogistic arrangement may require one of these figures, so they are not dispensable.

Next, the commentator states the condition for the fourth figure's productivity: either both premises are affirmative with the minor premise universal, or the premises differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative) with at least one premise universal. If neither condition is met, three problematic cases arise:
1. Both premises affirmative with a particular minor: example given 'Some animal is man, every horse is animal' yields no necessary conclusion.
2. Both premises negative: example 'No stone is man, no speaker is stone' yields no conclusion.
3. Mixed quality with both particular: example 'Some animal is crow, some black is not animal' yields ambiguous conclusion.

The commentator then lists the eight moods of the fourth figure as per later logicians: (1) AA (universal affirmative minor, universal affirmative major), (2) A with I (universal affirmative minor, particular affirmative major), (3) EA (universal negative minor, universal affirmative major), (4) its reverse AE? Actually 'its reverse' likely means (4) from universal affirmative minor and universal negative major, (5) IA (particular affirmative minor, universal negative major), (6) OA (particular negative minor, universal affirmative major), (7) AO (universal affirmative minor, particular negative major), (8) EO (universal negative minor, particular affirmative major).

Finally, the commentator notes a difference between early and later logicians. Early logicians (like Ibn al-Hajib) recognized only five productive moods, rejecting three that depend on the conversion of the particular negative proposition, which they did not accept. Later logicians defended these moods by arguing that the negative in those cases is of a special type that does allow conversion. This shows how logical doctrines evolved.

الإنتاج
الكلية
الجزئية

Key Takeaway
The fourth figure's productivity hinges on the distribution of the middle term, which requires universality in at least one premise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the following premises: (Minor) Some fard acts are prayers (I), (Major) All acts of worship are fard (A). Determine whether this is a valid fourth figure syllogism. If not, which condition does it violate?

The Eight Moods and the Five-Mood Dispute
Enumeration of the eight moods and historical dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the eight moods of the fourth figure and explain the difference between early and later logicians' positions.

Core Idea
Later logicians expanded the productive moods from five to eight by accepting conversion of the particular negative.

After establishing conditions, the commentator enumerates the eight moods of the fourth figure as recognized by later logicians. He then notes that early logicians, following Ibn al-Hajib, only accepted five moods because they rejected the conversion of particular negative propositions. This debate illustrates how logical principles can evolve over time and how later scholars refined earlier doctrines.

Guided Path
1
List the eight moods using traditional notation
Write the eight combinations using A, I, E, O for minor and major premises. Use the order: minor, major.
From the text: 1) AA, 2) AI, 3) EA, 4) AE, 5) IE, 6) OA, 7) AO, 8) EO. Note that some are pairs.
2
Identify the three moods disputed by early logicians
Which three are not productive according to early logicians? (Hint: those involving the particular negative in minor or major?)
The early logicians rejected OA (minor O, major A), AO (minor A, major O), and EO (minor E, major O). These are moods 6, 7, and 8.
3
Understand the early logicians' reasoning
Why did they reject these? Their rejection stems from the belief that the particular negative (O) cannot undergo conversion (aks). Without conversion, the syllogism cannot be reduced to the first figure.
Conversion is needed to bring the fourth figure moods to the first figure for validity check.
4
Understand the later logicians' defense
Later logicians argued that the negative in these moods is of a special type that does allow conversion (in some cases?). They maintained that the moods are valid if the negative is one of the two special types (probably referring to the discussion in al-Risala al-Shamsiyya).
This is a technical point; the text references al-Risala al-Shamsiyya and other works.
5
Compare the five accepted moods
List the five moods accepted by early logicians: AA, AI, EA, AE, IE. (These are moods 1-5). Notice that they accept IE but not OA. Why?
IE combines a particular affirmative minor with universal negative major, which can be reduced using conversion of the universal negative.

The commentator begins by defending the fourth figure against those who argue it reduces to the first and is therefore superfluous. The Syllogism
intermediate
7 min
Eight Moods of the Fourth Figure and the Early-Late Disagreement
Author enumerates the eight moods of the fourth figure and discusses the controversy over three moods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the eight moods of the fourth figure and explain the disagreement between early and later logicians regarding three of them.

Core Idea
The fourth figure's moods are not universally agreed upon; the dispute hinges on the conversion of the particular negative.

The fourth figure of the categorical syllogism has a specific arrangement of the middle term, leading to eight potential mood combinations. However, early logicians rejected three of these moods because they did not accept the conversion of the particular negative proposition. This lesson walks through the moods and the historical debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the structure of the fourth figure
In the fourth figure, the middle term (al-ḥadd al-awsaṭ) is the predicate of the minor premise and the subject of the major premise. Understand this orientation before examining the moods.
This figure is sometimes called Galen's figure.
2
Enumerate the eight moods from the text
List the moods as given: 1) AA (universal affirmatives), 2) AI (universal affirmative minor, particular affirmative major), 3) EA (universal negative minor, universal affirmative major), 4) AE (reverse of mood 3), 5) IE (particular affirmative minor, universal negative major), 6) OI? Actually the text says: sixth from particular negative minor and universal affirmative major – that is OE? But the author uses 'saliba juz'iyya' (particular negative) and 'mujiba kulliyya' (universal affirmative). So mood 6: O? Actually it's a particular negative and universal affirmative – that is (O, A). Similarly mood 7: universal affirmative and particular negative (A, O). Mood 8: universal negative and particular affirmative (E, I). Note that mood 8 is invalid as the footnote explains. Write them in standard logical form.
The moods are traditionally named: Bramantip, Camenes, Dimaris, Fesapo, etc. But the text does not use those names.
3
Identify the three disputed moods
The early logicians (al-mutaqaddimūn) rejected moods 6, 7, and 8: (O,A), (A,O), (E,I). They argued these are infertile because the conclusion would be negative universal, requiring the major premise to be universal, which it is not in these combinations. Their rejection is based on the denial of conversion of the particular negative.
The early logicians thus accepted only five moods: AA, AI, EA, AE, IE (i.e., moods 1-5).
4
Understand the later logicians' defense
The later logicians (al-muta'akhkhirūn) accepted the conversion of the particular negative and defended the three additional moods. They specified that the negative used in these moods must be from the 'two special properties' (al-khāṣṣatān), meaning the negative premise must be such that its subject term is universal or has a special property to allow the syllogism to still be productive. This shows how a technical logical disagreement changes the count of valid moods.
This dispute highlights the importance of immediate inferences (like conversion) in determining syllogistic validity.

والضروب في هذا الشكل ثمانية: الأول من موجبتين كليتين، الثاني من موجبة كلية صغرى وموجبة جزئية كبرى، الثالث من سالبة كلية صغرى وموجبة كلية كبرى، الرابع عكسه، الخامس من موجبة جزئية صغرى وسالبة كلية كبرى، السادس من سالبة جزئية صغرى وموجبة كلية كبرى، السابع من موجبة كلية صغرى وسالبة جزئية كبرى، الثامن من سالبة كلية صغرى وموجبة جزئية كبرى (۱) وهي أن تكون إحدى المقدمتين موجبة والأخرى سالبة وأن تكونا جزئيتين. وهذا القياس فاسد؛ لأن النتيجة سالبة مستغرقة المحمول، وهذا المحمول هو موضوع الكبرى الجزئية، فلكي نضمن له الاستغراق لزم كون الكبرى كلية. انظر مدخل لدراسة المنطق القديم: ١٣٤. (۲) اعلم أن هذا التفصيل على رأي المتأخرين، وأما على رأي المتقدمين فالضروب المنتجة لهذا الشكل خمسة، وعليه ابن الحاجب؛ فإن المتقدمين أسقطوا أيضا إنتاج الصغرى السالبة الجزئية مع الكبرى الموجبة الكلية، والصغرى الموجبة الكلية مع الكبرى السالبة الجزئية، والصغرى السالبة الكلية مع الكبرى الموجبة الجزئية، فيكون الساقط مجموع إحدى عشرة؛ لأن بيان هذه الثلاثة على انعكاس السالبة الجزئية والمتقدمون لما اعتقدوا عدم انعكاسها حصروا الضروب المنتجة في الخمسة، وبينوا عقم هذه الثلاثة بالاختلاف في النتيجة. وأجاب المتأخرون بأن بيان الاختلاف في هذه الضروب إنما يتم إذا كان القياس مركبا من المقدمات البسيطة، لكنه يشترط في إنتاجها أن تكون السالبة المستعملة فيها من إحدى الخاصتين. راجع الرسالة الشمسية مع شرح القطب: ٢٠٩/٢، ٢١٠، مختصر المنتهى مع شرح العضد ۲۹، ۳۰، حاشية العطار على الخبيصي: ٤٠٢، مناهج البحث عند مفكري الإسلام د/ على سامي النشار: ٦٨-٠٧٠.
The moods in this figure are eight: first from two universal affirmatives, second from a universal affirmative minor and a particular affirmative major, third from a universal negative minor and a universal affirmative major, fourth its reverse, fifth from a particular affirmative minor and a universal negative major, sixth from a particular negative minor and a universal affirmative major, seventh from a universal affirmative minor and a particular negative major, eighth from a universal negative minor and a particular affirmative major. (1) Which is that one of the two premises is affirmative and the other negative and that they are both particular. And this syllogism is invalid because the conclusion is a negative with a universal predicate, and this predicate is the subject of the particular major, so to guarantee its universality the major must be universal. See Madkhal li-Dirasat al-Mantiq al-Qadim: 134. (2) Know that this enumeration is according to the later logicians. As for the earlier logicians, the productive moods of this figure are five, and Ibn al-Hajib follows them. For the earlier logicians also dropped the production of the particular negative minor with the universal affirmative major, and the universal affirmative minor with the particular negative major, and the universal negative minor with the particular affirmative major, so the dropped total is eleven. Because the demonstration of these three depends on the conversion of the particular negative, and the earlier logicians, since they believed it does not convert, restricted the productive moods to five, and they showed the sterility of these three by the difference in the conclusion. The later logicians answered that the demonstration of the difference in these moods is only complete if the syllogism is composed of simple premises, but for their production it is required that the negative used in them be one of the two special properties. Refer to al-Risala al-Shamsiyya with Sharh al-Qutb: 2/209, 210, Mukhtasar al-Muntaha with Sharh al-Adud: 29, 30, Hashiyat al-Attar on al-Khubaysi: 402, Manahij al-Bahth by Ali Sami al-Nashshar: 68-70.
Source pages p. 512

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of the eight moods of the fourth figure of the categorical syllogism according to the later logicians, along with a discussion of the disagreement with the earlier logicians. The author first lists the moods: they consist of combinations of universal and particular, affirmative and negative premises. Then in footnote (1) he clarifies that the eighth mood (both premises particular with opposite quality) is invalid because it would require a universal major premise. In footnote (2) he contrasts the later logicians' view (eight productive moods) with the earlier logicians' view (only five). The early logicians rejected three moods because they did not accept the conversion of the particular negative. The later logicians defend the extra moods by specifying that the negative used must be from the two special properties, meaning the negative must be such that it can be used in a syllogism with simple premises. The passage thus provides both an enumeration and a historical dialectical context.

Fourth Figure Moods and Conclusion Rule
Commentator explains how the matn author indicated each productive mood and states the conclusion rule.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which fourth figure mood corresponds to a given pair of premises and state the conclusion rule based on negation presence.

Core Idea
The fourth figure's eight moods are mastered by grouping them under the matn's concise statements and a single conclusion rule.

The commentator unpacks the matn's dense indication of the eight productive moods of the fourth figure. Instead of listing each mood separately, the matn uses a few phrases that cover multiple moods. This lesson will guide you through those phrases and the general rule for determining the conclusion based on the presence or absence of negation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mood groups
The matn groups moods by the type of minor premise combined with specific majors. From the commentary, list the four groups: (1) universal affirmative minor with the four majors, covering moods 1, 2, 4, 7; (2) particular affirmative minor with universal negative major, covering mood 5; (3) two negative minors (universal and particular) with universal affirmative major, covering moods 3 and 6; (4) universal negative minor with particular affirmative major, covering mood 8.
2
Match each group to the matn's phrase
For each group, locate the exact Arabic phrase from the matn that the commentator quotes. For example, 'الموجبة الكلية الصغرى مع الكبريات الأربع' for the first group. This exercise trains you to read the matn's compressed expressions.
3
Apply the conclusion rule
The passage gives a rule: if both premises have no negation (i.e., both affirmative), the conclusion is affirmative particular; if there is negation in one premise, the conclusion is negative particular, except in mood 3 (universal negative minor and universal affirmative major) which yields universal negative. Understand that the exception arises because the universal negative minor and universal affirmative major together force a universal negative conclusion.
4
Verify with an example
Take mood 3: minor is universal negative (e.g., 'No A is B'), major is universal affirmative (e.g., 'Every B is C'). According to the rule, since there is negation (in the minor), the conclusion should be negative particular, but the exception says it is universal negative. Check by constructing a concrete example: 'No man is stone; every stone is inanimate; therefore no man is inanimate?' Actually, 'No man is stone' (SaP), 'Every stone is inanimate' (PaM) yields 'No man is inanimate' (SeP) which is universal negative. So the exception holds.

The Syllogism

The Eight Productive Moods of the First Figure
Enumerates the eight productive moods of the first figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which combination of minor and major premises yields each of the eight productive moods of the first figure and state which moods produce universal vs. particular conclusions.

Core Idea
Eight moods, only two are basic — the rest reduce to them via conversion or reductio.

The commentator enumerates the eight productive moods by pairing the four types of minor premise with the four types of major premise. The key is to notice that when the minor is affirmative, the conclusion follows the quality of the major; when the minor is negative, the conclusion is negative (except one case). Understanding this enumeration is the foundation for recognizing valid first-figure arguments in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Classify the minor premises
The minor premise can be universal affirmative (كلّيَة موجبة), particular affirmative (جزئية موجبة), universal negative (كلّيَة سالبة), or particular negative (جزئية سالبة). The commentator pairs each with the four major premises (the same four types).
2
Match the first two moods
When the minor is universal affirmative, with the four majors: with universal affirmative produces universal affirmative (mood 1); with particular affirmative produces particular affirmative (mood 2); with universal negative produces universal negative (mood 4); with particular negative produces particular negative (mood 7).
Moods 1 and 2 are the basic ones.
3
Match mood 5
When the minor is particular affirmative, it only produces a result with the universal negative major, yielding a particular affirmative (mood 5).
No negation in minor, so conclusion affirmative.
4
Match the negative minors
When the minor is universal negative with universal affirmative major, it yields universal negative (mood 3). When particular negative with universal affirmative, yields particular negative (mood 6).
The third mood is the only one that yields a universal negative; the rest with negative minors yield particular negatives.
5
Match mood 8
When the minor is universal negative with particular affirmative major, it yields particular affirmative (mood 8) because there is no negation in the major.
The commentator notes: 'since there is no negation in them' (the majors), the conclusion is affirmative.

Methods of proof by reductio ad absurdum (khalf), which here is joining the contradictory of the conclusion to one of the two premises, so that the first figure produces something that converts to contradict the other. So in the first two, he makes the major [premise] because of its universality, and the minor [remains] the minor, e.g., 'Every J is B', and 'Every A is J', so 'Some B is A'; otherwise, 'No B is A', and it with the minor produces 'No J is A', and it converts to 'No A is J', whereas in the major [it was] 'Every A is J', this is a contradiction. In the third, fourth and fifth, he makes its affirmative minor and the major of the syllogism because of its universality major, e.g., 'No J is B', and 'Every A is J', so 'No B is A'; otherwise, 'Some B is A', and it with the major produces 'Some B is J', and it converts to 'Some J is B', whereas in the minor [it was] 'No J is B', this is a contradiction. And it does not apply to the rest.
'Or by conversion of order (aks al-tartib), to revert to the first figure, so it produces a result, then conversion of the result to obtain the required. And it applies only to the first two, and the third and the eighth, not the rest, e.g., 'No J is B', and 'Some A is J', so we converted the order and said: 'Some A is J', and 'No J is B', so it produced by the first figure 'Some A is not B', and it converts to 'Some B is not A', based on the conversion of the particular negative of the two properties.'
'Or by conversion of both premises (aks al-muqaddimatayn), to revert to the first figure, so it produces the required result. And this applies only to the fourth and fifth, not the rest, as is not hidden, e.g., 'Every J is B', and 'No A is J', so we converted both premises and the form of the syllogism became: 'Some B is J', and 'No J is A', so 'Some B is not A', which is the required.
'Or by conversion of the minor (sughra), to revert to the second figure, so it produces the required result. And this applies only to the third, fourth, fifth and sixth, e.g., 'Some J is not B', and 'Every A is J', so we converted the minor and it became: 'Some B is not J', and 'Every A is J', so 'Some B is not A', which is the required. And this also...
Source pages p. 513

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the matn of Sullam, explaining the eight productive moods of the first figure of the categorical syllogism and the methods by which their conclusions can be proven or reduced to the basic moods of the first figure. The commentator first enumerates the eight moods by pairing the minor premise (which can be universal affirmative, particular affirmative, universal negative, or particular negative) with each of the four major premises, following the order of the matn's phrases. Thus, the first mood (minor universal affirmative with major universal affirmative) produces a universal affirmative conclusion; the second (minor universal affirmative with major particular affirmative) produces a particular affirmative; the third (minor universal negative with major universal affirmative) produces a universal negative; the fourth (minor universal affirmative with major universal negative) produces a universal negative; the fifth (minor particular affirmative with major universal negative) produces a particular affirmative — because there is no negation in the minor, negation only in the major, so the conclusion is affirmative and particular; the sixth (minor particular negative with major universal affirmative) produces a particular negative; the seventh (minor universal affirmative with major particular negative) produces a particular negative; the eighth (minor universal negative with major particular affirmative) produces a particular affirmative, since there is no negation in the major and the minor is negative, but the conclusion must be particular. The exception is the third mood, which yields a universal negative.

After listing the moods, the commentator explains four methods of proof (reduction) to the first figure. The first method is reductio ad absurdum (khalf). This works by taking the contradictory of the conclusion and adding it to one of the premises, which yields a conclusion that contradicts the other premise. The example given is for the first mood: if the original syllogism is 'Every J is B' and 'Every A is J' giving 'Some B is A', then assuming its contradictory 'No B is A' together with the minor 'Every A is J' gives 'No J is A', which converts to 'No A is J', contradicting the major 'Every A is J'. This method works for moods 1,2,3,4,5 but not the rest.

The second method is conversion of order ('aks al-tartib): swapping the order of premises to form a first-figure syllogism, then converting the result. This works for moods 1,2,3,8. Example: 'No J is B, Some A is J' becomes 'Some A is J, No J is B' (first figure), producing 'Some A is not B', then converting to 'Some B is not A' via conversion of the particular negative.

The third method is conversion of both premises ('aks al-muqaddimatayn) for moods 4 and 5. Example: 'Every J is B, No A is J' becomes 'Some B is J, No J is A' (after converting both premises), producing 'Some B is not A'.

The fourth method is conversion of the minor premise only ('aks al-sughra) to revert to the second figure, for moods 3,4,5,6. Example: 'Some J is not B, Every A is J' becomes 'Some B is not J, Every A is J' (second figure), producing 'Some B is not A'.

The commentator notes the specific applicability of each method, all within the framework of establishing the validity of the eight moods.

Checkpoint
Which mood of the first figure produces a universal negative conclusion?
A) Minor universal affirmative, major universal negative (mood 4)
B) Minor universal negative, major universal affirmative (mood 3)
C) Minor particular negative, major universal affirmative (mood 6)
D) Minor universal negative, major particular affirmative (mood 8)

Key Takeaway
The eight moods of the first figure are generated by pairing each of four minor premises with each of four major premises, with the third mood being the only one that yields a universal negative.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given a first-figure syllogism: 'All intoxicants are haram; khamr is an intoxicant; therefore khamr is haram.' Identify its mood by classifying the premises (universal affirmative minor and major) and note it produces a universal affirmative conclusion.
?
Construct a first-figure syllogism in the fourth mood (minor universal affirmative, major universal negative) using a fiqh example, e.g., 'All transactions with interest are prohibited; this contract is a transaction with interest; therefore this contract is prohibited.' Check the conclusion: universal negative.

The Syllogism
advanced

Methods of Reducing Moods to the First Figure
Explains four methods of proof/reduction for non-basic moods of the first figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply four proof methods (khalf, aks al-tartib, aks al-muqaddimatayn, aks al-sughra) to reduce non-basic moods of the first figure to the basic moods and verify their validity.

Core Idea
Non-basic moods are proven valid by reducing them to the basic moods using conversion or reductio.

The commentator explains four methods to prove the remaining six moods (beyond the first two) by reducing them to the first figure. These methods are: reductio ad absurdum (khalf), conversion of order (aks al-tartib), conversion of both premises (aks al-muqaddimatayn), and conversion of the minor (aks al-sughra). Each method works for specific moods, and understanding them is essential for establishing the validity of all first-figure syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the goal of reduction
We want to show that a mood like 'No J is B, Every A is J, therefore No B is A' is valid. The method takes the contradictory of the conclusion and combines it with one premise to deduce a contradiction with the other premise.
2
Apply khalf (reductio)
Assume the contradictory of the conclusion (e.g., 'Some B is A' instead of 'No B is A'). Combine it with one premise using first-figure rules. The result will contradict the other premise. For example, in mood 3: assume 'Some B is A', with the minor 'No J is B'? Actually the example given is for mood 1: assume 'No B is A' with minor 'Every A is J' gives 'No J is A', which converts to 'No A is J', contradicting 'Every A is J'.
Khalf works for moods 1,2,3,4,5.
3
Apply aks al-tartib (conversion of order)
Swap the minor and major premises to form a first-figure syllogism with the original major now as minor. Then convert the conclusion to obtain the desired result. This works for moods 1,2,3,8.
Example: 'No J is B, Some A is J' becomes 'Some A is J, No J is B' (first figure), producing 'Some A is not B', then convert to 'Some B is not A'.
4
Apply aks al-muqaddimatayn (conversion of both premises)
Convert both premises individually, then reorder to get a first-figure syllogism. The new conclusion will be the required one. This works for moods 4 and 5.
Example: 'Every J is B, No A is J' becomes 'Some B is J, No J is A', producing 'Some B is not A'.
5
Apply aks al-sughra (conversion of the minor)
Convert only the minor premise, then form a second-figure syllogism, which yields the required conclusion. This works for moods 3,4,5,6.
Example: 'Some J is not B, Every A is J' becomes 'Some B is not J, Every A is J' (second figure), producing 'Some B is not A'.
