Determining Conclusion Modality in Mixed Syllogisms
Rule for conclusion modality in mixed syllogisms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the modality of the conclusion in a mixed modal syllogism by applying the rules for descriptive and non-descriptive major premises.

Core Idea
The conclusion's modality mirrors the major premise unless the major is descriptive; then it mirrors the minor minus its existential condition.

In mixed syllogisms, premises have different modal strengths—some are necessary, others are conditional or temporal. This passage lays down the precise rule for how the modality of the conclusion is derived. Understanding this rule is crucial for evaluating arguments in Islamic theology (kalām) and jurisprudence (fiqh) that combine different types of statements, such as necessary divine attributes with temporal created things.

Guided Path
1
Identify the modality of each premise
Determine whether each premise is necessary, assertoric, descriptive (wasfiyya), or conditional. In the passage, focus on the distinction between 'descriptive' and non-descriptive. Descriptive propositions include a time or condition (e.g., 'as long as it exists').
2
Check if the major premise is descriptive
The rule: if the major premise is not descriptive, the conclusion's modality matches the major premise directly. If it is descriptive, proceed to modify the minor premise.
3
Modify the minor premise when major is descriptive
When the major is descriptive, the conclusion takes the modality of the minor but with two changes: (a) remove any existential condition (qayd al-wujud) from the minor, and (b) add the existential condition from the major. This ensures the conclusion reflects the temporal or conditional scope of the major.
4
Apply the rule to a concrete example
Consider the example: minor: 'Every human is laughing (not permanently)' [la-dawām], major: 'Every laughing thing is an animal as long as it laughs' [descriptive]. Here the major is descriptive, so the conclusion must be like the minor (la-dawām) but with the major's condition: 'Every human is an animal as long as it laughs' (since the minor's existential condition is removed and the major's condition is added).
The original example shows that if the minor were permanent, the conclusion would be false, demonstrating the necessity of the removal.
5
Consider the special case of conditional-general (mashrūṭah ʿāmmah) major
The author notes an exception: if the major is a 'conditional-general' proposition, the conclusion is like the minor without removing the existential condition. This is because the major’s condition already ties the predicate to the middle term, so no removal is needed.
This nuance shows the depth of Islamic modal logic.

Chapter on Mixed [Syllogisms]
It is impossible that its occurrence [the supposition] is impossible with respect to its essence, but it is permissible that it entails [something] with respect to what is actual, so it is permissible that the necessary conclusion after supposing occurrence is impossible, like the non-existence of the first intellect entails the non-existence of the Necessary [Existence]—exalted is He above that greatly—according to what is well-known, likewise in al-Hashiya. (Then the conclusion is like the major premise) with respect to modality, if it [the major] is not from the descriptive [propositions]; because of clear subsumption; for the minor premise judges that the minor term is among the individuals of the middle term, and the major premise judges that what is an individual of it [the middle] is such that the major term is predicated of it with one of the modalities, so it necessarily follows that the minor term also is predicated of the major term with those same modalities.
(1)
(Otherwise it is like the minor premise), i.e., if the major is from one of the descriptive [propositions], then the conclusion is like the minor, with the existential condition removed from it, i.e., non-permanence and non-necessity, and the necessity specific to the minor, i.e., which is not in the major, and with the existential condition in the major attached to it, if it [the major] is from one of the two special [propositions]. As for its being like the minor: because the major judged the relation of the major term to all individuals of the middle term in its time or with its condition, so it is established for the minor as long as the middle term is for it, if permanently then permanently, and if for a time then for a time.
As for the removal of the existential condition: because of the possibility of one of the two general [propositions] being true in a necessary matter, so the major term is not permanently for the minor, like our statement 'Every human is laughing — not permanently' and 'Every laughing [thing] is an animal as long as it laughs', with the non-permanent [proposition] being false. Also, the existential condition is negative, and it has no role in production in this figure, as they said.
And it is not far to say: if the major is a conditional-general [proposition], then it [the conclusion] is like the minor, without removing the existential condition; because the major judged that what is among the individuals of the middle term has a relation of the major term necessarily with the condition of the middle term, so for the minor it is also necessary with that condition, so that relation is negated by the negation of the middle term, and since the minor judged that the middle term is for the minor not permanently, the relation of the major term to it is also not permanently, and then there is a mixture of the true conditional with the non-permanent in a necessary matter, and the mentioned example does not make the conditional true, and the non-production of a negative alone is not its non-production within a composite, so understand and rely on God; indeed, accurate knowledge is with the Knower of the Unseen.
Source pages p. 520

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the rules for determining the conclusion's modality in mixed syllogisms, specifically when the premises involve modal propositions. The author begins by stating the general principle: the conclusion's modality is like that of the major premise if the major premise is not one of the 'descriptive' propositions (al-wasfiyyat). The reason given is the clear subsumption: the minor premise asserts that the minor term falls under the middle term, and the major premise asserts a modal relation between the middle and major terms, so the same modal relation must hold for the minor term. However, if the major premise is descriptive, then the conclusion is like the minor premise but with modifications: the existential condition (qayd al-wujud) is removed from it, along with the necessity specific to the minor (if any), and the existential condition from the major is added. The author explains that the conclusion is like the minor premise because the major premise predicates the major term of all individuals of the middle term in a particular time or condition, so it holds for the minor as long as the middle term holds for it. The removal of the existential condition is justified by the possibility of a general proposition being true in a necessary matter, which would make the conclusion not permanent. An example is given: 'Every human is laughing — not permanently' and 'Every laughing thing is an animal as long as it laughs' — here the conclusion 'Every human is an animal not permanently' would be false if the minor were 'Every human is laughing permanently'. The author then considers a special case: if the major is a 'conditional-general' (mashrutah 'ammah) proposition, the conclusion is like the minor without removing the existential condition, because the major's condition already ties the major term to the middle term's existence, so the conclusion's modality follows the minor's temporality. The passage ends with an exhortation to rely on God, noting that true knowledge is with Him.

الوصفيات (al-wasfiyyat)

الخاصتان (al-khassatan)

Mixing Modal Premises in First Figure: Conditional Majors
Author continues explaining effects of removing necessity conditions in modal syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine when a necessary minor premise does not yield a necessary conclusion in a first-figure syllogism with a conditional major premise.

Core Idea
Necessity does not transmit from minor to conclusion if the major is conditional on a non-necessary middle.

In modal syllogisms, the interaction between necessity and conditionality is subtle. The author explains that even if the minor premise is necessary, the conclusion may not be necessary if the major premise holds only conditionally on the middle term. You will learn to spot this crucial distinction and avoid a common fallacy in modal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the major premise type
Determine whether the major premise is a 'mashruta' (conditional) proposition, which asserts a relation only as long as the middle term holds, not permanently.
The text says: 'له نسبة الأكبر بالضرورة بشرط الأوسط' – the major's relation is necessary conditionally on the middle.
2
Examine the minor premise's modality
Check if the minor premise is 'la-da'ima' (non-permanent) or 'da'ima' (perpetual). Here the minor is non-permanent, meaning the middle does not always hold for the subject.
The author states: 'الصغرى حاكمة بكون الأوسط للأصغر لا دائما' – the minor judges that the middle is not always for the minor.
3
Analyze the transmission of necessity
The major's necessity is conditional on the middle. Since the middle is not always present for the subject, the major's necessity does not fully transfer. The conclusion's necessity is therefore not guaranteed.
The text: 'وحينئذ اختلاط المشروطة الصادقة مع اللادائمة في مادة الضرورة' – mixing true conditional with non-permanent in necessity matter.
4
Contrast with unconditional major
If the major were unconditional (e.g., absolute perpetual), a necessary minor might produce a necessary conclusion. But here the major's conditionality blocks that transmission.
The author later discusses removing the condition of existence to see what remains.

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on mixing modal propositions, likely from the first figure. The author begins by explaining that if the major premise is necessary conditionally on the middle term, and the minor premise judges that the middle is not always for the minor, then the major's relation to the minor is also not always. This leads to a mixing where a true conditional major (e.g., 'Every A is B as long as it is C') with a non-permanent minor (e.g., 'Some C is not always A') yields difficulties in the matter of necessity. The author then inserts two possible marginal notes: 'impossible' and 'necessitates', perhaps indicating alternative outcomes. Next, he discusses removing the specific necessity from the minor: the major's judgment applies to whatever the middle holds for absolutely, regardless of necessity, so even if the minor is necessary for the subject, the major's relation does not become necessary. Similarly, the necessity specific to the major does not transfer because it only gives necessity conditionally with the middle, which is not necessary for the minor. Then, the author explains adding the condition of existence in the major: this leads to a clear subsumption. He reiterates that an absolute minor with a general conditional major produces an absolute conclusion, and a compound with a compound. Finally, he lists what remains after removing the condition of existence: for different types of propositions (perpetual, descriptive, temporal, common, etc.), the results are specified. He notes that mixing one of the two perpetuals with one of the two particular does not yield a valid conclusion even with true premises, and the passage cuts off mid-sentence.

Conditions for Valid Mixed Modal Syllogisms in the Second Figure
Author states and explains the two conditions for productive mixed modal syllogisms in the second figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two conditions required for valid mixed modal syllogisms in the second figure and apply them to check the validity of arguments.

Core Idea
In the second figure, mixed modal syllogisms require either a perpetual minor or a convertible major, and possible premises pair only with necessary or conditional majors.

Mixed modal syllogisms combine premises of different modalities, such as necessity, perpetuity, and possibility. The second figure has specific constraints: not every mixing yields a valid conclusion. The author lays down two conditions, each with alternatives, to ensure productiveness. Understanding these conditions allows you to judge whether an argument in the second figure is sound.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first condition
The first condition has two alternatives: either the minor premise is perpetual (daima) or necessary (daruriya), or the major premise is from the six proposition types whose negatives are convertible (i.e., contraposition applies).
These six types include necessary, perpetual, and their negatives, etc.
2
Interpret 'perpetuity of the minor'
'Dawam al-sughra' means the minor premise must assert a permanent or necessary property. For example, 'Every A is B perpetually' or 'Necessarily every A is B'.
3
Interpret 'conversion of the paths of the major'
'In'ikas masalik al-kubra' means the major premise is of a type whose negation is convertible. In practice, this restricts the major to a set of six modal propositions that allow contraposition.
If the minor is not perpetual/necessary, the major must be from this set to ensure a valid conclusion.
4
Identify the second condition
The second condition governs the use of possible (mumkina) premises: a possible minor must be paired with a necessary or conditional major; a possible major may only be paired with a necessary minor.
This prevents false conclusions like the donkey example: 'No donkey is Zayd's riding animal perpetually' and 'Every donkey is Zayd's riding animal possibly' yield a false conclusion.
5
Apply the conditions to an example
Consider the syllogism: 'Necessarily, no temporal being is eternal; possibly, every attribute of Allah is eternal; therefore possibly, no attribute of Allah is temporal.' Check: minor (possibly, every attribute of Allah is eternal) is possible, major (necessarily, no temporal being is eternal) is necessary. This satisfies the second condition (possible minor with necessary major). Also, the major is necessary and thus from the six convertible types, satisfying the first condition as well. Hence it is valid.
If the minor were not perpetual and the major not convertible, the syllogism would be invalid.

Chapter on Mixed Modalities
It follows that the conclusion is necessary not perpetual, or perpetual not perpetual, that's it. And reliance on God; for He is the All-Knowing, the Aware.
(And in) the second figure, two conditions are required. One of them is one of two things: (The condition for production of the second figure is perpetuity of the minor), that it be perpetual or necessary, or the conversion of the paths of the major. (According to the modality, i.e., that the major be among the six propositions whose negatives are convertible, whether they are affirmative or negative. Otherwise, the minor is from the eleven non-perpetual, and the major from the seven whose negatives are not convertible. And then the conclusion may fail, such as: 'Every eclipse is dark as long as it is an eclipse, not perpetually, or at the time of the earth's interposition, not perpetually'; 'No moon is dark at the time of quadrature, not perpetually', while the conclusion is false. And this more specific mixing, since it produces nothing, produces nothing more general than it.) (And) the second condition is also one of two things: (The possible), whether minor or major, (with the necessary, or a conditional major). If it is the minor, the gist of this condition is that the possible minor is not used except with the necessary major, or one of the two conditionals; or the possible major only with the necessary, no other. Explanation of that: otherwise, the possible major would be either with the non-perpetual, and you have known that it does not produce in the more specific mixing, or with the perpetual minor, and the conclusion is not true in our saying: 'No donkey is a riding animal of Zayd perpetually, and every donkey is a riding animal of Zayd possibly'; or the possible minor would be either with the seven majors whose negatives are not convertible, and you have known that they do not produce with the more specific, or with the two simple [propositions] the general assertoric and the perpetual, and not.
Source pages p. 521

Commentary
This passage begins a new chapter on mixed modal syllogisms, specifically addressing the second figure. The author first states a general point: the conclusion can be necessary-not-perpetual or perpetual-not-perpetual, but then turns to the two conditions for valid mixed syllogisms in the second figure. The first condition has two alternatives: either the minor premise is perpetual or necessary (i.e., permanent modal), or the major premise is such that its negative is convertible (i.e., from the six proposition types that allow contraposition). The author elaborates: the major must be from a specific set where negatives convert, otherwise if the minor is from the eleven non-perpetual types and the major from the seven non-convertible types, the conclusion may fail. He provides an example: 'Every eclipse is dark as long as it is an eclipse, but not perpetually; no moon is dark at quadrature, not perpetually' yields a false conclusion. The second condition also has two alternatives: either a possible minor with a necessary major or a conditional major, or a possible major only with a necessary minor. The reasoning is that otherwise, the possible major with non-perpetual minor fails, and with a perpetual minor gives a false conclusion (as in the donkey example). Similarly, the possible minor with the seven non-convertible majors fails, or with the perpetual and general assertoric also fails. Thus, the conditions ensure validity.

Checkpoint
In the second figure, what must be true for a mixed syllogism to be valid if the minor is not perpetual?
A) The major must be necessary.
B) The major must be from the six propositions whose negatives convert.
C) The minor must be possible.
D) The major must be perpetual.

Key Takeaway
The second figure imposes two conditions: either the minor is perpetual/necessary, or the major's negative is convertible; and possible premises only combine with necessary or conditional majors.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Check the validity of the following mixed modal syllogism in the second figure: 'Necessarily, every valid contract is permissible; possibly, no gambling contract is valid; therefore possibly, no gambling contract is permissible.' Use the two conditions to determine if it is valid.
?
Construct a valid mixed modal syllogism in the second figure using Islamic examples (e.g., from fiqh or kalam) and specify which condition applies.
